The Hierarchy of Modal Propositions
Author enumerates modal proposition hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank the five principal modal propositions from most general to most specific according to the categories of simple/composite and actuality/necessity as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Modal propositions form an ordered network—some are most general, others most specific.

In this passage, the author presents a key tool for navigating the complex landscape of modal propositions in classical Islamic logic: a hierarchy of generality and specificity. This hierarchy helps the logician determine the logical relationships between different modal statements, which is essential for constructing and evaluating arguments in Kalām and other Islamic sciences. We will parse the passage to extract the precise ranking and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five modal propositions listed
Look for the following terms: ممكنة عامة, ممكنة خاصة, مطلقة عامة, ضرورية مطلقة, مشروطة خاصة. These are the key propositions discussed in the passage.
2
Categorize each proposition as simple or composite
The passage divides propositions into 'basā'iṭ' (simple) and 'murakkabāt' (composite). For example, the absolute necessary is classified as the most specific of simples, while the special conditioned is the most specific of composites.
3
Note the claimed rank for each proposition
The passage states: general possible is most general of all; special possible is most general of composites; general absolute is most general of actualities; absolute necessary is most specific of simples; special conditioned is most specific of composites.
4
Understand the logical basis for the ranking
Generality reflects the least restrictive truth condition. The general possible's condition is the broadest—it only requires possibility in at least one time. The absolute necessary requires necessity in all times, making it highly restrictive.
5
Compare initial appearances with precise principles
The passage warns that under precise philosophical principles (الأصول الدقيقة), some equivalences change: e.g., general possible equals general absolute; special conditioned equals specific customary. This shows the ranking depends on the logical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا يُسْتَصْعَبُ عَلَيْكَ اسْتِخْرَاجُ النَّسَبِ بَيْنَ الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ ، وَلَوِ اسْتَقْرَيْتَ) حال الموجهات عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ الْعَامَّةَ أَعَمُّ الْقَضَايَا) كلها، بسائط كانت أو مركبة ; فإن ضرورة النسبة مطلقا ودوامها مطلقا وفعليتها مطلقا لا تجامع مع ضرورة ،مقابلها، وأما إمكان النسبة فقد يوجد من غير ضرورة ودوام وفعلية. وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فالممكنة الميزانية والمطلقة العامة متساويتان ; لأن نقيضي المتساويين متساويان . (وَالْمُمْكِنَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ أَعَمُّ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ) ; فإن تحقق النسبة من غير ضرورة أو دوام، بل وقوع مقابلها في حين من الأحيان مستلزم لتساوي الطرفين من غير عكس; لجواز تساوي طرفي النسبة مع عدم وقوع أحدهما . أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوع جَانِبِ الأَصْلِ ] (١) ، وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فهي متساوية للوجوديتين; لأن تساوي المطلقتين والقيدين أوجب تساوي المقيدين. وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ أَعَمُّ الْفِعْلِيَّاتِ (۲) ، وهو ظاهر، (وَالضَّرُورِيَّةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ أَخَصُّ الْبَسَائِطِ ) ; فإن ضرورة نسبة في جميع أوقات الذات توجب دوامها في (۳) تلك الأوقات وضرورتها ودوامها في جميع أوقات الوصف الذي هي بعض أوقات الذات وفعليتها وإمكانها من غير عكس. أما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فقد علمت تساوي الدوام والضرورة. (وَالْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ أَخَصُّ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ) ; فإن الضرورة ما دام الوصف (٤) من غير دوام مستلزم للضرورة في وقت معين هو وقت الوصف، ووقت ما من غير دوام والدوام في زمان الوصف من غير دوام والفعلية من غير دوام وضرورة، ولتساوي الطرفين من غير عكس وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فالمشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة متساويتان إلا إذا أخذ الضرورة بشرط الوصف. لكن كون الضرورية أخص البسائط والمشروطة الخاصة أخص المركبات عَلَى وَجْهِ)، هو أخذ المشروطة مشروطة ما دام الوصف، وإن أخذت مشروطة بشرط الوصف كما هو المعتبر في هذا الفن فالضرورية أخص منها من وجه، وكذا المشروطة الخاصة من الوقتيتين; لجواز أن لا يكون
And at that point, it is not difficult for you to derive the relations between the mentioned modal propositions. And if you investigate the state of the modals, you know that the general possible is the most general of all propositions, whether simple or composite. For the necessity of the relation absolutely, its permanence absolutely, and its actuality absolutely do not coexist with necessity of its opposite. But possibility of the relation may exist without necessity, permanence, and actuality. But built on precise principles, the balanced possible and the general absolute are equivalent, because the contradictories of equivalents are equivalent. And the special possible is the most general of composites, for the realization of the relation without necessity or permanence, rather the occurrence of its opposite at some time entails equality of the two sides, without conversion, because the two sides of the relation may be equal without the occurrence of one of them. Or the non-occurrence of the side of the original? [incomplete] And built on precise principles, it is equivalent to the existential propositions, because the equality of the two absolutes and the two restrictions necessitates equality of the two restricted. And the general absolute is the most general of actualities, which is obvious. And the absolute necessary is the most specific of simples, for the necessity of a relation in all times of the subject entails its permanence in those times and its necessity and permanence in all times of the description that are some times of the subject, and its actuality and its possibility, without conversion. But built on precise principles, you have known the equality of permanence and necessity. And the special conditioned is the most specific of composites, for the necessity as long as the description, without permanence, entails necessity at a specific time which is the time of the description, and at some time without permanence, and permanence in the time of the description without permanence, and actuality without permanence and necessity, and equality of the two sides without conversion. And built on precise principles, the special conditioned and the specific customary are equivalent, except if necessity is taken with the condition of the description. But the fact that the necessary is the most specific of simples and the special conditioned is the most specific of composites in one way is when the conditioned is taken as 'as long as the description', but if it is taken as 'with the condition of the description' as is considered in this art, then the necessary is more specific from one aspect, and likewise the special conditioned is more specific than the two time-related [propositions], because it is possible that...
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage enumerates the most general and most specific modal propositions according to the logical perspective of initial appearances. The author states that if you investigate the modal propositions, you will know that the general possible (al-mumkina al-'āmma) is the most general of all propositions, both simple and composite. The reason is that necessity, permanence, and actuality cannot coexist with their opposites, whereas possibility can exist without them. The special possible (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa) is the most general of composite propositions, because its predicate's actuality without necessity or permanence, coupled with the occurrence of its opposite at some time, entails equality of the two sides (but without conversion). The general absolute (al-muṭlaqa al-'āmma) is the most general of actuality propositions, which is obvious. The absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) is the most specific of simple propositions, because its necessity in all times of the subject entails permanence, actuality, and possibility in those times, and also in times of the description, without conversion. The special conditioned (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) is the most specific of composite propositions, because its necessity as long as the description exists, without permanence, entails various other modal relations. The author also notes the differences when built on precise philosophical principles, e.g., the general possible and the general absolute become equivalent, and the special conditioned and the specific customary become equivalent except when necessity is taken with the condition of the description. The passage concludes with a clarification that the absolute necessary being the most specific of simples and the special conditioned being the most specific of composites holds only when the conditioned is taken as 'as long as the description', not 'with the condition of the description' as usually taken in this art.

Key Terms
ممكنة عامة
ممكنة خاصة
مطلقة عامة
ضرورية مطلقة
مشروطة خاصة

Checkpoint
According to the text, which modal proposition is the most general of all propositions (simple and composite)?
A) The absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة)
B) The general possible (الممكنة العامة)
C) The special possible (الممكنة الخاصة)
D) The general absolute (المطلقة العامة)

Key Takeaway
The general possible (al-mumkina al-‘āmma) is the most general of all modal propositions, while the absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) and the special conditioned (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) are the most specific for simples and composites respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a hierarchy of the following modal propositions from most general to most specific: الضرورية المطلقة, الممكنة العامة, الممكنة الخاصة, المطلقة العامة, المشروطة الخاصة. Check your order against the passage.
?
Explain why the general possible is considered the most general of all propositions, using the reasoning given in the passage.

Propositions
beginner

The General Possible as Most General of All
Author states the general possible is most general of all propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general possible proposition (الممكنة العامة) and explain why it is considered the most general of all propositions according to the passage.

Core Idea
The general possible applies to any proposition whose predicate is possible in every instance.

This lesson focuses on a key claim in the passage: the general possible (الممكنة العامة) is the most general of all modal propositions. We will dissect its definition and understand the logical reasoning behind its primacy.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term 'الممكنة العامة' in the passage
Find the first occurrence: 'الممكنة العامة أعم القضايا كلها'. This is the statement we are analyzing.
2
Analyze the definition of the general possible
From the key terms, define it: a proposition whose predicate is possible in all times (not necessary or permanent). This condition is less restrictive than necessity, permanence, or actuality.
3
Understand why it is the most general
The passage explains: necessity, permanence, and actuality do not coexist with their opposites, but possibility can exist without them. This means any necessary, permanent, or actual proposition also entails possibility, but not vice versa.
For example, if it is necessary that the world is created, it is also possible.
4
Consider the exception under precise principles
The passage notes that under precise philosophical principles, the general possible and the general absolute become equivalent. This nuance shows the hierarchy is not absolute but depends on the logical framework.
5
Test your understanding
Given the proposition 'Every human is possibly rational' (generic example from Islamic thought), identify whether it qualifies as a general possible. Yes, because it states possibility in all cases.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the most general and most specific modal propositions according to the logical perspective of initial appearances. The author states that if you investigate the modal propositions, you will know that the general possible (al-mumkina al-'āmma) is the most general of all propositions, both simple and composite. The reason is that necessity, permanence, and actuality cannot coexist with their opposites, whereas possibility can exist without them. The special possible (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa) is the most general of composite propositions, because its predicate's actuality without necessity or permanence, coupled with the occurrence of its opposite at some time, entails equality of the two sides (but without conversion). The general absolute (al-muṭlaqa al-'āmma) is the most general of actuality propositions, which is obvious. The absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) is the most specific of simple propositions, because its necessity in all times of the subject entails permanence, actuality, and possibility in those times, and also in times of the description, without conversion. The special conditioned (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) is the most specific of composite propositions, because its necessity as long as the description exists, without permanence, entails various other modal relations. The author also notes the differences when built on precise philosophical principles, e.g., the general possible and the general absolute become equivalent, and the special conditioned and the specific customary become equivalent except when necessity is taken with the condition of the description. The passage concludes with a clarification that the absolute necessary being the most specific of simples and the special conditioned being the most specific of composites holds only when the conditioned is taken as 'as long as the description', not 'with the condition of the description' as usually taken in this art.

The Absolute Necessary as Most Specific of Simples
Author declares the absolute necessary is most specific of simples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the absolute necessary proposition (الضرورية المطلقة) and explain why it is the most specific of simple propositions according to the passage.

Core Idea
The absolute necessary proposition is the most restrictive simple modal proposition.

In this lesson, we examine the claim that the absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة) is the most specific of simple propositions. We will dissect its definition and the logical reasoning that establishes its narrowness compared to other simple modals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'الضرورية المطلقة' in the passage
Find the statement: 'والضرورية المطلقة أخص البسائط'. This is our focus.
2
Analyze the definition of the absolute necessary
It is a proposition whose predicate is necessary for the subject in all times of the subject's existence. This is expressed as 'ضرورة نسبة في جميع أوقات الذات'.
3
Understand why it is the most specific
Because its condition is the most restrictive: it requires necessity at every time. Other simple modals (like permanence, actuality, possibility) are either broader or do not require necessity in all times.
4
Compare with other simple propositions
The passage notes that necessity in all times entails permanence, actuality, and possibility in those times, but not vice versa. Thus, the absolute necessary implies all others but is implied by none.
5
Consider the nuance under precise principles
Under precise principles, permanence and necessity become equivalent. This might affect the ranking, but the initial claim remains foundational.

( وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا يُسْتَصْعَبُ عَلَيْكَ اسْتِخْرَاجُ النَّسَبِ بَيْنَ الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ ، وَلَوِ اسْتَقْرَيْتَ) حال الموجهات عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ الْعَامَّةَ أَعَمُّ الْقَضَايَا) كلها، بسائط كانت أو مركبة ; فإن ضرورة النسبة مطلقا ودوامها مطلقا وفعليتها مطلقا لا تجامع مع ضرورة ،مقابلها، وأما إمكان النسبة فقد يوجد من غير ضرورة ودوام وفعلية. وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فالممكنة الميزانية والمطلقة العامة متساويتان ; لأن نقيضي المتساويين متساويان . (وَالْمُمْكِنَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ أَعَمُّ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ) ; فإن تحقق النسبة من غير ضرورة أو دوام، بل وقوع مقابلها في حين من الأحيان مستلزم لتساوي الطرفين من غير عكس; لجواز تساوي طرفي النسبة مع عدم وقوع أحدهما . أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوع جَانِبِ الأَصْلِ ] (١) ، وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فهي متساوية للوجوديتين; لأن تساوي المطلقتين والقيدين أوجب تساوي المقيدين. وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ أَعَمُّ الْفِعْلِيَّاتِ (۲) ، وهو ظاهر، (وَالضَّرُورِيَّةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ أَخَصُّ الْبَسَائِطِ ) ; فإن ضرورة نسبة في جميع أوقات الذات توجب دوامها في (۳) تلك الأوقات وضرورتها ودوامها في جميع أوقات الوصف الذي هي بعض أوقات الذات وفعليتها وإمكانها من غير عكس. أما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فقد علمت تساوي الدوام والضرورة. (وَالْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ أَخَصُّ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ) ; فإن الضرورة ما دام الوصف (٤) من غير دوام مستلزم للضرورة في وقت معين هو وقت الوصف، ووقت ما من غير دوام والدوام في زمان الوصف من غير دوام والفعلية من غير دوام وضرورة، ولتساوي الطرفين من غير عكس وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فالمشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة متساويتان إلا إذا أخذ الضرورة بشرط الوصف. لكن كون الضرورية أخص البسائط والمشروطة الخاصة أخص المركبات عَلَى وَجْهِ)، هو أخذ المشروطة مشروطة ما دام الوصف، وإن أخذت مشروطة بشرط الوصف كما هو المعتبر في هذا الفن فالضرورية أخص منها من وجه، وكذا المشروطة الخاصة من الوقتيتين; لجواز أن لا يكون
And at that point, it is not difficult for you to derive the relations between the mentioned modal propositions. And if you investigate the state of the modals, you know that the general possible is the most general of all propositions, whether simple or composite. For the necessity of the relation absolutely, its permanence absolutely, and its actuality absolutely do not coexist with necessity of its opposite. But possibility of the relation may exist without necessity, permanence, and actuality. But built on precise principles, the balanced possible and the general absolute are equivalent, because the contradictories of equivalents are equivalent. And the special possible is the most general of composites, for the realization of the relation without necessity or permanence, rather the occurrence of its opposite at some time entails equality of the two sides, without conversion, because the two sides of the relation may be equal without the occurrence of one of them. Or the non-occurrence of the side of the original? [incomplete] And built on precise principles, it is equivalent to the existential propositions, because the equality of the two absolutes and the two restrictions necessitates equality of the two restricted. And the general absolute is the most general of actualities, which is obvious. And the absolute necessary is the most specific of simples, for the necessity of a relation in all times of the subject entails its permanence in those times and its necessity and permanence in all times of the description that are some times of the subject, and its actuality and its possibility, without conversion. But built on precise principles, you have known the equality of permanence and necessity. And the special conditioned is the most specific of composites, for the necessity as long as the description, without permanence, entails necessity at a specific time which is the time of the description, and at some time without permanence, and permanence in the time of the description without permanence, and actuality without permanence and necessity, and equality of the two sides without conversion. And built on precise principles, the special conditioned and the specific customary are equivalent, except if necessity is taken with the condition of the description. But the fact that the necessary is the most specific of simples and the special conditioned is the most specific of composites in one way is when the conditioned is taken as 'as long as the description', but if it is taken as 'with the condition of the description' as is considered in this art, then the necessary is more specific from one aspect, and likewise the special conditioned is more specific than the two time-related [propositions], because it is possible that...
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage enumerates the most general and most specific modal propositions according to the logical perspective of initial appearances. The author states that if you investigate the modal propositions, you will know that the general possible (al-mumkina al-'āmma) is the most general of all propositions, both simple and composite. The reason is that necessity, permanence, and actuality cannot coexist with their opposites, whereas possibility can exist without them. The special possible (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa) is the most general of composite propositions, because its predicate's actuality without necessity or permanence, coupled with the occurrence of its opposite at some time, entails equality of the two sides (but without conversion). The general absolute (al-muṭlaqa al-'āmma) is the most general of actuality propositions, which is obvious. The absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa) is the most specific of simple propositions, because its necessity in all times of the subject entails permanence, actuality, and possibility in those times, and also in times of the description, without conversion. The special conditioned (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) is the most specific of composite propositions, because its necessity as long as the description exists, without permanence, entails various other modal relations. The author also notes the differences when built on precise philosophical principles, e.g., the general possible and the general absolute become equivalent, and the special conditioned and the specific customary become equivalent except when necessity is taken with the condition of the description. The passage concludes with a clarification that the absolute necessary being the most specific of simples and the special conditioned being the most specific of composites holds only when the conditioned is taken as 'as long as the description', not 'with the condition of the description' as usually taken in this art.

بسائط
ذات
وصف

B) The absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة)
C) The special possible (الممكنة الخاصة)
D) The general absolute (المطلقة العامة)

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة) is the most specific simple modal proposition because it requires necessity in all times.

Propositions
intermediate
5 min
The Special Conditioned as Most Specific of Composites
Author declares the special conditioned is most specific of composites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the special conditioned proposition (المشروطة الخاصة) and explain why it is the most specific of composite propositions according to the passage.

Core Idea
The special conditioned proposition is the most restrictive composite modal proposition.

This lesson examines the claim that the special conditioned (المشروطة الخاصة) is the most specific of composite propositions. We will parse its definition, understand its logical properties, and see why it is narrower than other composite modals.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term 'المشروطة الخاصة' in the passage
Find the statement: 'والمشروطة الخاصة أخص المركبات'. This is our target.
2
Analyze the definition of the special conditioned
It is a composite proposition where the predicate is necessary for the subject as long as the subject is described by a certain description, but not permanent beyond that description.
The condition is 'ما دام الوصف' (as long as the description exists).
3
Understand why it is the most specific
Because its truth condition requires necessity only during a specific description, and it explicitly excludes permanence. Other composite propositions like the general or time-related have broader conditions.
4
Compare with other composite propositions
The passage says it entails other composite propositions (like special time-related) but is not entailed by them, making it the narrowest.
5
Note the nuance about the reading of the condition
If the special conditioned is taken as 'مع شرط الوصف' (with the condition of the description) instead of 'ما دام الوصف' (as long as the description), its specificity may change. The passage indicates this nuance is important in the art of logic.

Identifying the Four Core Modal Propositions
Commentator compares modal proposition types and their relations based on precise principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four modal proposition types: general absolute (المطلقة العامة), absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة), special conditional (المشروطة الخاصة), and temporal propositions (الوقتيتان), based on their definitions and scopes.

Core Idea
The four modal propositions form a hierarchy from most general to most specific, defined by their temporal and descriptive constraints.

In this passage, the commentator defines the key modal propositions used in Islamic logic and explains their relationships. The general absolute is unconditional; the absolute necessary holds at all times of the essence; the special conditional holds only as long as a description applies; the temporal propositions are time-bound. Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating modal arguments in theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four terms
Read the Arabic passage and find the terms: المطلقة العامة, الضرورية المطلقة, المشروطة الخاصة, and الوقتيتان. Note their English equivalents.
The passage uses these terms in a comparative context.
2
Extract defining characteristics
From the commentary, identify what makes each type unique: general absolute is unconditional necessity? (actually it's unconditional actuality); absolute necessary is necessary at all times of the essence; special conditional is necessary as long as a description holds; temporal propositions involve specific or indefinite times.
The passage contrasts 'simple' vs 'compound' modals.
3
Compare their scopes
Determine which is most general and which are most specific. The passage states: general absolute is the most general of actualities; absolute necessary is the most specific of simple propositions; special conditional is the most specific of compound propositions.
Scope depends on whether the modal is simple or compound.
4
Verify with examples
Test your understanding by considering examples from Islamic sciences: e.g., 'Every human is necessarily mortal' (absolute necessary); 'Every writer moves his fingers while writing' (special conditional).
Ensure examples match the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عكس؛ لجواز تساوي طرفي النسبة مع عدم وقوع أحدهما . أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوع جَانِبِ الأَصْلِ، وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فهي متساوية للوجوديتين؛ لأن تساوي المطلقتين والقيدين أوجب تساوي المقيدين. وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ أَعَمُّ الْفِعْلِيَّاتِ، وهو ظاهر، وَالضَّرُورِيَّةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ أَخَصُّ الْبَسَائِطِ؛ فإن ضرورة نسبة في جميع أوقات الذات توجب دوامها في تلك الأوقات وضرورتها ودوامها في جميع أوقات الوصف الذي هي بعض أوقات الذات وفعليتها وإمكانها من غير عكس. أما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فقد علمت تساوي الدوام والضرورة. وَالْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ أَخَصُّ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ؛ فإن الضرورة ما دام الوصف من غير دوام مستلزم للضرورة في وقت معين هو وقت الوصف، ووقت ما من غير دوام والدوام في زمان الوصف من غير دوام والفعلية من غير دوام وضرورة، ولتساوي الطرفين من غير عكس وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فالمشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة متساويتان إلا إذا أخذ الضرورة بشرط الوصف. لكن كون الضرورية أخص البسائط والمشروطة الخاصة أخص المركبات على وجه، هو أخذ المشروطة مشروطة ما دام الوصف، وإن أخذت مشروطة بشرط الوصف كما هو المعتبر في هذا الفن فالضرورية أخص منها من وجه، وكذا المشروطة الخاصة من الوقتيتين؛ لجواز أن لا يكون الوصف ضروريا للذات بل بالاتفاق المحض، فما بال المشروطة به؟ فلا يصدق الوقتيتان والضرورية. وأيضا يجوز أن لا يكون للوصف دخل في الضرورة، ويكون المحمول ثبوتا أو سلبا ضروريا للذات، فيصدق الضرورة مطلقا، أو في وقت لا دائما، فيصدق الوقتيتان أو الضرورية، ولا يصدق المشروطتان. وأما بناء على الأصول الدقيقة فيجب ثبوت الوصف للذات؛ لبطلان الاتفاق، فيجب المشروط به فيلزم صدق الوقتيتين عند صدق المشروطة الخاصة. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Converse; because of the permissibility of equality of the two extremes of the relation with the non-occurrence of one of them, or the non-occurrence of the side of the origin. But based on the precise principles, they are equal to the two existential propositions; because the equality of the two absolutes and the two conditions necessitates the equality of the two conditioned ones. The general absolute is the most general of the actualities, and that is apparent. The absolute necessary is the most specific of the simple ones; for the necessity of a relation in all times of the essence necessitates its permanence in those times and its necessity and permanence in all times of the description which are some times of the essence, and its actuality and possibility without converse. But based on the precise principles, you have known the equality of permanence and necessity. The special conditional is the most specific of the compound ones; for the necessity as long as the description is without permanence entails necessity in a specific time which is the time of the description, and [necessity] at some time without permanence, and permanence in the time of the description without permanence, and actuality without permanence and necessity, and because of the equality of the two extremes without converse. But based on the precise principles, the special conditional and the special conventional are equal except when necessity is taken with the condition of the description. However, that the necessary is the most specific of the simple ones and the special conditional is the most specific of the compound ones in one respect is that the conditional is taken as conditional "as long as the description", but if it is taken as conditional "on condition of the description" as is considered in this art, then the necessary is more specific than it in one respect, and likewise the special conditional than the two temporal propositions; because it is possible that the description is not necessary for the essence but by mere agreement, so what about the conditional with it? So the two temporal propositions and the necessary are not true. Also, it is possible that the description has no part in the necessity, and the predicate is necessary for the essence affirmatively or negatively, so the necessary is true absolutely, or at a time not always, so the two temporal propositions or the necessary are true, and the two conditionals are not true. But based on the precise principles, the affirmation of the description for the essence is necessary; because agreement is invalid, so the conditional with it is necessary, and thus the truth of the two temporal propositions follows when the special conditional is true. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a detailed comparison of modal proposition types from a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum. It discusses the relationships among the general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma), absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa), special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), and temporal propositions (al-waqtiyyatan), particularly under the 'precise principles' (al-usul al-daqiqa). The commentator begins by explaining that based on precise principles, certain modal propositions become equal, specifically the two existential propositions, because the equality of the absolutes and conditions necessitates equality of the conditioned. Then it states that the general absolute is the most general of actualities, and the absolute necessary is the most specific of simple propositions. The necessary proposition involves a relation that is necessary at all times of the essence, implying permanence. However, under precise principles, permanence and necessity are equal. Next, the special conditional is described as the most specific of compounds; it involves necessity only as long as the description holds, without full permanence. Under precise principles, the special conditional and special conventional are equal unless necessity is taken with the condition of the description. The commentator then clarifies that the precise principles affect the scope: if the conditional is taken 'as long as the description', the necessary is more specific in one respect; if taken 'on condition of the description', the necessary is more specific in another. The special conditional is also more specific than the two temporal propositions because the description might be merely accidental, not essential. Finally, under precise principles, since accidental agreement is invalid, the description must be essential, making the conditional true and thus the temporal propositions true when the conditional holds.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة
الضرورية المطلقة
المشروطة الخاصة
الوقتيتان
الأصول الدقيقة

The general absolute is the most general actuality, the absolute necessary is the most specific simple, and the special conditional is the most specific compound modal proposition.

Refining Modal Relations with Precise Principles
Commentator compares modal proposition types and their relations based on precise principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the precise principles (al-usul al-daqiqa) to determine equivalences among modal propositions and explain how they affect the hierarchy of specificity.

Core Idea
Under precise principles, permanence equals necessity, making the special conditional and special conventional equal except when necessity is conditioned by a description.

The commentator introduces a set of precise logical principles that refine the standard understanding of modal propositions. These principles show that permanence (dawam) and necessity (darura) are equivalent, and that the special conditional and special conventional become equal under certain conditions. This lesson focuses on how these principles reshape the relationships between modal types and what they imply for temporal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard hierarchy
Before precise principles, the general absolute is most general, absolute necessary most specific simple, special conditional most specific compound, and temporal propositions are less specific.
The passage contrasts 'standard' vs 'precise'.
2
Identify the key precise principles
The passage mentions two key points: (1) equality of the two absolutes and conditions leads to equality of the conditioned; (2) permanence and necessity are equal. Find the Arabic phrases: 'تساوي المطلقتين والقيدين' and 'تساوي الدوام والضرورة'.
These principles are specific to this commentary tradition.
3
Apply to the special conditional
Under precise principles, the special conditional and special conventional are equal except when necessity is taken with the condition of the description. This means if the necessity is tied to the description as a condition, they differ.
This nuance affects how conditional propositions are interpreted.
4
Determine the effect on temporal propositions
The passage states that under precise principles, because the description must be essential (not accidental), the truth of the special conditional entails the truth of the temporal propositions. Work through the logical necessity: if description is essential, then the conditional holds at all times of the description, which are specific times.
This reverses a standard counterexample.

Modal Propositions: Conditioned and Temporal Truth Conditions
Author discusses truth conditions for conditioned and temporal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary, temporal, and conditioned modal propositions and determine their truth conditions based on whether the description is essential or accidental.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditioned proposition depends on whether its description is essential to the subject, not merely accidental.

This passage explores how modal propositions interrelate when the predicate's necessity is tied to a description. The author distinguishes between a simpler view, where the description may be accidental, and a more precise view, where the description must be essential. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing modal arguments in Islamic logic, especially in kalam discussions about divine attributes and temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modal types
The passage mentions three types: necessary (الضرورية), temporal (الوقتيتان), and conditioned (المشروطة). Note that temporal propositions are two (time-specific positive and negative).
Temporal propositions are sometimes called 'time-bound'.
2
Understand the simpler view on accidental description
The author states that if the description is 'by pure agreement' (accidental), then the conditioned proposition may be false, and the necessary and temporal propositions are not true in that case. This is the less rigorous position.
Here 'الوصف ضروريا للذات بل بالاتفاق المحض' means the description is necessary for the essence but by pure coincidence.
3
Contrast with the precise principles
The precise view requires the description to be essential (ثبوت الوصف للذات). If so, the conditioned proposition must be true, and its truth entails the truth of the temporal propositions (فيلزم صدق الوقتيتين عند صدق المشروطة الخاصة).
الأصول الدقيقة refers to the exacting standards of later logicians.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider the proposition 'Every writer moves his hand when writing.' The description 'when writing' may be accidental (the person is essentially a human, not essentially a writer). In the precise view, if 'writer' is an essential description, then the conditioned proposition holds necessarily, and the temporal proposition 'at the time of writing' is true.
Use an Islamic example: 'Every prophet speaks truthfully when delivering revelation' – if 'when delivering revelation' is essential to prophethood, the conditioned proposition is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Continuation of the investigations of modals. The description is necessary for the essence, but by pure agreement; so what about the conditioned proposition? Then the temporal and necessary propositions are not true. Also, it is possible that the description has no role in the necessity, and the predicate, in affirmation or negation, is necessary for the essence, so the necessary proposition absolutely is true, or at a time not always, so the temporal or necessary propositions are true, and the conditioned ones are not true. Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe discussing the interrelations among modal propositions, specifically necessary, temporal, and conditioned propositions. The author explains that if a description is accidental (by pure agreement) and not essential, then conditioned propositions may not hold, and temporal and necessary propositions are false. Conversely, if the description plays no role in necessity, different modal truths follow. The passage contrasts a simpler view with a more precise one ('الأصول الدقيقة'), which requires the description to be essential, thus making conditioned propositions true when their conditions are met, and leading to the truth of temporal propositions under certain conditions. The passage ends with a standard deferral to divine knowledge.

Connected Conditionals: Types and Classification
Author defines and classifies connected conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a connected conditional as necessary (luzūmiyya), coincidental (ittifāqiyya), or absolute (muṭlaqa) based on the nature of the relation between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
The link in a connected conditional may be necessary, coincidental, or unspecified.

Connected conditionals express a relation of dependence between two propositions. The author distinguishes three types based on how the consequent depends on the antecedent: necessary (luzūmiyya) where the consequence follows by a genuine relation, coincidental (ittifāqiyya) where there is no necessary dependency, and absolute (muṭlaqa) which is indeterminate. This classification is essential for evaluating conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify antecedent and consequent
Find the two component propositions: the first is the condition, the second is the conditioned.
2
Check the nature of the connection
Ask: Does the consequent necessarily follow from the antecedent, or do they merely coincide? If necessary, it is luzūmiyya; if coincidental, it is ittifāqiyya; if unspecified, it is muṭlaqa.
The necessary and coincidental are mutually exclusive; absolute is broader.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرطيات. الشرطية إن حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى لزوما، بأن يجب مصاحبتها تلك بعلاقة، أو اتفاقا، بأن لا يجب مصاحبتها تلك، لكن وهما إلى يصاحبها، أو إطلاقا من غير تقييد باللزوم والاتفاق فمتصلة لزومية على الأول، أو اتفاقية على الثاني، وهما متباينتان، أو مطلقة على الثالث، وهي أعم منهما. وإن حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين صدقا وكذبا معا، بأن لا يجتمعا ولا يرتفعا، أو صدقا فقط، من غير تناف في الكذب، بأن لا يجتمعا ويجوز ارتفاعهما، أو كذبا فقط، من غير تناف في الصدق، بأن لا يرتفعا مع جواز الاجتماع، سواء كان تلك التنافيات الثلاث عنادا، بأن يجب التنافي، أو اتفاقا، بأن لا يجب لكن تتنافيان بالاتفاق المحض، أو إطلاقا، أو من غير تقييد بالعناد والاتفاق فمنفصلة حقيقية على الأول، أو مانعة الجمع على الثاني، أو مانعة الخلو على الثالث.
Section on conditionals. If a conditional proposition is judged by the affirmation of a relation upon the condition of another [relation] with necessity—meaning that it must accompany that [other] by a relation—or by agreement—meaning that it need not accompany it, but they happen to accompany each other—or absolutely without restriction to necessity or agreement—then it is a connected [conditional]: (a) necessary on the first, (b) agreed upon on the second—and they are mutually exclusive—or (c) absolute on the third, which is more general than them. And if it is judged by the mutual exclusion of the two relations in truth and falsehood together—meaning that they neither coexist nor are both absent—or in truth only, without conflict in falsehood—meaning that they do not coexist but can both be absent—or in falsehood only, without conflict in truth—meaning that they are not both absent while they can coexist—whether these three exclusions are by opposition (that the exclusion is necessary) or by agreement (that it is not necessary but they happen to exclude each other by pure agreement) or absolutely (without restriction to opposition or agreement)—then it is a disjunctive [conditional]: (a) real (ḥaqīqiyya) on the first, (b) exclusive of conjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘) on the second, or (c) exclusive of absence (māni‘at al-khalā’) on the third.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of conditional propositions (al-sharṭiyya) into two main categories: connected (muttaṣila) and disjunctive (munfaṣila). For connected conditionals, the author explains that the relation of the antecedent to the consequent can be necessary (luzūmiyya), merely agreed upon (ittifāqiyya), or absolute (muṭlaqa) without specifying the nature of the connection. The necessary and agreed-upon are mutually exclusive; the absolute encompasses both. For disjunctive conditionals, the mutual exclusion of the two propositions (the two 'relations') can occur in three ways: both cannot be true together and both cannot be false together (real, ḥaqīqiyya); they cannot be true together but can both be false (māni‘at al-jam‘); or they cannot be false together but can be true together (māni‘at al-khalā’). Each of these three can be further qualified as by opposition (ʿinād, i.e., necessary exclusion), by agreement (ittifāq, accidental exclusion), or absolute. The passage thus provides a comprehensive taxonomy of conditional propositions, which is foundational for understanding compound syllogisms (qiyās istithnā’ī) in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
شرطية
متصلة
لزومية
اتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Luzūmiyya
B) Ittifāqiyya
C) Muṭlaqa
D) Mani'a al-jam'

Key Takeaway
Connected conditionals are classified by the necessity, coincidence, or indeterminacy of the link between antecedent and consequent.

Disjunctive Conditionals: Real, Exclusive of Conjunction, Exclusive of Absence
Author defines and classifies disjunctive conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditionals: real (ḥaqīqiyya), exclusive of conjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘), and exclusive of absence (māni‘at al-khalā‘).

Core Idea
Disjunctives exclude either truth, falsehood, or both between two propositions.

Disjunctive conditionals express mutual exclusion between two propositions. The author identifies three kinds: real (ḥaqīqiyya) where the two cannot be true together nor false together; exclusive of conjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘) where they cannot both be true but can both be false; and exclusive of absence (māni‘at al-khalā‘) where they cannot both be false but can both be true. Each can be further qualified as by opposition (necessary), agreement (accidental), or absolute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two component propositions
Locate the two propositions that are mutually exclusive.
2
Check coexistence in truth
Can both be true at the same time? If no, proceed; if yes, then it cannot be real or māni‘at al-jam‘ (but could be māni‘at al-khalā‘).
3
Check coexistence in falsehood
Can both be false at the same time? If no, then it is māni‘at al-khalā‘; if yes, then it is māni‘at al-jam‘; if neither both true nor both false, it is ḥaqīqiyya.
4
Classify the disjunctive
Use the table: ḥaqīqiyya (cannot both true, cannot both false), māni‘at al-jam‘ (cannot both true, can both false), māni‘at al-khalā‘ (cannot both false, can both true).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرطيات. الشرطية إن حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى لزوما، بأن يجب مصاحبتها تلك بعلاقة، أو اتفاقا، بأن لا يجب مصاحبتها تلك، لكن وهما إلى يصاحبها، أو إطلاقا من غير تقييد باللزوم والاتفاق فمتصلة لزومية على الأول، أو اتفاقية على الثاني، وهما متباينتان، أو مطلقة على الثالث، وهي أعم منهما. وإن حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين صدقا وكذبا معا، بأن لا يجتمعا ولا يرتفعا، أو صدقا فقط، من غير تناف في الكذب، بأن لا يجتمعا ويجوز ارتفاعهما، أو كذبا فقط، من غير تناف في الصدق، بأن لا يرتفعا مع جواز الاجتماع، سواء كان تلك التنافيات الثلاث عنادا، بأن يجب التنافي، أو اتفاقا، بأن لا يجب لكن تتنافيان بالاتفاق المحض، أو إطلاقا، أو من غير تقييد بالعناد والاتفاق فمنفصلة حقيقية على الأول، أو مانعة الجمع على الثاني، أو مانعة الخلو على الثالث.
Section on conditionals. If a conditional proposition is judged by the affirmation of a relation upon the condition of another [relation] with necessity—meaning that it must accompany that [other] by a relation—or by agreement—meaning that it need not accompany it, but they happen to accompany each other—or absolutely without restriction to necessity or agreement—then it is a connected [conditional]: (a) necessary on the first, (b) agreed upon on the second—and they are mutually exclusive—or (c) absolute on the third, which is more general than them. And if it is judged by the mutual exclusion of the two relations in truth and falsehood together—meaning that they neither coexist nor are both absent—or in truth only, without conflict in falsehood—meaning that they do not coexist but can both be absent—or in falsehood only, without conflict in truth—meaning that they are not both absent while they can coexist—whether these three exclusions are by opposition (that the exclusion is necessary) or by agreement (that it is not necessary but they happen to exclude each other by pure agreement) or absolutely (without restriction to opposition or agreement)—then it is a disjunctive [conditional]: (a) real (ḥaqīqiyya) on the first, (b) exclusive of conjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘) on the second, or (c) exclusive of absence (māni‘at al-khalā’) on the third.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of conditional propositions (al-sharṭiyya) into two main categories: connected (muttaṣila) and disjunctive (munfaṣila). For connected conditionals, the author explains that the relation of the antecedent to the consequent can be necessary (luzūmiyya), merely agreed upon (ittifāqiyya), or absolute (muṭlaqa) without specifying the nature of the connection. The necessary and agreed-upon are mutually exclusive; the absolute encompasses both. For disjunctive conditionals, the mutual exclusion of the two propositions (the two 'relations') can occur in three ways: both cannot be true together and both cannot be false together (real, ḥaqīqiyya); they cannot be true together but can both be false (māni‘at al-jam‘); or they cannot be false together but can be true together (māni‘at al-khalā’). Each of these three can be further qualified as by opposition (ʿinād, i.e., necessary exclusion), by agreement (ittifāq, accidental exclusion), or absolute. The passage thus provides a comprehensive taxonomy of conditional propositions, which is foundational for understanding compound syllogisms (qiyās istithnā’ī) in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
شرطية
منفصلة
حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Checkpoint
Which type of disjunctive conditional is characterized by the impossibility of both propositions being false (i.e., they cannot both be absent)?
A) Ḥaqīqiyya
B) Māni‘at al-jam‘
C) Māni‘at al-khalā‘
D) Ittifāqiyya

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive conditionals are classified by whether they exclude truth, falsehood, or both between the two propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Connected Conditionals: Necessary, Accidental, Absolute
Author defines connected conditional and its three subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three subtypes of connected conditionals and identify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
In a connected conditional, the consequent either necessarily accompanies the antecedent or merely happens to.

Conditional propositions in Islamic logic are either connected or disconnected. In this lesson, we focus on the connected type (muttasila), where the truth of one relation depends on another. The author divides it into necessary, accidental, and absolute. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for analysing conditional syllogisms in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of judgment
In a connected conditional, the judgment is about the affirmation of one relation (consequent) based on another condition (antecedent). The author specifies three modes: necessarily, accidentally, or absolutely.
The mode determines the subtype.
2
Distinguish necessary from accidental
In luzumiyya, the consequent must accompany the antecedent due to a causal relation (e.g., 'if it is created, then it has a creator'). In ittifaqiyya, the accompaniment is not necessary but happens coincidentally (e.g., 'if the moon is eclipsed, then the sky is dark').
The key is necessity vs. chance.
3
Recognize the absolute as the general type
The absolute connected conditional includes both necessary and accidental without specifying which. It is more general. For example, 'if the sun rises, then it is day' is necessary, but 'if the sun rises, then I am awake' is accidental. The absolute form covers both.
Mutlaqa is the supertype.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In usul al-fiqh, a conditional like 'if a contract is valid, then it produces effects' can be luzumiyya if the effect necessarily follows, or ittifaqiyya if it is conditional on something else. Identify the relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرطيات
الشرطية إن حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى لزوما، بأن يجب مصاحبتها تلك بعلاقة، أو اتفاقا، بأن لا يجب مصاحبتها تلك، لكن وهما إلى يصاحبها، أو إطلاقا من غير تقييد باللزوم والاتفاق فمتصلة لزومية على الأول، أو اتفاقية على الثاني، وهما متباينتان، أو مطلقة على الثالث، وهي أعم منهما.
وإن حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين صدقا وكذبا معا، بأن لا يجتمعا ولا يرتفعا، أو صدقا فقط، من غير تناف في الكذب، بأن لا يجتمعا، ويجوز ارتفاعهما، أو كذبا فقط، من غير تناف في الصدق، بأن لا يرتفعا، مع جواز الاجتماع، سواء كان تلك التنافيات الثلاث عنادا، بأن يجب التنافي، أو اتفاقا، بأن لا يجب، لكن تتنافيان بالاتفاق المحض، أو إطلاقا، أو من غير تقييد بالعناد والاتفاق فمنفصلة حقيقية على الأول، أو مانعة الجمع على الثاني، أو مانعة الخلو على الثالث، وبينهما تباين كلي، وكل منها عنادية إن كان التنافي عنادا أو اتفاقية إن كان اتفاقا، وهما متباينتان، أو مطلقة إن كان مطلقا، وهي أعم منهما.
وربما يعتبر في مانعتي الجمع والخلو التنافي في الصدق أو في الكذب مطلقا، سواء كان التنافي في الصدق مع التنافي في الكذب أو لا، وكذا التنافي في الكذب مع التنافي في الصدق أو لا، وبهذا المعنى يكونان أعم من الحقيقية ومانعتي الجمع والخلو بالمعنى الأول.
Section on conditionals. The conditional: if it is judged in it by the affirmation of a relation upon another condition, necessarily, i.e., its accompaniment must be by a connection, or accidentally, i.e., its accompaniment is not necessary, but it happens to accompany it, or absolutely without restriction to necessity or accident, then it is a connected proposition: necessary on the first, or accidental on the second, and they are two distinct types, or absolute on the third, and it is more general than them. And if it is judged in it by the mutual exclusion of the two relations in truth and falsehood together, i.e., they neither coexist nor both vanish, or in truth only, without conflict in falsehood, i.e., they do not coexist, but their vanishing is possible, or in falsehood only, without conflict in truth, i.e., they do not both vanish, while their coexistence is possible, whether these three mutual exclusions are by opposition, i.e., the exclusion must be, or by accident, i.e., it is not necessary, but they exclude each other by pure accident, or absolutely, or without restriction to opposition and accident, then it is a disconnected proposition: real on the first, or preventing conjunction on the second, or preventing emptiness on the third, and between them is complete distinctness. And each of them is by opposition if the exclusion is by opposition, or by accident if it is by accident, and they are two distinct types, or absolute if it is absolute, and it is more general than them. And sometimes in the two 'preventing conjunction' and 'preventing emptiness', the exclusion in truth or in falsehood is considered absolutely, whether the exclusion in truth is with or without exclusion in falsehood, and similarly the exclusion in falsehood is with or without exclusion in truth. And by this meaning they become more general than the real and the two 'preventing conjunction' and 'preventing emptiness' in the first sense.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies conditional propositions (al-shartiyyat) in Islamic logic. It first addresses the connected conditional (al-muttasila), where the judgment is about the affirmation of a relation based on another condition. Three subtypes are given: necessary (luzumiyya) where the accompaniment is obligatory by a causal link; accidental (ittifaqiyya) where the accompaniment is not necessary but happens; and absolute (mutlaqa) which includes both without qualification. The absolute is more general, covering both necessary and accidental cases. Next, the disconnected conditional (al-munfasila) is treated, where the judgment is about the mutual exclusion of two relations. Three primary subtypes are distinguished based on the kind of exclusion: real (haqiqiyya) excludes both coexistence and joint absence; preventing conjunction (mani'at al-jam') excludes only coexistence; preventing emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) excludes only joint absence. Each of these can be further subdivided into oppositional (if the exclusion is necessary) or accidental, or absolute, with the absolute being more general. The passage also notes a broader interpretation of the preventing types, where exclusion is considered in truth or falsehood absolutely, making them more general than the real and the earlier defined preventing types. This classification is crucial for understanding the structure of compound syllogisms in Islamic logic, similar to how in fiqh one might distinguish between necessary and accidental conditions for a contract.

لزومية
اتفاقية
مطلقة

Disconnected Conditionals: Real, Preventing Conjunction, Preventing Emptiness
Author defines disconnected conditional and its three primary subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the three primary types of disconnected conditionals based on the exclusion pattern.

Core Idea
A disconnected conditional excludes the two relations in truth, falsehood, or both.

While the connected conditional affirms a relation, the disconnected conditional (munfasila) judges a mutual exclusion between two relations. The author identifies three patterns: real (both truth and falsehood excluded), preventing conjunction (only truth excluded), and preventing emptiness (only falsehood excluded). These map directly onto logical disjunctions used in categorical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of judgment
In a disconnected conditional, the judgment is about the mutual exclusion (تنافي) of two relations. The author specifies three dimensions: exclusion in truth (لا يجتمعان), in falsehood (لا يرتفعان), or both.
2
Understand the three patterns
Haqiqiyya excludes both (e.g., 'The world is either eternal or created' – cannot be both true, cannot be both false). Mani'at al-jam' excludes only coexistence in truth (e.g., 'This liquid is either water or oil' – cannot be both, but could be neither if it is something else). Mani'at al-khuluw excludes only coexistence in falsehood (e.g., 'Either this is a fish or it swims' – it is possible both are true but not both false).
These correspond to exclusive disjunction (haqiqiyya), exclusive OR (mani'at al-jam'), and inclusive OR (mani'at al-khuluw).
3
Distinguish by truth tables
Draw truth tables: For haqiqiyya, TT=impossible, TF=possible, FT=possible, FF=impossible. For mani'at al-jam': TT=impossible, TF, FT, FF=possible. For mani'at al-khuluw: TT=possible, TF, FT, FF=impossible.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In fiqh, a ruling might be 'This action is either wajib or mubah' – this could be a mani'at al-khuluw (cannot be both false, but could be both true? No, an action cannot be both wajib and mubah; so it is haqiqiyya if it cannot be both true and cannot be both false; but if it can be both false (e.g., haram), then it is mani'at al-jam'. So classification depends on the subject.

Secondary Subdivisions: Oppositional, Accidental, Absolute Disconnected
Author subdivides disconnected conditionals and provides broader interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the secondary classification of disconnected conditionals into inadiyya, ittifaqiyya, and mutlaqa, and understand the broader interpretation of the preventing types.

Core Idea
Every disconnected conditional may be oppositional (necessary), accidental, or absolute, adding modality to the exclusion.

After establishing the three primary types, the author further subdivides each into oppositional, accidental, and absolute, depending on whether the exclusion is necessary or coincidental. Additionally, a broader interpretation is given for the preventing types, making them more general. This modal dimension is crucial for precise logical analysis in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the secondary subdivision
Each of the three primary types (haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khuluw) can be inadiyya if the exclusion is necessary (e.g., 'The world is either eternal or created' – necessarily exclusive), ittifaqiyya if accidental (e.g., 'Either it is raining or it is sunny' – by coincidence, but theoretically both could happen), or mutlaqa if unspecified.
This adds a modal element: necessary vs. accidental vs. absolute.
2
Recognize the broader interpretation
The author notes that sometimes in mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw, the exclusion is considered absolutely in truth or falsehood, even if the other dimension may or may not hold. This makes them more general than the real type and the first sense of preventing types.
This broadens the applicability.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
An example of an oppositional mani'at al-khuluw: 'Either the Prophet is truthful or the Qur'an is not from Allah' – the exclusion of both being false is necessary. An accidental mani'at al-jam': 'Either it is Friday or it is the weekend' – they cannot both be true, but by accident (if Friday is part of weekend, they can be both true? Actually, Friday can be both a weekday and weekend in some calendars? So it is accidental.

This passage defines and classifies conditional propositions (al-shartiyyat) in Islamic logic. It first addresses the connected conditional (al-muttasila), where the judgment is about the affirmation of a relation based on another condition. Three subtypes are given: necessary (luzumiyya) where the accompaniment is obligatory by a causal link; accidental (ittifaqiyya) where the accompaniment is not necessary but happens; and absolute (mutlaqa) which includes both without qualification. The absolute is more general, covering both necessary and accidental cases. Next, the disconnected conditional (al-munfasila) is treated, where the judgment is about the mutual exclusion of two relations. Three primary subtypes are distinguished based on the kind of exclusion: real (haqiqiyya) excludes both coexistence and joint absence; preventing conjunction (mani'at al-jam') excludes only coexistence; preventing emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) excludes only joint absence. Each of these can be further subdivided into oppositional (if the exclusion is necessary) or accidental, or absolute, with the absolute being more general. The passage also notes a broader interpretation of the preventing types, where exclusion is considered in truth or falsehood absolutely, making them more general than the real and the earlier defined preventing types. This classification is crucial for understanding the structure of compound syllogisms in Islamic logic, similar to how in fiqh one might distinguish between necessary and accidental conditions for a contract.

Key Terms
عنادية
منفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Ittifaqiyya
C) Mutlaqa
D) None

Key Takeaway
Every disconnected conditional may be oppositional (necessary), accidental, or absolute; the broader interpretation of the preventing types expands their applicability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Universal and Particular Conditional Propositions
Classification of conditionals by quantity and negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish universal from particular conditional propositions by identifying the role of suppositions (taqdirat) in their quantification.

Core Idea
A universal conditional judges all suppositions of the antecedent, not the antecedent itself.

Conditional propositions (al-shartiyyāt) are fundamental in Islamic reasoning, especially in uṣūl al-fiqh where clauses like 'if...then' govern rulings. This passage clarifies how conditionals are quantified and warns against a common mistake: thinking that a universal conditional judges the antecedent in isolation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting legal and theological conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation of a conditional
The author states that negatives of conditionals negate the implication (luzūm), not imply the negation. For example, the negative of 'If A then B' is 'It is not the case that if A then B', not 'If A then not B'.
This subtlety avoids confusion between negating the implication and implying the negation.
2
Recognize the necessity of a supposition (taqdir)
A conditional judgment must be relative to a specific supposition or range of suppositions. Without a taqdir, no conditional judgment is meaningful.
Taqdīrāt are the scenarios under which the antecedent is considered.
3
Differentiate universal from particular conditionals
If the judgment covers all suppositions of the antecedent, it is universal (kulliyya). If it covers only some suppositions, it is particular (juz'iyya). The universal is not about the antecedent itself but about its relation across all suppositions.
The author emphasizes that even intrinsic entailment requires universality across all suppositions.
4
Correct the misconception about universal conditionals
Some mistakenly think a universal conditional judges the antecedent as such. The author refutes this: conditionals always involve suppositions.
This error would render the logical structure of conditionals unintelligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And all of these are the truths of the affirmative [conditionals]. As for their negatives, they are the removal of their affirmations. Thus the negative [conditional] of implication is that in which it is judged by the negation of implication, not by the implication of negation. And on this, analogize to the rest of the negatives. Then the judgment in it – whatever judgment it is – if it is upon a specific supposition, then it is particular and individual; and if not, then a conditional judgment without a supposition is not permissible. Rather, judgments necessarily have suppositions. Then if the quantity of the judgment is specified as that it is upon all suppositions of the antecedent or some of them, then it is quantified: universal if the judgment is upon all suppositions, or particular if the judgment is upon some of them. And know that the antecedent may be entailing the consequent with respect to its own essence, such that it is necessary for it without the intervention of a supposition, whether that necessity is by essence or by cause. So this antecedent, whichever supposition it is taken under, accompanies the consequent. But the suppositions have no role in this accompaniment. Therefore, when one intends to express this implication, one must judge that the consequent persists with the antecedent upon all suppositions; for a conditional judgment without involving suppositions is unintelligible. And sometimes suppositions have a role in this persistence, so the persistence may be upon some suppositions or all suppositions, and that is not known to occur. The universal that is known to occur is the one that expresses the implication of the consequent to the antecedent itself and the judgment upon the suppositions. And whoever claims that in the universal, the judgment is upon the antecedent itself as antecedent without considering the suppositions has erred; for a conditional judgment without suppositions is unintelligible. And the particular that expresses upon some suppositions expresses the implication of the consequent to the antecedent, whether the implication is for some or all suppositions, or for the antecedent itself as antecedent. Thus the particular is more general than the universal. So preserve this, for it will benefit you in places. And the knowledge of the truth is with Allah.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of universal and particular conditional propositions (al-shartiyyat) and clarifies common misconceptions. The author begins by stating that the 'truths' (haqa'iq) of affirmative conditionals are the implications (luzum) they assert; their negatives negate that implication. He then explains that a conditional judgment must be relative to a specific 'supposition' (taqdir) or a range of suppositions. If the judgment is about a single supposition, it is particular or individual. Without any supposition, a conditional judgment is impossible. When the quantity is specified, it becomes either universal (all suppositions) or particular (some suppositions).

The author then refines this: sometimes the antecedent (muqaddam) entails the consequent (tali) intrinsically, without dependence on any supposition. In such a case, the conditional must be universal, as the entailment holds for all suppositions. However, if the entailment depends on suppositions, the conditional may be universal (for all those suppositions) or particular (for some). A common mistake is to think that universal conditionals judge the antecedent in itself without suppositions. The author corrects this: the universal judgment always involves suppositions; it is about the relation of the antecedent to the consequent across all suppositions. The particular is broader because it can either report entailment through some or all suppositions, or through the antecedent itself. This distinction is crucial for understanding conditional logic in Islamic jurisprudence, e.g., 'Whenever (kullama) the sun rises, daytime exists' (universal) versus 'Sometimes (qad) when a woman menstruates, she does not pray' (particular).

تقدير
كلية
جزئية

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are always relative to suppositions; universality quantifies over those suppositions, not over the antecedent's essence.

Quantifiers and Particles for Conditional Propositions
Author lists quantifiers for each type of conditional and explains the usage of conditional particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct quantifier for each type of conditional proposition (universal/particular, affirmative/negative, conjunctive/disjunctive) and distinguish between quantified and indeterminate conditionals.

Core Idea
Conditional quantifiers give direction to implication — without them, the conditional is adrift.

In this passage, the author provides a precise inventory of quantifiers (sawr) for conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, analogous to the quantifiers in categoricals but with suppositions (taqdirāt) instead of individuals. Understanding these quantifiers is essential for correctly interpreting conditional statements in Islamic legal and theological arguments. We'll walk through each type, then examine the particles used when no quantifier is present.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish conjunctive from disjunctive conditionals
Recognize that a conjunctive conditional (muttaṣila) expresses implication (if-then), while a disjunctive conditional (munfaṣila) expresses separation (either-or). This sets the context for the quantifiers.
2
List the four types of quantified conditionals
The author gives quantifiers for universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Note that universal affirmative uses different quantifiers for conjunctive vs disjunctive: 'mata', 'mahma', 'kullama' for conjunctive; 'da'iman' for disjunctive.
3
Understand the role of suppositions
Recall that in conditional propositions, the judgment is about suppositions (taqdirāt). A universal conditional covers all suppositions; a particular covers some. Without specifying suppositions, the conditional is indeterminate (muhmala) and unintelligible.
4
Recognize the particles of indeterminacy (ihmal)
When particles like 'in', 'law', 'idha' (conjunctive) or 'aw', 'imma' (disjunctive) are used without a quantifier, they express an indeterminate conditional. The author rates their strength: 'in' strong, 'mata' weak, 'idha' intermediate.
The distinction between quantified and indeterminate conditionals is crucial for evaluating arguments in usul al-fiqh, where conditional phrasing can imply different degrees of obligation.

And the unrestricted use of 'law', 'in', and 'idha' in the conjunctive, and 'aw' and 'imma' in the disjunctive, is for indeterminacy (ihmal). Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a compact enumeration of the quantifiers (sawr) and particles used in conditional propositions, followed by a note on their shades of meaning. The author first repeats the key point from the previous discussion: universal and particular conditionals always involve suppositions, and the particular is more general. He then clarifies that if the suppositions are not specified, the conditional is 'indeterminate' (muhmala); the 'natural universal' (tabi'iyya) is not applicable here because conditionals require observed suppositions.

The core of the passage lists the four types of quantified conditionals:
- Universal affirmative conjunctive (mutasila): quantifiers are 'mata' (whenever), 'mahma' (whatever), and 'kullama' (every time).
- Universal affirmative disjunctive (munfasila): quantifier is 'da'iman' (always).
- Universal negative (both): quantifier is 'laysa albattata' (not at all).
- Particular affirmative (both): quantifier is 'qad yakunu' (it may be).
- Particular negative (both): quantifier is 'qad la yakunu' (it may not be).
The author notes that a particular negative can be obtained by negating a universal affirmative quantifier, because the negation of 'all' entails 'some not'.

Finally, the passage discusses particles used without quantification, which indicate indeterminacy: 'law' and 'in' and 'idha' for conjunctive, and 'aw' and 'imma' for disjunctive. It ends with a quote from the Sheikh (likely al-Shaykh al-Ra'is Ibn Sina or a later commentator) ranking their strength: 'in' strongly indicates implication, 'mata' is weak, and 'idha' is moderate.

For example, in fiqh: 'In jami'a al-khamr, fa-huwa haram' uses 'in' for strong implication; 'Mata shariqat al-shams, fa-al-naharu mawjud' uses 'mata' for a weaker conditional. This distinction is important for deducing rulings from conditional statements.

Conditional propositions must be quantified to be intelligible; the quantifiers vary by type and mood, and particles used alone indicate indeterminacy.
