The Role of Estimations in Classifying Conditional Propositions
Defines classification by quantifier scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional proposition as universal, particular, or indefinite by analyzing the scope of its estimations.

Core Idea
In conditionals, quantifiers apply to estimations, not to the antecedent itself.

In categorical propositions, quantifiers range over individuals. In conditional propositions, the quantifier ranges over hypothetical conditions called 'estimations' (taqdīrāt). Understanding this distinction is the key to correctly classifying conditionals. The passage explains that a universal conditional holds for all estimations, a particular holds for some, and an indefinite lacks a quantifier. It also warns against mistakenly thinking the quantifier applies to the antecedent alone.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of 'estimations' (taqdīrāt)
Estimations are the hypothetical cases or conditions under which the conditional proposition is considered. For example, in 'If Zayd comes, then Amr leaves', each possible situation (coming or not) is an estimation. The judgment (entailment) is assessed over these estimations.
Estimations are analogous to individuals in categoricals.
2
Distinguish universal from particular based on estimations
A universal conditional asserts that the entailment holds for all estimations. For instance, 'Whenever Zayd comes, Amr leaves' is universal if for every estimation where Zayd comes, Amr leaves. A particular conditional asserts it holds for some estimations – it may or may not hold for all.
The passage says the particular is more general than the universal because it allows both partial and full coverage of estimations.
3
Identify indefinite (muhmala) conditionals
If the quantifier is omitted and no particle explicitly indicates universality or particularity, the proposition is indefinite. In Arabic, particles like 'law', 'in', 'idha' (for conjunctive) and 'aw', 'imma' (for disjunctive) are used for indefinite conditionals. The indefinite is considered neither universal nor particular.
Previously, some logicians treated muhmala as equivalent to particular; here it is explicitly distinguished.
4
Apply the classification to an Islamic science example
In Usul al-Fiqh, a conditional ruling such as 'If the contract is valid, then ownership transfers' may be understood as universal (applies to all valid contracts) or particular (applies only to some). Recognizing the quantifier helps determine the scope of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرطيات
وربما يكون للتقديرات دخل في ذلك الاستصحاب، فإما أن يكون الاستصحاب على بعض التقديرات أو جميع التقديرات، وذلك غير معلوم الوقوع، والكلية المعلومة الوقوع هي الحاكية عن لزوم التالي لنفس المقدم، والحكم على التقادير، ومن زعم أن الحكم في الكلية على نفس المقدم بما هو مقدم من غير اعتبار التقادير فقد أخطأ؛ فإن الحكم الشرطي بدون التقادير غير معقول، والجزئية الحاكية على بعض التقادير حاكية عن لزوم التالي للمقدم، سواء كان اللزوم للتقادير بعضا أو كلا، أو لنفس المقدم بما هو مقدم، فالجزئية أعم من الكلية، فاحفظ هذا؛ فإنه ينفعك في مواضع، والعلم بالحقيقة عند الله.
القضية
(وَإِلَّا) يبيَّن التقادير، بل يحكم على التقادير، سواء كان كلا أو بعضا المهملة في نفس الأمر (فَمُهْمَلَةٌ، وَالطَّبَعِيَّةُ ههنا غير معقولة): إذ الحكم الشرطي من غير ملاحظة التقديرات التي كالأفراد في الحملية غير معقول.
وَسُورُ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ «متى» و«مهما» و«كلما»، وَسُورُ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ فِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ «دائما»، وَسُورُ السَّالِبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ فِيهِمَا «ليس ألبتة»، وَسُورُ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ فِيهِمَا «قد يكون»، وَسُورُ السَّالِبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ فِيهِمَا «قد لا يكون»، والحاصل بإدخال حرف السلب على سور الإيجاب الكلي؛ لأن رفع الإيجاب الكلي مستلزم للسلب الجزئي، وإطلاق «لو وإن» و«إذا» في المتصلة، و«أو» و«إما» في المنفصلة للإهمال.
Chapter on Conditional Propositions. And perhaps the estimations have an influence in that istishāb. So either the istishāb is on some estimations or all estimations, and that is unknown in occurrence. The known universal in occurrence is that which reports about the entailment of the consequent to the antecedent itself, and the judgment upon the estimations. And whoever claims that the judgment in the universal is upon the antecedent itself insofar as it is an antecedent, without consideration of the estimations, has erred; for the conditional judgment without estimations is inconceivable. And the particular that reports about some estimations reports about the entailment of the consequent to the antecedent, whether the entailment is to the estimations partially or wholly, or to the antecedent itself insofar as it is an antecedent. So the particular is more general than the universal. So memorize this, for it will benefit you in many places. And the knowledge of the truth is with God. The proposition: (And otherwise) the estimations are not clarified, but rather judgment is made upon the estimations, whether wholly or partially, the indefinite in actuality (so it is indefinite, and the natural [proposition] here is inconceivable): because the conditional judgment without noting the estimations, which are like individuals in the categorical, is inconceivable. And the quantifier of the universal affirmative in the conjunctive [conditional] is 'matā', 'mahmā', and 'kullamā'. And the quantifier of the universal affirmative in the disjunctive is 'dā'imān'. And the quantifier of the universal negative in both is 'laysa al-battata'. And the quantifier of the particular affirmative in both is 'qad yakūnu'. And the quantifier of the particular negative in both is 'qad lā yakūnu'. And the result by entering the negation particle upon the quantifier of the universal affirmative is [the particular negative]; because the removal of the universal affirmative necessitates the particular negative. And the application of 'law' and 'in' and 'idhā' in the conjunctive, and 'aw' and 'immā' in the disjunctive, is for indefiniteness.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage defines the quantifiers (aswār) for conditional propositions and explains their classification into universal, particular, and indefinite. The commentary first explains the role of 'estimations' (taqdīrāt) in conditionals: unlike categorical propositions which predicate of individuals, conditional propositions involve a relation between two propositions under various hypothetical conditions. A universal conditional is one where the entailment holds for all estimations, while a particular holds for some. The indefinite (muhmala) conditional is one where the quantifier is omitted. The author then lists the specific Arabic quantifiers: 'matā', 'mahmā', 'kullamā' for universal affirmative conjunctive; 'dā'imān' for disjunctive; 'laysa al-battata' for universal negative; 'qad yakūnu' for particular affirmative; 'qad lā yakūnu' for particular negative. He notes that adding a negation to the universal affirmative quantifier yields the particular negative, as lifting the universal affirmative entails the particular negative. Finally, he states that the particles 'law', 'in', 'idhā' (conjunctive) and 'aw', 'immā' (disjunctive) are used for indefinite conditionals. This is important for distinguishing between quantified and unquantified conditional propositions in logical analysis.

كلية شرطية
مهملة

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition's quantifier ranges over hypothetical estimations, not the antecedent itself; the universal covers all estimations, the particular covers some, and the indefinite is unspecified.

The Quantifiers of Conditional Propositions in Arabic
Enumerates Arabic quantifier words for conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and use the correct Arabic quantifier words for each type of conditional proposition (conjunctive/disjunctive, universal/particular, affirmative/negative).

Core Idea
Each conditional type has a distinct quantifier: 'matā' for universal affirmative conjunctive, 'dā'imān' for disjunctive, and 'qad yakūnu' for particular affirmative.

After understanding the role of estimations, we now turn to the actual words used to quantify conditional propositions in Arabic. The passage lists specific quantifiers for each type: 'matā', 'mahmā', 'kullamā' for universal affirmative conjunctive; 'dā'imān' for disjunctive; 'laysa al-battata' for universal negative; 'qad yakūnu' for particular affirmative; 'qad lā yakūnu' for particular negative. It also explains how negation of a universal affirmative yields a particular negative, and notes that particles like 'law', 'in', 'idhā' are used for indefinite conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types of conditional propositions
Conjunctive conditional (muttaṣila) uses 'if…then' structure; disjunctive conditional (munfaṣila) uses 'either…or'. The quantifiers differ for each type.
Universal affirmative conjunctive quantifiers: متى (matā), مهما (mahmā), كلما (kullamā).
2
Memorize the universal affirmative quantifiers
For conjunctive: 'matā', 'mahmā', 'kullamā' all mean 'whenever' or 'every time'. For disjunctive: 'dā'imān' (always). Note that 'dā'imān' is used only for universal affirmative disjunctive.
These are standard in later Mantiq; earlier works may use different terms.
3
Memorize the universal negative and particular quantifiers
Universal negative: 'laysa al-battata' (not at all) for both types. Particular affirmative: 'qad yakūnu' (it may be that). Particular negative: 'qad lā yakūnu' (it may not be that). Forming the particular negative by adding negation to the universal affirmative is an immediate inference.
The passage explicitly derives the particular negative from negation of the universal affirmative: 'رفع الإيجاب الكلي مستلزم للسلب الجزئي'.
4
Identify quantifier in an Islamic science example
In a tafsir statement: 'كلما كان الخطاب عامًا، كان التخصيص جائزًا' (Whenever the address is general, specification is permissible) – the quantifier 'كلما' indicates a universal affirmative conjunctive conditional.
Also note that 'law', 'in', 'idha' are used for indefinite conjunctives, and 'aw', 'imma' for indefinite disjunctives.

Conditional Particles Do Not Indicate Entailment
Critiques the view that particles signal entailment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacious reasoning in attributing logical entailment to conditional particles and explain why particles indicate only conjunction.

Core Idea
Conditional particles signal epistemic modality, not logical necessity.

Many earlier logicians claimed that certain Arabic conditional particles like 'in' strongly indicate entailment, while others are weaker. This passage critically examines that view, arguing that the failure of an example like 'If the resurrection occurs, people are reckoned' stems from the particle's requirement of doubt, not from lack of entailment. Ultimately, the commentator asserts that no particle inherently indicates entailment; only mere conjunction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find the earlier scholar's statement that 'in' strongly indicates entailment, 'matā' weakly, and 'idhā' is intermediate.
2
Examine the counterexample
The commentator says the example 'If the resurrection occurs, people are reckoned' is not a case of entailment because the resurrection is certain and 'in' only enters on doubtful matters. So the particle's meaning is about certainty, not logical connection.
This refutation uses the particle's own semantic condition, not the logical relation.
3
Generalize the critique
The commentator also points out that counting 'idh' as a conditional particle is an error, and that no particle indicates entailment; they all indicate only mere conjunction.
4
Understand the conclusion
The truth is that particles indicate only mere conjunction (ittiṣāl), which includes both entailment and accidental coincidence.
This is a critical refinement: logical connection is not grammatical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ: «إن شديد الدلالة على اللزوم، ومتى ضعيفها، و«إذا» كالمتوسط». قال الشيخ: ههنا حروف شرط في الشرطيات المتصلة تدل على النحو المذكور من اللزوم، وحروف أخرى لا تدل عليه، فالتي تدل عليه لفظة «إن»؛ فإنك لا تقول إن قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس؛ إذ ليس التالي يلزم من وضع المقدم؛ لأن ذلك ليس بضروري، بل إرادي من الله، وتقول «إذا قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس»، وكذلك لا تقول إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالإثنان زوج، والخلاء أيضا معدوم فيشبه أن تكون لفظة إن شديد القوة في الدلالة على اللزوم، ومتى ضعيفة في ذلك، و«إذا» كالمتوسط، ولفظة إذا كان كذا لا تدل على اللزوم ألبتة، وكذا لفظة «كلما» لا تدل أيضا على اللزوم، ولفظة «لما» إذ تقول «لما كان كذا كان كذا» تصلح للأمرين، ولا يوجب أحدهما، اهـ.
(وفيه نظر)؛ فإن عدم صحة «إن قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس» ليس لأجل عدم استلزام قيام القيامة المحاسبة للناس، بل لأن قيام القيامة أمر متيقن الوجود، و«إن» لا يدخل إلا على مشكوك الوجود، وأيضا عدّ «إذ» من حروف الشرط خطأ فاحش، والحق أن لا دلالة لحروف الشرط إلا على مطلق الاتصال، أعم من اللزوم والاتفاق، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
(وأطراف الشرطية لا حكم فيها الآن) أي حال كونها أطرافا؛ ضرورة أن ما يفيد السكوت لا يرتبط بغيره، وما يقصد به الحكاية عن أمر لا يكون مرتبطا بالغير، وهذا ضروري وإنكاره مكابرة.
وما قال بعض الأجلة: إن اقتران حروف الشرط لا ينافي أن يكون الطرفان قضية، بل التركيب معه ينافيه، وكذا اشتمال القضية على النسبة الغير
The Shaykh said: 'In is strong in indicating entailment, matā is weak in it, and idhā is intermediate.' The Shaykh said: Here are conditional particles in conjunctive conditionals that indicate the aforementioned kind of entailment, and other particles that do not indicate it. Those that indicate it are the word 'in'; for you do not say 'If the resurrection occurs, then people are reckoned', because the consequent does not follow from positing the antecedent, since that is not necessary but rather an act of God's will. And you say 'When the resurrection occurs, people are reckoned.' Likewise, you do not say 'If man exists, then two is even', and also 'vacuity is non-existent'. So it appears that the word 'in' is very strong in indicating entailment, 'matā' is weak in that, and 'idhā' is intermediate. And the word 'idhā' when something is such does not indicate entailment at all; likewise the word 'kullamā' also does not indicate entailment; and the word 'lammā' when you say 'when something was such, such was the case' is suitable for both, and does not necessitate one of them. End. (And there is consideration in it): For the incorrectness of 'If the resurrection occurs, then people are reckoned' is not due to the absence of entailment of the reckoning from the occurrence of the resurrection, but because the occurrence of the resurrection is a certain matter of existence, and 'in' only enters upon something doubtful in existence. And also, counting 'idh' among the conditional particles is a gross error. The truth is that there is no indication for conditional particles except of mere conjunction, more general than entailment and coincidence. That is it, and God knows best what is correct. (And the parts of the conditional have no judgment at present), i.e., while they are parts; because what conveys silence does not relate to another, and what is intended to report about something is not related to another. This is self-evident and its denial is obstinacy. And what some eminent scholars said: that the coupling with conditional particles does not preclude the two parts from being a proposition, but rather the composition with it precludes that. Likewise, the inclusion of the proposition of the relative...
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage critically examines the view that certain Arabic conditional particles inherently indicate logical entailment (luzūm). The author quotes a previous scholar (likely al-Qutb) who claimed that 'in' strongly indicates entailment, 'matā' weakly, and 'idhā' is intermediate. The commentator then provides a detailed refutation. First, he argues that the example 'If the resurrection occurs, people are reckoned' is invalid not because entailment is absent, but because 'in' is used only for doubtful matters, while the resurrection is certain. Thus the particle's meaning is about epistemic modality, not logical necessity. He also points out that counting 'idh' as a conditional particle is a mistake. His conclusion is that no particle indicates entailment per se; they only indicate mere conjunction (ittiṣāl), which includes both necessary and accidental connection. This is a significant critical refinement, arguing that logical connection is a matter of meaning, not of particles. The passage then adds a related point: the parts of a conditional proposition (the antecedent and consequent) have no judgment when considered as parts. This is because they are not isolated propositions; their function is tied to the conditional context. The commentator also notes that the presence of conditional particles does not prevent the parts from being propositions, but the composition itself changes their status. This clarifies the nature of conditional propositions in logic.

Key Terms
حروف الشرط (ḥurūf al-sharṭ): Conditional particles like 'in', 'idhā', 'matā', 'kullamā', 'lammā', etc., used in conjunctive conditionals. [Note: In earlier logic, these are simply syntactic markers; here the commentator argues they have no inherent logical force beyond indicating connection.]
لزوم (luzūm): Logical entailment; the necessary connection between antecedent and consequent in a conditional. [Note: No significant shift, but the commentator challenges the idea that particles directly signal this.]
اتصال (ittiṣāl): Mere conjunction or connection between two propositions, broader than entailment, including accidental link. [Note: This usage is specific to the commentator's critique; elsewhere it may mean the connective in conditional propositions.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that the statement 'If the resurrection occurs, people are reckoned' is not a good example of entailment?
A) Because the resurrection is uncertain
B) Because the particle 'in' is only used for doubtful matters
C) Because the antecedent is impossible
D) Because the consequent does not follow

Key Takeaway
Conditional particles do not inherently signal logical entailment; they only indicate a connection, and their usage is governed by epistemic conditions like doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Parts of a Conditional Have No Independent Judgment
Explains that parts of a conditional have no judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the antecedent and consequent of a conditional proposition do not constitute independent judgments.

Core Idea
In a conditional, the parts are not propositions; they are mere topics awaiting connection.

This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial point in logic: the antecedent and consequent of a conditional proposition are not standalone judgments. They only function as parts of the whole conditional. Understanding this prevents confusion when analyzing conditional statements, especially in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the claim
The text says: 'And the parts of the conditional have no judgment at present,' meaning while they are parts. This is the base claim.
2
Understand the reasoning
Because what conveys silence (i.e., when considered in isolation) does not relate to another; and what is intended to report about something is not related to another. So the parts are not independent assertions.
The commentator explains this as self-evident.
3
Apply to an example
Consider the conditional 'If Zayd comes, then Amr goes'. The parts 'Zayd comes' and 'Amr goes' are not asserted or denied independently; they are only evaluated in the context of the conditional.
4
Contrast with a competing view
Some scholars said that coupling with conditional particles does not preclude the parts from being propositions. The commentator argues that the composition itself precludes that.
This refinement strengthens the point that the conditional structure suspends independent judgment.

قال الشيخ: «إن شديد الدلالة على اللزوم، ومتى ضعيفها، و«إذا» كالمتوسط». قال الشيخ: ههنا حروف شرط في الشرطيات المتصلة تدل على النحو المذكور من اللزوم، وحروف أخرى لا تدل عليه، فالتي تدل عليه لفظة «إن»؛ فإنك لا تقول إن قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس؛ إذ ليس التالي يلزم من وضع المقدم؛ لأن ذلك ليس بضروري، بل إرادي من الله، وتقول «إذا قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس»، وكذلك لا تقول إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالإثنان زوج، والخلاء أيضا معدوم فيشبه أن تكون لفظة إن شديد القوة في الدلالة على اللزوم، ومتى ضعيفة في ذلك، و«إذا» كالمتوسط، ولفظة إذا كان كذا لا تدل على اللزوم ألبتة، وكذا لفظة «كلما» لا تدل أيضا على اللزوم، ولفظة «لما» إذ تقول «لما كان كذا كان كذا» تصلح للأمرين، ولا يوجب أحدهما، اهـ.
(وفيه نظر)؛ فإن عدم صحة «إن قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس» ليس لأجل عدم استلزام قيام القيامة المحاسبة للناس، بل لأن قيام القيامة أمر متيقن الوجود، و«إن» لا يدخل إلا على مشكوك الوجود، وأيضا عدّ «إذ» من حروف الشرط خطأ فاحش، والحق أن لا دلالة لحروف الشرط إلا على مطلق الاتصال، أعم من اللزوم والاتفاق، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
(وأطراف الشرطية لا حكم فيها الآن) أي حال كونها أطرافا؛ ضرورة أن ما يفيد السكوت لا يرتبط بغيره، وما يقصد به الحكاية عن أمر لا يكون مرتبطا بالغير، وهذا ضروري وإنكاره مكابرة.
وما قال بعض الأجلة: إن اقتران حروف الشرط لا ينافي أن يكون الطرفان قضية، بل التركيب معه ينافيه، وكذا اشتمال القضية على النسبة الغير
The Shaykh said: 'In is strong in indicating entailment, matā is weak in it, and idhā is intermediate.' The Shaykh said: Here are conditional particles in conjunctive conditionals that indicate the aforementioned kind of entailment, and other particles that do not indicate it. Those that indicate it are the word 'in'; for you do not say 'If the resurrection occurs, then people are reckoned', because the consequent does not follow from positing the antecedent, since that is not necessary but rather an act of God's will. And you say 'When the resurrection occurs, people are reckoned.' Likewise, you do not say 'If man exists, then two is even', and also 'vacuity is non-existent'. So it appears that the word 'in' is very strong in indicating entailment, 'matā' is weak in that, and 'idhā' is intermediate. And the word 'idhā' when something is such does not indicate entailment at all; likewise the word 'kullamā' also does not indicate entailment; and the word 'lammā' when you say 'when something was such, such was the case' is suitable for both, and does not necessitate one of them. End. (And there is consideration in it): For the incorrectness of 'If the resurrection occurs, then people are reckoned' is not due to the absence of entailment of the reckoning from the occurrence of the resurrection, but because the occurrence of the resurrection is a certain matter of existence, and 'in' only enters upon something doubtful in existence. And also, counting 'idh' among the conditional particles is a gross error. The truth is that there is no indication for conditional particles except of mere conjunction, more general than entailment and coincidence. That is it, and God knows best what is correct. (And the parts of the conditional have no judgment at present), i.e., while they are parts; because what conveys silence does not relate to another, and what is intended to report about something is not related to another. This is self-evident and its denial is obstinacy. And what some eminent scholars said: that the coupling with conditional particles does not preclude the two parts from being a proposition, but rather the composition with it precludes that. Likewise, the inclusion of the proposition of the relative...
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage critically examines the view that certain Arabic conditional particles inherently indicate logical entailment (luzūm). The author quotes a previous scholar (likely al-Qutb) who claimed that 'in' strongly indicates entailment, 'matā' weakly, and 'idhā' is intermediate. The commentator then provides a detailed refutation. First, he argues that the example 'If the resurrection occurs, people are reckoned' is invalid not because entailment is absent, but because 'in' is used only for doubtful matters, while the resurrection is certain. Thus the particle's meaning is about epistemic modality, not logical necessity. He also points out that counting 'idh' as a conditional particle is a mistake. His conclusion is that no particle indicates entailment per se; they only indicate mere conjunction (ittiṣāl), which includes both necessary and accidental connection. This is a significant critical refinement, arguing that logical connection is a matter of meaning, not of particles. The passage then adds a related point: the parts of a conditional proposition (the antecedent and consequent) have no judgment when considered as parts. This is because they are not isolated propositions; their function is tied to the conditional context. The commentator also notes that the presence of conditional particles does not prevent the parts from being propositions, but the composition itself changes their status. This clarifies the nature of conditional propositions in logic.

Key Terms
أطراف الشرطية (aṭrāf al-shartiyya): The two parts (antecedent and consequent) of a conditional proposition, which have no judgment in themselves when considered as parts. [Note: Standard doctrine; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because they are always false
D) Because the commentator says so

Key Takeaway
The parts of a conditional are not independent propositions; they only function as parts of a single logical statement.

Classifying Conditional Particles by Implication
Author classifies conditional particles into those indicating necessary implication and those not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional particles (in, idha, mata, kullama, lamma) according to their strength in indicating necessary implication (luzum).

Core Idea
Conditional particles differ in how strongly they imply necessity; 'in' is strongest, 'kullama' weakest.

In this passage, the author categorizes Arabic conditional particles used in connected conditionals. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly analyzing conditional arguments in Islamic logic, as the choice of particle can affect the logical force of the statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particles listed
Locate the five particles mentioned: in, idha, mata, kullama, lamma. Note their Arabic forms.
2
Understand the strength scale
The author ranks 'in' as strongest in indicating implication, 'mata' as weak, 'idha' as intermediate, and 'kullama' as not indicating implication at all. 'Lamma' is described as suitable for both.
3
Analyze the example with 'in' and the resurrection
The example 'If the resurrection occurs, God will account people' is deemed invalid with 'in' because the resurrection is certain, not doubtful. This shows that 'in' requires a doubtful antecedent.
The author's point is that 'in' indicates implication but its use is restricted to uncertain matters.
4
Distinguish between implication and absolute connection
The final part of the passage introduces a critical note (nazr) that challenges the initial classification. However, for this lesson, focus on the primary classification and the idea that particles signal different strengths.
The deeper principle that particles only indicate connection will be explored in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ: ههنا حروف شرط في الشرطيات المتصلة تدل على النحو المذكور من اللزوم، وحروف أخرى لا تدل عليه، فالتي تدل عليه لفظة «إن» ؛ فإنك لا تقول إن قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس ؛ إذ ليس التالي يلزم من وضع المقدم ؛ لأن ذلك ليس بضروري، بل إرادي من الله الله ، وتقول «إذا قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس ، وكذلك لا تقول إن كان الإنسان موجودا فالإثنان زوج، والخلاء أيضا معدوم فيشبه أن تكون لفظة إن شديدة القوة في الدلالة على اللزوم، ومتى ضعيفة في ذلك، و«إذا» كالمتوسط، ولفظة إذا كان كذا لا تدل على اللزوم ألبتة، وكذا لفظة «كلما» لا تدل أيضا على اللزوم، ولفظة «لما» إذ تقول «لما كان كذا كان كذا» تصلح للأمرين، ولا أحدهما، اهـ . يوجب (وَفِيهِ نَظْرٌ)؛ فإن عدم صحة «إن قامت القيامة فيحاسب الناس» ليس لأجل عدم استلزام قيام القيامة المحاسبة للناس، بل لأن قيام القيامة أمر متيقن الوجود، و«إن» لا يدخل إلا على مشكوك الوجود وأيضا عَدَّ «إذ» من حروف الشرط خطأ فاحش والحق أن لا دلالة لحروف الشرط إلا على مطلق الاتصال، أعم من اللزوم والاتفاق، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
The author said: Here there are conditional particles in connected conditionals that indicate the mentioned manner of implication, and other particles that do not indicate it. So those that indicate it are the word 'in' (if); for you do not say 'If the resurrection occurs, then God will account people' because the consequent does not follow from the placement of the antecedent; because that is not necessary but volitional from God (الله الله). And you say 'When the resurrection occurs, then God will account people'. And likewise you do not say 'If man exists, then two is even', and emptiness is also non‑existent, so it seems that the word 'in' is very strong in indicating implication, while 'mata' (whenever) is weak in that, and 'idha' (when) is intermediate. And the phrase 'idha kana kadha' does not indicate implication at all, and likewise the word 'kullama' (every time) also does not indicate implication. And the word 'lamma' (since), when you say 'lamma kana kadha kana kadha' (since it was so, it was so), is suitable for both possibilities, not just one. End. He says: There is a consideration in it; for the incorrectness of 'If the resurrection occurs, then God will account people' is not due to the non‑implication of the occurrence of the resurrection necessitating the accounting for people, but because the occurrence of the resurrection is a certain matter, and 'in' only enters on a doubtful existence. Also, counting 'idh' among the conditional particles is a gross error. The truth is that conditional particles only indicate absolute connection, more general than implication and coincidence. This, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage classifies the conditional particles used in muttasila (connected conditionals) according to their ability to indicate necessary implication (luzum). The author begins by stating that some particles indicate implication in the manner previously mentioned, while others do not. He then details that 'in' (if) is the strongest indicator of implication, but warns that it is only used with matters that are uncertain (mashkuk) as opposed to certain (mutayaqqan). The example 'If the resurrection occurs, then God will account people' is invalid because the resurrection is certain, not doubtful. He distinguishes 'idha' (when) as intermediate, 'mata' (whenever) as weak, 'kullama' (every time) as not indicating implication, and 'lamma' (since) as suitable for both possibilities. Then the author presents a critical note (nazr) that the incorrectness of using 'in' with the resurrection is not due to lack of implication but to the certainty of the antecedent. He also criticizes counting 'idh' among conditional particles. His final stance is that conditional particles indicate only absolute connection (mutlaq al-ittisal), which includes both necessary implication and mere coincidence (ittifaq). This is a crucial point: the particles do not by themselves guarantee logical necessity; that must be judged by content.

إذا
لما
اللزوم
الاتصال
الاتفاق

Key Takeaway
The choice of conditional particle affects the perceived logical strength of a conditional, but the particle alone does not guarantee necessity.

Conditional Particles Indicate Absolute Connection, Not Necessity
Author criticizes the earlier classification and asserts that particles indicate only absolute connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between necessary implication (luzum) and absolute connection (ittisal) and critique the earlier classification using the commentator's arguments.

Core Idea
Conditional particles only indicate connection; whether that connection is necessary or coincidental depends on the content, not the particle.

The commentator challenges the initial classification of conditional particles, arguing that the incorrectness of certain examples stems from the particle's restriction to doubtful antecedents, not from lack of implication. He then delivers the crucial insight: conditional particles in themselves indicate only absolute connection (ittisal), which encompasses both necessary implication (luzum) and mere coincidence (ittifaq). This principle is vital for evaluating the logical strength of conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the critical note (nazr)
Locate the phrase 'وَفِيهِ نَظْرٌ' and the argument that follows. The commentator is objecting to the idea that 'in' lacks implication in the resurrection example.
2
Understand the objection
The commentator says the incorrectness is not due to non-implication, but because the resurrection is certain and 'in' requires a doubtful antecedent. This shows the limitation is about certainty, not logical force.
3
Recognize the error of including 'idh'
The commentator points out that counting 'idh' among conditional particles is a gross error, refining the classification.
4
Grasp the final truth about absolute connection
The culmination: 'لا دلالة لحروف الشرط إلا على مطلق الاتصال' – particles only indicate absolute connection, which includes both necessary implication and coincidence. This overrides the initial classification.
This is the key takeaway: never assume necessity from a particle alone.

Truth Criterion for Conditional Propositions
Truth criterion for conditionals is the relation, not the parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth criterion for an Arabic conditional proposition by distinguishing between the relational judgment (ittisal/infisal) and the judgments of its parts.

Core Idea
For conditional propositions, truth lies in the relation, not in the parts.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common confusion about conditional propositions (shartiyyat). Many students mistakenly think that a conditional is true only if its antecedent and consequent are true. The author explains that, unlike categoricals, conditionals have no independent judgment in their parts; instead, truth is determined solely by the relation of connection (ittisal) or disconnection (infisal) between them. Understanding this distinction is crucial for working with conditional arguments in Islamic logic, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify structure of conditional
A conditional (shartiyya) consists of two parts (muqaddam and tali) linked by a conditional particle. Note that these parts are not independent propositions with truth values until they are connected.
In Arabic, conditionals are introduced by particles like 'in' or 'idha'.
2
Understand why parts have no judgment
The author argues that judgment in the parts is not necessary: before they become parts, they are not judged; after analysis (removing particles), the conditional structure is lost. So the parts themselves do not carry a truth value within the conditional.
This is why the criterion shifts to the relation.
3
Contrast with categoricals
In a categorical (hamliyya), truth depends on affirmation/negation of subject-predicate. In a conditional, there is no such internal judgment; the only relevant judgment is the relation.
4
Apply the criterion
For a conditional muttasila, truth means the antecedent implies the consequent; for munfasila, truth means the disjuncts are mutually exclusive. Test: 'If the sun rises, then it is day' is true because of the connection, not because the parts are true.
5
Handle exceptional cases
If the parts themselves have judgments (e.g., they are complex propositions), then the truth criterion becomes the sum of the parts' judgments and the relational judgment. This is an advanced case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلاَ يَلْزَمُ الحكم في الأطراف قَبْلَهُ، أي قبل كونها أطرافا؛ فإن ذلك ليس ضروريا، وَلاَ يَلْزَمُ الحكم بَعْدَ التَّحْلِيل، أي بعد حذف أدوات الشرط؛ لأن الانحلال إلى ما منه التركيب، ولما كان التركيب من القضايا ممتنعا فكذا التحليل، إلا بعد اعتبار المعتبر. وَمِنْ ثَمَّ، أي من أجل أن أطراف الشرطية لا حكم فيها بالفعل كَانَ مَنَاطُ صِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ وَكَذِبِهَا هُوَ الْحُكْمُ بِالاِتِّصَالِ وَالاِنْفِصَالِ، لا الحكم الذي في الأطراف لعدمه، بخلاف ما إذا كان فيها حكم؛ إذ حينئذ يصير مناط الصدق مجموع حكمي الطرفين والحكم بالاتصال والانفصال؛ لامتناع صدق الكل وتحققه من غير صدق الجزء وتحققه. فما قال بعض الشراح المقصود من الشرطية الحكم بالاتصال والانفصال، فمناط الصدق والكذب عليه سواء كان في الأطراف حكم أم لا، فالتفريع في غير محله ساقط، كَالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ، أي كما أن مناط الصدق والكذب الحكم بالاتصال والانفصال، كذلك مناط الإيجاب والسلب الحكم بهما. نَعَمْ، يَكُونُ كل واحد من الطرفين شَبِيهَةً بِحَمْلِيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ، والأمثلة واضحة. وَتَلَازُمُ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ وَتَعَانُدُهَا مَعَ قِلَّةِ جَدْوَاهَا، أي نفعها وعدم صحة بياناتها إلا إذا كان الطرفان من الممكنات مَبْسُوطٌ فِي الْمُطَوَّلَاتِ التي للمتأخرين، فلا فائدة في الإيراد.
And judgment in the parts is not necessary before it, i.e., before they become parts; for that is not necessary, and judgment after analysis is not necessary, i.e., after removing the conditional particles; because decomposition is into what from which the composition was made, and since composition of propositions is impossible, so likewise analysis, except after the consideration of a considerer. And hence, i.e., because the parts of a conditional have no actual judgment, the criterion of truth and falsity of the conditional is the judgment of connection and disconnection, not the judgment that is in the parts due to its absence, contrary to when there is judgment in them; for then the criterion of truth becomes the sum of the two judgments of the parts and the judgment of connection and disconnection; because it is impossible for the whole to be true and realized without the part being true and realized. So what some commentators said that the intent of the conditional is the judgment of connection and disconnection, and the criterion of truth and falsity is upon it whether there is judgment in the parts or not, so the branching is out of place and dropped. Like affirmation and negation, i.e., just as the criterion of truth and falsity is the judgment of connection and disconnection, likewise the criterion of affirmation and negation is the judgment of them. Yes, each of the two parts becomes similar to two categoricals, or two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or two different ones, and the examples are clear. And the mutual implication of conditionals and their opposition, with their little benefit, i.e., their benefit and the unsoundness of their explanations unless the two parts are from the possibles, is expounded in the detailed works of the later scholars, so there is no benefit in presenting it.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage explains the criterion for the truth and falsity of conditional propositions (shartiyya). The author begins by stating that judgment in the parts (al-atraf) is not necessary before they become parts nor after analysis (tahlil) because decomposition goes back to the original composition, and since composition of propositions is impossible, analysis is also impossible except by a considerer. The key point is that since the parts of a conditional have no actual judgment (hukm), the criterion for truth and falsity is solely the judgment of connection (ittisal) or disconnection (infisal). This is contrasted with cases where the parts do have judgment, in which case the criterion is the combination of the judgments of the parts and the relational judgment. The author then criticizes some commentators who claimed that the relational judgment alone is always the criterion, regardless of whether the parts have judgment; he asserts that their view is incorrect. He uses the analogy of affirmation (ijab) and negation (salab): just as the criterion for a categorical proposition is the judgment of affirmation or negation, for conditionals it is the judgment of connection or disconnection. Finally, he mentions that each part of a conditional can resemble two categoricals, two conditionals, two disjunctives, or two different ones, and that the detailed discussion of mutual implication (talazum) and opposition (ta'anud) is found in longer works, so it is not covered here.

Key Terms
شَرْطِيَّة
مَنَاط الصِّدْق
اِتِّصَال
اِنْفِصَال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a conditional proposition (shartiyya), what is the sole criterion for truth and falsity according to the author?
A) The truth of the antecedent part
B) The truth of the consequent part
C) The judgment of connection or disconnection
D) The sum of the judgments of both parts

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conditional proposition is determined by the relation (connection or disconnection) between its parts, not by the truth of the parts themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an Islamic example of a conditional muttasila (e.g., from fiqh) and state whether it is true or false based on the connection. Write the conditional in Arabic and explain the relational judgment.
?
Identify a conditional in a kalam text (e.g., from al-Sanusiyya) and determine its truth criterion: does it depend on the parts or on the relation? Justify your answer using the author's reasoning.

Conditions for Mutual Entailment (Talazum)
Author sets conditions for mutual entailment: causation or common effect; discusses correlatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid relationships that justify mutual entailment between two propositions and evaluate the correlatives objection.

Core Idea
Mutual entailment requires a causal link — either direct causation or shared effect — not mere coexistence.

In this passage, the author lays down the well-known conditions for two propositions to be mutually entailing (talazum). Without a proper relationship, any two existents could be claimed to entail each other. The author argues that the relationship must be either one causing the other or both being effects of a single cause. He then addresses an objection from correlatives (mutadayifan) and explains why some proposed alternatives fail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
The author states that mutual entailment requires a relationship; otherwise, any two existents would be mutually entailing. Recognize that this is a necessary condition to avoid trivial entailment.
2
Extract the Two Valid Relationships
The author lists two relationships: one is a cause of the other, or both are effects of a single cause. Note that if only one is a cause, entailment is one-sided.
Causation here means positive cause (علة موجبة).
3
Analyze the Correlatives Objection
Correlatives like father and son entail each other without one causing the other. Understand why this is an objection to the causality condition.
The author dismisses the solution that correlation itself is a sufficient condition, calling it rhetorical.
4
Understand the Author's Preferred Solution
The author adopts the view that both correlatives are effects of a third cause. This preserves the common-effect condition.
This requires two principles: effect cannot fail its complete cause, and independent causes cannot converge on one effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات فِيهَا مَبَاحِثُ الْأَوَّلُ : قَدِ اشْتَهَرَ بَيْنَ الْقَوْمِ أَنَّ الْمُتَلَازِمَيْنِ يَجِبْ أن يكون بينهما علاقة، وإلا لصار كل موجودين متلازمين، وهي أن يكون أَحَدُهُمَا عِلَّةً موجبة لِلْآخَرِ ، أَوْ كِلَاهُمَا مَعْلُولَي عِلَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ موجبة لهما، أما إذا كان أحدهما علة موجبة كان بينهما لزوم من جانب فقط، ونوقض بالمتضايفين، فبعضهم كالإمام وأتباعه عَمَّموا العلاقة، وقالوا: لا بد للتلازم أحد الأمرين: العلية أو التضايف، ولم يفهموا أن هذا شأن الخطابيات، وبعضهم قالوا: هما معلولا ثالث ، واختاره المصنف وقال: (كَالْمُتَضَابِفَيْنِ). هذا مبني على أمرين أحدهما: عدم جواز تخلف المعلول عن العلة الموجبة ، وقد عرفت حاله في صدر الكتاب، والثاني: امتناع توارد العلل المستقلة على معلول واحد، ولو على البدل، وهو مبين في الواحد الشخصي، وما يتراءا في بعض الصور من تعدد العلل فبالحقيقة القدر المشترك. وكون تحصل المعلول أقوى من تحصل العلة، وإن كان ممنوعا في الفاعل المستقل بالتأثير، لكنه غير ممنوع في العلة التامة ؛ على ما صرح به الشيخ في إلهيات «الشفاء» . (۱) قال عبد الحكيم في حواشي الشمسية (۹۰/۲): «لا كيفما اتفق ، وإلا لكانت الموجودات بأسرها متلازمة ؛ لكونها معلولة للواجب، بل لا بد ذلك مع من اقتضاء تلك العلة ارتباط أحدهما بالآخر بحيث يمتنع الانفكاك بينهما كيلا يكون مجرد مصاحبة، كالفلك الأول ؛ والعقل الثاني، كذا أفاده المحقق الطوسي».
Continuation for the topic of conditionals. It contains topics; the first: It is well-known among the people that the two mutually entailing must have a relationship between them, otherwise every two existents would become mutually entailing. And that relationship is that one of them is a positive cause for the other, or both are effects of a single cause that is positive for them. But if one is a positive cause, there is entailment from only one side. And it was objected with correlatives (mutadayifan). Some, like the Imam and his followers, generalized the relationship and said: For entailment, one of two things is necessary: causality or correlation (tadayuf). And they did not understand that this is the affair of rhetorical statements. And some said: they are both effects of a third (cause), and the author chose this and said: like the two correlatives. This is based on two matters: one: the impossibility of the effect failing the positive cause, and you have known its status in the beginning of the book; and the second: the impossibility of independent causes converging on a single effect, even alternately, and this is explained in the topic of the individual unity. And what appears in some forms of multiplicity of causes, in reality it is the common factor. And that the attainment of the effect is stronger than the attainment of the cause—although this is prohibited in the independent agent of influence, it is not prohibited in the complete cause, as the Shaykh stated in the Ilahiyyat of al-Shifa'.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the discussion on conditional propositions, specifically addressing mutual entailment (talazum). The author states the well-known rule that two mutually entailing propositions must have a relationship between them; otherwise, any two existents would be mutually entailing. The required relationship is either: (1) one is a positive cause of the other, resulting in one-sided entailment; or (2) both are effects of a single positive cause. Objections are raised using the example of correlatives (mutadayifan, e.g., father and son), which entail each other without one being the cause. Some scholars, like the Imam (al-Ghazali?) and his followers, generalized the relationship to include correlation (tadayuf) as an alternative to causality. However, the author dismisses this as merely rhetorical. Other scholars propose that both are effects of a third cause, and the author of this commentary (or the matn author) chooses this view. The passage then explains that this position rests on two principles: (1) an effect cannot fail its sufficient cause, and (2) independent causes cannot converge on a single effect (even alternately). The footnote from 'Abd al-Hakim al-Siyalkuti adds that the relationship must be such that the connection is by way of the cause's requirement, preventing mere coexistence, and gives examples like the First Heaven and the Second Intellect.

Key Terms
تَلَازُم
عِلَّة
مُتَضَايِف
عِلَّة تَامَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which relationships justify mutual entailment between two propositions?
A) One is the cause of the other, or both are effects of a single cause
B) They are correlatives, like father and son
C) They have the same meaning
D) They are both created by Allah

Key Takeaway
Mutual entailment between propositions requires a causal relationship — either one causes the other or both share a common cause — and mere correlation or coexistence is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair: 'The Qur'an is the word of Allah' and 'Allah speaks'. Does this pair exhibit mutual entailment according to the conditions given? Explain using either causation or common effect.
?

Critique: Mutual Implication Without Causality
Author critiques the view that two things must have a causal relation to be mutually necessary, using correlatives as counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical fallacy of excluding correlatives from a rule that applies equally to them, and reconstruct the author's counterexample against causal reductionism.

Core Idea
Correlatives refute the claim that mutual implication always requires causality.

In this passage, the author critiques the view that two things must have a causal relation to be mutually necessary. He uses correlatives like father and son to show that mutual implication can exist without causal dependence. He also warns against the fallacy of establishing a rule with a proof and then excepting a case that the proof covers equally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being critiqued
Find the statement attributed to 'some scholars' that it is impossible for two things to be necessarily together without a causal relation.
This is the target doctrine.
2
Extract the counterexample
The author uses correlatives (المتضايفين) like father and son. Note that each cannot exist without the other, yet neither causes the other.
Correlatives are conceptually interdependent.
3
Analyze the refutation of Mullā Ṣadrā's response
Mullā Ṣadrā argued that even correlatives have dependence. The author refutes this by distinguishing between intrinsic mutual implication and extrinsic dependence (on a substrate).
The dependence is not on the correlative itself but on something outside.
4
Identify the logical fallacy of selective exception
The author notes that if someone establishes a rule with a proof, they cannot except a case that falls under the same proof without justification. This would be a fallacy.
The proof applies equally to correlatives, so they cannot be excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات المعارف الإلهية والعلوم الفلسفية أبي، قدس سره، والله أعلم. ثم اعلم : أنه قال الشيخ المقتول في حكمة الإشراق»: «وما ظن بعض أهل العلم - أنه لا يتصور أن يكون] شيئان، كل [واحد] منهما مع الآخر بالضرورة - ينتقض عليه بالمتضايفين ؛ فإنه لا يتصور وجود كل [واحد] منهما إلا مع الآخر [بالضرورة]» (۲) . ورد عليه الصدر الشيرازي بأن في المتضايفين افتقارا أيضا ؛ فإن الحقيقيين يفتقر كل منهما إلى معروض الآخر ، والمشهوريين جزء كل منهما إلى جزء الآخر، كما قال الطوسي، وهو فاسد ؛ فإن المتضايفين نفسهما متلازمان ، فالافتقار الذي لكل منهما إلى معروض الآخر افتقار في أمر أجنبي من المتلازمين، فثبت التلازم بين المعنيين من غير افتقار . ثم قال: وحجته أن كل واحد منهما إن استغنى عن الآخر فيصح وجوده دونه، وإن كان لكل واحد منهما مدخل في وجود الآخر، فيتوقف كل منهما على الآخر، وإن كان لأحدهما مدخل في وجود الآخر، فيتقدم، فلا معية، وهذا إذا منع لا يقدر على إقامة الحجة عليه ثم هو بعينه متوجه في هذا المتضايفين في وجودهما العيني، وفي وجوب تعلقهما معا، وربما يستثني القائل المتضايفين عن القاعدة ومن جملة المغالطة أن تثبت قاعدة بحجة ، يستثنى عنها شيء، يكون نسبة الحجة إليه وإلى غيره على السواء، مما يدخل تحت القاعدة من دون حجة .
Continuation of the discussion of conditionals. The divine sciences and philosophical sciences of Abu, may his secret be sanctified, and God knows best. Then know: The murdered sheikh said in the Wisdom of Illumination: 'What some scholars thought - that it is inconceivable that two things, each necessarily together with the other - is refuted by correlatives; for it is inconceivable that each of them exists except with the other necessarily.' And al-Sadr al-Shirazi replied to him that in correlatives there is also dependence; for the real ones each depend on the substrate of the other, and the common ones each part depends on the part of the other, as al-Tusi said, and it is false; for the correlatives themselves are mutually implicative, so the dependence that each has on the substrate of the other is a dependence on something extrinsic to the implicative pair, so mutual implication between the two meanings is established without dependence. Then he said: His argument is that if each of them is independent of the other, then its existence without the other is valid; if each has an entry in the existence of the other, then each depends on the other; if one has an entry in the existence of the other, then it precedes, so there is no coexistence; and if this is denied, he cannot establish a proof against it. Then it is exactly the same in these correlatives in their concrete existence and in the necessity of their mutual connection. Perhaps the proponent excludes correlatives from the rule, and among the fallacies is to establish a rule with a proof, then except something from it, while the proof applies equally to that thing and to others, such that it falls under the rule without proof.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum, a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum, addresses a critique of a claim made by Suhrawardi in Hikmat al-Ishraq. Suhrawardi stated that some scholars believe it is impossible for two things to be necessarily concomitant without a causal relation. The author counters that this is refuted by the example of correlatives (mutadāyifān), such as father and son, which necessarily exist together but are not causally dependent on each other in the sense required. Mullā Ṣadrā responded that even in correlatives there is dependence: the real ones depend on each other's substrate, and the common ones depend on each other's parts, as per Ṭūsī. The author rejects this, arguing that the correlatives themselves are mutually implicative (mutalāzimān), and the dependence on the substrate is extrinsic to the implicative pair. Therefore, mutual implication exists without causal dependence. The passage then discusses a potential objection: if someone denies that two things can be mutually necessary without a cause, they can offer an argument based on independence or dependence, but this argument applies equally to correlatives, so excluding them would be a fallacy. The author stresses that the proof for the rule must hold for all cases equally, otherwise it is invalid.

Key Terms
المتضايفين
التلازم
الافتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to refute the claim that mutual implication requires a causal relation?
A) The existence of the Necessary Being and its non-existence
B) The relationship between father and son (correlatives)
C) The relationship between cause and effect
D) The relationship between genus and species

Key Takeaway
Mutual implication can exist without causality, as demonstrated by correlatives; excluding a case from a rule without evidence is a fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Intellect's Judgment on Impossible Antecedents
Epistemological discussion on conditionals with impossible antecedents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the intellect cannot directly judge impossible antecedents in conditionals and identify the conditions under which implication holds for impossibles.

Core Idea
The intellect judges reality; impossibles are exempt, but their implications still hold.

This passage examines a subtle epistemological issue: can the intellect rule on conditional statements with impossible antecedents? The author clarifies that while mere supposition of impossibles yields no real judgment, the implications that impossibles entail are genuine and fall under rational scrutiny. This distinction is critical for avoiding overextension of reason in theological and philosophical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence where the author states that when something is outside reality, it is not under the intellect's judgment. This is the core epistemological principle.
The phrase 'خَارِجًا عَنْهُ، أي عن الواقع (لَمْ يَكُنْ تَحْتَ حُكْمِهِ)' marks this claim.
2
Examine the example of indirect judgment
The author gives an example: judgment about the First Intellect and the Necessary is allowed because it follows via contrapositive from a conditional that is itself about reality. Understand how indirect judgment differs from direct judgment on impossibles.
The example is about 'كلما وجد العقل الأول وجد الواجب' and its contrapositive.
3
Analyze the objection (nazar)
The author raises two points: first, premises of proofs may involve indirect judgment, which is conceded; second, impossibles have real judgments (e.g., implication) that are under the intellect's judgment. See how this challenges the initial claim.
Look for 'وفيه نظر' and the following 'أما أولا' and 'أما ثانيا'.
4
Apply the final resolution
The author concludes that impossibles can necessitate impossibles, and this necessitation can be necessary, theoretical, or doubtful. Distinguish between direct judgment (on the impossible itself) and judgment on its implications.
Examples like 'If the body were an accident, it would be in a subject' illustrate necessary implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات أَصْلًا)، والمراد نفي الجزم ابتداء كليا ؛ فإنه قد يجزم به إذا كان لازما لجزم آخر، كما إذا جزمنا بقولنا كلما وجد العقل الأول وجد الواجب»، فيلزم أن يجزم بواسطة عكس النقيض أنه كلما لم يوجد الواجب - العياذ بالله ـ لم يوجد العقل الأول» ، كذا في «الحاشية». (نَعَمْ ، التَّجْوِيزُ لَا حَجْرَ فِيهِ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ؛ فَإِنَّ الْعَقْلَ حَاكِمٌ فِي عَالَم الْوَاقِعِ ، وَإِذَا كَانَ شَيْءٍ خَارِجًا عَنْهُ ، أي عن الواقع (لَمْ يَكُنْ تَحْتَ حُكْمِهِ)، أي العقل، (وَمُجَرَّدُ فَرْضِهِ لَهُ مِنْهُ أي فرض العقل للمحال من الواقع (لا يُجْدِي فِي جَرَيَانِ الْحُكْمِ الْوَاقِعِيَّ ] ) ، وَبَقَاءُ الْأَحْكَامِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ فِي عَالَمِ التَّقْدِيرِ مَشْكُوكُ)، فلا يمكن حكم العقل على المحال ؛ لكونه خارجا عن الواقع باللزوم أصلا . وفيه نظر، أما أولا فلأن مقدمات الدليل تجري فيما يجزم العقل بالواسطة ، وقد سلم الجزم فيه . وأما ثانيا: فلأن للمحال أحكاما واقعية من غير اعتبار المعتبر، وتلك الأحكام تحت حكم العقل قطعا، ومن جملتها اللزوم، وكون وجود المحال خارجا عن الواقع، وعدم حكم العقل به لا يكون أحكامه النفس الأمرية خارجة عن حكم العقل، بل الحق أن المحال ربما يستلزم المحال، كالممكن يستلزم الممكن وربما يكون هذا الاستلزام ضروريا، كقولنا إن كان الجسم عرضا كان في موضوع » ، وربما يكون نظريا ، كقولنا إن لم ينقسم جوهر ذو وضع لكان ينقسم ، وربما لا يستلزم ضرورة أو بدليل ، كلو كان الجسم عرضا كان واجبا بالذات، وربما يكون مشكوكا . هذا، وحقيقة الأمر عند الله تعالى سبحانه .
Continuation of the section on conditionals. Originally, the intended meaning is the negation of absolute initial judgment; for it may be judged when it is necessary for another judgment, as when we judge 'Whenever the First Intellect exists, the Necessary exists,' it follows by contrapositive that 'Whenever the Necessary does not exist — God forbid — the First Intellect does not exist,' as in the Hashiyah. Yes, possibility has no restriction, and that is the truth; for the intellect judges in the realm of reality, and when something is outside it, i.e., outside reality, it is not under its judgment, i.e., the intellect's. Mere supposition of it from it, i.e., the intellect's supposition of the impossible from reality, does not avail in the application of real judgment. And the persistence of real judgments in the world of estimation is doubtful, so the intellect cannot judge the impossible because it is outside reality by necessity originally. But there is consideration: First, because the premises of the proof apply to what the intellect judges indirectly, and he has conceded judgment in it. Second, because the impossible has real judgments without consideration of a considerer, and those judgments are definitely under the intellect's judgment, among them implication, and the fact that the existence of the impossible is outside reality and the intellect does not judge it does not mean that its primary essential judgments are outside the intellect's judgment. Rather, the truth is that the impossible sometimes necessitates the impossible, just as the possible necessitates the possible, and sometimes this necessitation is necessary, as in our saying 'If the body were an accident, it would be in a subject,' and sometimes it is theoretical, as in our saying 'If it does not divide, a substance with position would divide,' and sometimes it does not necessitate necessarily or through evidence, like 'If the body were an accident, it would be necessary by essence,' and sometimes it is doubtful. This, and the true reality is with Allah, Exalted.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the scope of rational judgment in conditional propositions, specifically regarding impossible antecedents. The author begins by clarifying that the negation of absolute initial judgment is intended; sometimes we judge indirectly through contrapositive, as in the example of the First Intellect and the Necessary. Then he affirms that possibility (tajwiz) is unrestricted, and that the intellect judges only within the realm of reality (al-waqi'). If something is impossible and outside reality, it is not subject to the intellect's judgment. However, a mere supposition of the impossible does not cause real judgments to apply. The author then raises a consideration (wa fihi nazar): first, that premises of proofs may involve indirect judgment, which is conceded; second, that the impossible has real judgments (e.g., implications) that are under the intellect's judgment. He concludes that the impossible can necessitate the impossible, just as the possible necessitates the possible, and this necessitation can be necessary, theoretical, or doubtful. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that the intellect can rule on impossible matters directly, emphasizing that rational judgment applies only to what is within reality.

Key Terms
التجويز
المحال
اللزوم
الحكم الواقعي

D) Because they are unknown to the intellect due to their impossibility.

Key Takeaway
The intellect judges only within reality, but the implications of impossible antecedents can still be rationally assessed.

Ibn Sina's Restriction of Estimates in Universal Conditionals
Ibn Sina qualifies the estimates for universal conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that estimates must satisfy for a universal conditional to be true, and explain why that condition is necessary.

Core Idea
For a universal conditional to hold, estimates must be compatible with the antecedent.

In logic, universal conditionals like 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' are fundamental. But without restrictions, such statements can become false. Ibn Sina introduced a key qualifier: the estimates (hypothetical scenarios) under which the conditional is considered must be possible to coexist with the antecedent. This lesson unpacks his reasoning from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main elements
Recognize that the passage discusses 'estimates and positions' (التقادير والأوضاع) and the 'antecedent' (المقدم) of a conditional. The estimates are the hypothetical scenarios under which the conditional is evaluated.
2
Understand the condition for universality
Ibn Sina states that estimates must be 'possible to coexist with the antecedent' (يمكن اجتماعها مع المقدم). Even if the estimates are impossible in themselves, they must at least be compatible with the antecedent. For example, the scenario 'Zayd is standing' can coexist with the antecedent 'Zayd is laughing'.
The estimates themselves can be logically impossible (like 'the donkey is laughing'), but they must still be able to be true simultaneously with the antecedent.
3
See the consequence of violating the condition
If we allow estimates that are incompatible with the antecedent, then no universal conditional would be true. For example, if we assume the antecedent with the absence of the consequent, the consequent does not follow. This shows that universality would break.
The passage gives examples: in a conditional, assuming the antecedent without the consequent violates the implication; in a disjunctive, assuming the antecedent with the consequent does not contradict it.
4
Compare the commentator's view and the author's correction
Some commentators argue that the antecedent alone requires the consequent independently of estimates. But the author points out a flaw: estimates are essential to conditionals, just as individuals are to categoricals. Without estimates, conditionals cannot be understood as universal.
The author emphasizes that the restriction is necessary to preserve the truth of universal conditionals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثَّالِثُ): الشيخ (الرَّئِيسُ قَيَّدَ التَّقَادِيرَ وَالْأَوْضَاعَ) التي سبقت (في تَفْسِيرِ الكليةِ بِالَّتِي يُمْكِنُ اجْتِمَاعُهَا مَعَ الْمُقَدَّم ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُحَالَةٌ في أَنْفُسِهَا) ، فمنى كلما كان (أ) (ب) فـ (ج) (د) أن كون (ج) (د) لازم لكون (أ) (ب) في كل وقت وفي كل وضع ، من قيام زيد، وضحكه وكونه ناطقا، والحمار ضاحكا، وغيرها مما يمكن اجتماعها مع كون (أ) (ب). (وَبَيَّنَ (٢) بِأَنَّهُ لَوْ عَمَّمْنَا يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ كُلَّيَّةٌ أَصْلًا)، لا متصلة ولا منفصلة؛ (فَإِنَّهُ إِذَا فُرِضَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَعَ عَدَمِ التّالي)، ومع عدم لزومه للمقدم، (أو مَعَ وُجُودِهِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ التَّالِيَ) في المتصلة على الأولين؛ لأن عدم الشيء لا يستلزم وجوده ، (وَلَا يُنَافِيهِ في المنفصلة على الثالث؛ فإن وجود الشيء لا يعاند نفسه . قال بعض الشراح: إن الحكم في الكلية ليس إلا بلزوم التالي نفس المقدم من حيث هو، والمقدم مستقل في اقتضاء التالي، ولا دخل فيه للتقدير، وإلا لما كان المقدم مستقلا في الاقتضاء، فالمقدم على أي تقدير فرض، ولو منافيا للتالي يكون التالي لازما ،له كيف، وليس التالي منافيا للمقدم نفسه، إنما هو مناف لمجموع المقدم والتقدير، وليس التالي لازما له. ولا يخفى ما فيه من الخلل ؛ فإنا قدمنا لك أن التقادير في الشرطية كالأفراد في الحملية، ولا يعقل الحكم الشرطي إلا على التقادير، ولذا لم يعقل فيها المهملة القدمائية والطبعية . نعم، قد يكون في مصداقها اقتضاء نفس المقدم، من غير مداخلة أمر
(Third): The Chief Master (Ibn Sina) restricted the estimates and positions that preceded in the interpretation of universality to those with which it is possible for them to coexist with the antecedent, even if they are impossible in themselves. So whenever (A)(B) then (C)(D) means that (C)(D) is a necessary consequence of (A)(B) in every time and every position, such as Zayd's standing, his laughing, his being rational, and the donkey's laughing, and other things that can coexist with (A)(B). And he explained that if we generalize, it would follow that no universal would be true at all, neither conditional nor disjunctive; for if the antecedent is assumed with the absence of the consequent, and with its lack of implication for the antecedent, or with its existence without entailing the consequent in the conditional in the first two cases, because the absence of a thing does not entail its existence, and it does not contradict it in the disjunctive in the third case, because the existence of a thing does not oppose itself. Some commentators said: The judgment in universality is only about the implication of the consequent from the antecedent itself as such, and the antecedent is independent in requiring the consequent, and estimation has no role in it, otherwise the antecedent would not be independent in requirement. So the antecedent, on any estimation it is assumed, even if contrary to the consequent, the consequent would be necessary for it, but the consequent is not contrary to the antecedent itself, it is only contrary to the combination of antecedent and estimation, and the consequent is not necessary for it. And what it contains of defect is not hidden; for we have previously stated that estimates in conditional are like individuals in categorical, and conditional judgment is not understood except on estimates, and therefore the absolute (muhmala) and natural (tabi'iyya) conditionals are not understood in them. Yes, there may be in their instances a requirement of the antecedent itself, without the intervention of any matter.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage defines Ibn Sina's qualification for universal conditional propositions. It states that the estimates (taqadir) and positions (awda') allowed in interpreting universality must be possible to coexist with the antecedent, even if they are impossible in themselves. That is, when we say 'Whenever A is B, then C is D,' the implication holds in every time and every position that can coexist with the antecedent. The author gives examples like Zayd's standing and laughing, or the donkey's laughing. Then Ibn Sina explains that if we did not restrict the estimates, no universal would be true at all: in a conditional, assuming the antecedent with the absence of the consequent does not entail the consequent; in a disjunctive, assuming the antecedent with the existence of the consequent does not contradict it (since existence does not oppose itself). Some commentators argue that in universality, judgment is only about the implication from the antecedent as such, independent of any estimation. But the author points out a flaw: estimates in conditionals are like individuals in categorical propositions; conditional judgment is only understood with respect to estimates, so absolute and natural conditionals are not valid. Thus, the passage clarifies that to preserve truth in universal conditionals, the estimates must be possible to combine with the antecedent.

Key Terms
التقادير
الأوضاع
الكلية
المتصلة
المنفصلة

Key Takeaway
For a universal conditional to be true, the estimates (hypothetical scenarios) must be possible to coexist with the antecedent; otherwise, universality collapses.

The Accidental Conditional: Two Interpretations
Author defines the accidental conditional and gives two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of the accidental conditional and identify the key conditions for each.

Core Idea
The accidental conditional has no causal link—only coincidental truth of its parts.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth type of conditional proposition: the accidental conditional (al-ittiqaqiyya). The author presents two ways to understand this conditional, each with different truth conditions. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying conditionals in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'al-ittiqaqiyya' in the Arabic. Note that it refers to a conditional proposition where the connection between antecedent and consequent is merely coincidental.
2
Extract the first interpretation
The first interpretation requires 'sidq al-tarafayn' (truth of both sides) and specifies that the judgment is on 'al-taqadir al-waqi'iyya' (real estimations) without any causal relation ('ilaqa) between the parts.
This interpretation emphasizes that both antecedent and consequent must actually be true, but their truth is not causally linked.
3
Extract the second interpretation
The second interpretation is introduced with 'wa qad yuktafa fiha' (sometimes sufficiency in it). It requires only the truth of the consequent (sidq al-tali), while the antecedent is merely assumed.
This interpretation relaxes the condition: the consequent must be true in reality, but the antecedent need not be true; it is only supposed.
4
Compare the two interpretations
Note the difference: the first requires both parts true; the second requires only the consequent true. Both agree that there is no causal relation. Understanding which interpretation is being used is crucial when analyzing conditional statements in Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ: الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ قَدِ اعْتُبِرَ فِيهَا صِدْقُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، فيفسر بالتي حكم فيها بتحقق نسبة في نفس الأمر على تقدير تحقق أخرى فيها لا بعلاقة، ويكون الحكم في كليهما على التقادير الواقعية، (وَقَدْ يُكْتَفَى فِيهَا) أي في الاتفاقية بِصِدْقِ التَّالِي فَقَطْ، فيفسر بالتي حكم فيها بصدق قضية في الواقع على
The fourth: The accidental (conditional); in it the truth of both sides is considered. It is interpreted as that in which a judgment is made of the realization of a relation in reality upon the estimation of the realization of another relation in it, without a causal relation. And the judgment in both is upon real estimations. And sometimes sufficiency in it, i.e., in the accidental, is with the truth of the consequent only; thus it is interpreted as that in which a judgment is made of the truth of a proposition in reality upon
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the fourth type of conditional proposition: the accidental conditional (al-ittiqaqiyya). The author states that in this type, the truth of both sides (the antecedent and consequent) is considered. It is then defined in two ways. The first interpretation: it is a conditional in which a judgment is made that a certain relation holds in reality upon the estimation of another relation holding, but without any causal connection between them—the connection is purely accidental. The judgment in both the antecedent and consequent is based on real (actual) estimations, not hypothetical ones. The second interpretation is introduced with the remark that sometimes sufficiency in the accidental conditional is with the truth of the consequent only. This means that the accidental conditional can be interpreted as a proposition in which only the consequent is true in reality, given the antecedent. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but it likely continues with a full explication of this second view. The passage is defining a key logical concept and providing two nuanced interpretations, showing that the accidental conditional can be understood either as requiring both parts to be true (with a non-causal link) or as requiring only the consequent to be true (while the antecedent is merely assumed).

Key Terms
الاتفاقية
صدق الطرفين
التقادير الواقعية
العلاقة

Key Takeaway
The accidental conditional has two interpretations: one requiring both antecedent and consequent to be true, and another requiring only the consequent to be true—both lack a causal connection.
