The Coincidental Conditional: Definition and Two Interpretations
Definition of the coincidental conditional and its two truth-condition interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the coincidental conditional and distinguish its two interpretations regarding truth conditions.

Core Idea
A coincidental conditional has no causal link between its parts; its truth is debated between requiring both true or just the consequent.

In the previous lessons, we discussed the implicative conditional (luzūmiyya) which requires a real connection. Now we turn to the fourth type: the coincidental conditional (ittifāqiyya), where no such connection exists. The author presents two interpretations of its truth conditions, which have significant implications for how we evaluate conditional statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'al-ittifāqiyya' in the Arabic. Note that it is a type of conditional proposition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'conditional proposition'. The differentia is the absence of a causal relation (ʿalāqa) between antecedent and consequent.
3
Compare the two interpretations
First interpretation: both antecedent and consequent must be true in reality. Second interpretation: only the consequent's truth is required, allowing the antecedent to be false or impossible.
4
Determine the impact of each interpretation
How does each affect the validity of arguments using coincidental conditionals? The first restricts truth to cases where both parts are actual; the second expands it to any true consequent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ: الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ قَدِ اعْتُبِرَ فِيهَا صِدْقُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، فَيُفَسَّرُ بِالَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِتَحَقُّقِ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ تَحَقُّقِ أُخْرَى فِيهَا لَا بِعَلَاقَةٍ، وَيَكُونُ الْحُكْمُ فِي كِلَيْهِمَا عَلَى التَّقَادِيرِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ، وَقَدْ يُكْتَفَى فِيهَا، أَيْ فِي الاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، بِصِدْقِ التَّالِي فَقَطْ، فَيُفَسَّرُ بِالَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِصِدْقِ قَضِيَّةٍ فِي الْوَاقِعِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ فَرْضِ تَحَقُّقِ أُخْرَى، فَيَجُوزُ تَرَكُّبُهَا مِنْ مُقَدَّمٍ كَاذِبٍ، بَلْ مُحَالٍ وَتَالٍ صَادِقٍ، بِخِلَافِهَا بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَوَّلِ؛ فَإِنَّ الصَّادِقَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بَاقٍ عَلَى فَرْضِ كُلِّ كَاذِبٍ، بَلْ مُحَالٍ؛ لِأَنَّ التَّقْدِيرَاتِ لَا تُغَيِّرُ الْأَشْيَاءَ الْوَاقِعِيَّةَ. صَرَّحَ بِهِ الشَّيْخُ الرَّئِيسُ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الثَّالِيَ لَوْ كَانَ مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّمِ لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الْإِنْفَاقِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا أَمْكَنَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، أَيِ الْمُتَنَافِيَيْنِ؛ لِأَنَّ حَاصِلَ الْحُكْمِ الشَّرْطِيِّ اسْتِصْحَابُ التَّالِي لِلْمُقَدَّمِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ فَرْضِهِ، فَلَوْ صَحَّ تَرَكُّبُ الإِنْفَاقِيَّةِ مِنْ مُتَنَافِيَيْنِ كَانَ الْحُكْمُ فِيهَا بِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا بَعْدَ فَرْضِ الْمُقَدَّمِ، وَهُوَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، وَهَذَا ظَاهِرٌ جِدًّا إِذَا كَانَ الْمُقَدَّمُ الْمُنَافِي أَمْرًا مُمْكِنًا فِي الْوَاقِعِ، بِخِلَافِ اللُّزُومِيَّةِ؛ إِذْ لَا حُكْمَ فِيهَا إِلَّا بِوُقُوعِ مُحَالٍ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ مُحَالٍ، لَا اسْتِصْحَابَ وَاقِعِيٍّ لِأَمْرٍ بَعْدَ فَرْضِهِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ. وَقَوْلُهُ إِنَّ التَّقْدِيرَاتِ لَا تُغَيِّرُ الْأَشْيَاءَ الْوَاقِعِيَّةَ دَعْوَى مِنْ غَيْرِ دَلِيلٍ، بَلِ الظَّاهِرُ أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ فَإِذَا لَمْ يَكُنِ التَّقْدِيرُ مُنَاقِضًا، وَحِينَئِذٍ قَوِيَ الْمَنْعُ الَّذِي مَرَّ فِي أَوَائِلِ الْكِتَابِ عَلَى اسْتِلْزَامِ الدَّوْرِ التَّسَلْسُلَ. قَالَ بَعْضُ الشُّرَّاحِ: إِنَّمَا صِدْقُ الِاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ بِتَحَقُّقِ التَّالِي فِي الْوَاقِعِ، سَوَاءٌ كَانَ مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّمِ أَوْ لَا؛ فَإِنَّ تَحَقُّقَ شَيْءٍ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ شَيْءٍ لَيْسَ بَيْنَهُمَا عَلَاقَةٌ إِنَّمَا يُتَصَوَّرُ إِذَا كَانَ هَذَا الشَّيْءُ مُتَحَقِّقًا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، فَمَرْجِعُ صِدْقِ الِاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ لَيْسَ إِلَّا صِدْقَ التَّالِي، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ مُنَافَاةِ الْمُقَدَّمِ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، كَيْفَ وَلَمْ يُحْكَمْ فِيهَا بِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ؟ فَإِنَّ مَا لِلاِتِّصَالِ فِيهَا نَفْسُ تَحَقُّقِ التَّالِي، كَمَا أَنَّ مَا لِلُّزُومِ نَفْسُ تَحَقُّقِ الْعَلَاقَةِ بَيْنَ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي. وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ؛ فَإِنَّكَ قَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ الشَّرْطِيَّ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا عَلَى
The fourth: the coincidental conditional. In it, the truth of both sides is considered. It is interpreted as that in which a judgment is made of the actuality of a relation in reality upon the supposition of the actuality of another relation in it without a connection, and the judgment in both of them is according to actual suppositions. And sometimes in it, i.e., in the coincidental conditional, only the truth of the consequent is deemed sufficient. It is then interpreted as that in which a judgment is made of the truth of a proposition in reality upon the supposition of assuming the actuality of another. So it is permissible for it to be composed of an antecedent that is false, even impossible, and a true consequent, unlike the first meaning. For the truth in reality persists upon the supposition of any false, even impossible, thing, because suppositions do not change real things. The Chief Master (Ibn Sina) explicitly stated this. But the correct view is that if the consequent were contrary to the antecedent, the coincidental conditional would not be true, otherwise the conjunction of two contradictories would be possible, i.e., the two mutually exclusive things. For the upshot of the conditional judgment is the co-existence of the consequent with the antecedent upon the supposition of its assumption. So if the composition of the coincidental conditional from two mutually exclusive things were valid, the judgment in it would be of their conjunction after assuming the antecedent, which is the conjunction of two contradictories. And this is very clear when the contrary antecedent is something possible in reality, unlike the implicative conditional; for in it there is only a judgment of the occurrence of an impossible upon the supposition of an impossible, not a real co-existence of a thing after its assumption in reality. And his statement that suppositions do not change real things is a claim without proof; rather, the apparent is that if it were so, then when the supposition is not contradictory. And then the objection that was mentioned at the beginning of the book against the entailment of vicious regress by circularity becomes strong. One of the commentators said: The truth of the coincidental conditional is only due to the actuality of the consequent in reality, whether it is contrary to the antecedent or not; for the actuality of something upon the supposition of something else with no relation between them is only conceivable if this thing is actual in reality. So the basis of the truth of the coincidental conditional is only the truth of the consequent, and the contradiction of the antecedent does not entail the conjunction of two contradictories—how so, when no judgment of their conjunction in reality is made? For what there is of connection in it is the very actuality of the consequent, just as what there is of implication is the very actuality of the relation between the antecedent and consequent. And what is in it is not hidden; for you have known that the conditional judgment is only upon...
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of conditional proposition: the coincidental (ittiqāqiyya). It presents two interpretations. The first requires both the antecedent and consequent to be true in reality, with the judgment being based on actual suppositions, and there is no causal connection (ʿalāqa) between them. The second interpretation suffices with only the truth of the consequent, allowing the antecedent to be false or even impossible, because suppositions do not alter reality. The author attributes this second view to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh al-Raʾīs). However, the author then argues that the correct position (al-ḥaqq) is that if the consequent were contrary to the antecedent, the coincidental conditional would not be true, because the conditional judgment implies the coexistence of the two upon the supposition of the antecedent. If they were mutually exclusive, this would entail the conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible. This reasoning applies when the antecedent is possible in reality; for implicative conditionals (luzūmiyya) involving impossible antecedents, this issue does not arise because they only state the occurrence of an impossible under an impossible supposition, not a real coexistence. The author then criticizes the claim that suppositions do not change real things as unsupported, suggesting that if the supposition is not contradictory, then the objection about circularity leading to regress becomes strong. Finally, a commentator is cited who defends the second view, asserting that the truth of the coincidental conditional depends solely on the truth of the consequent, and that even if the antecedent is contrary, no conjunction of contradictories is actually judged because the only connection is the actuality of the consequent. The author hints that this view has flaws, but the text cuts off before elaborating.

المقدم
التالي
العلاقة

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation of the coincidental conditional mentioned in the passage, what is required for its truth?
A) Only the consequent must be true.
B) Both antecedent and consequent must be true in reality.
C) The antecedent must be impossible and the consequent true.
D) There must be a causal link between antecedent and consequent.

Key Takeaway
The coincidental conditional is defined by the absence of a causal connection, and its truth conditions are subject to two interpretations: requiring both sides true or only the consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify the following conditional as implicative or coincidental: 'If a person prays, then the prayer is valid.' (Assume no additional context.) Explain your reasoning using the definition of ʿalāqa.
?

The Debate on Contradictory Antecedents in Coincidentals
Debate on whether the coincidental conditional can have a contradictory antecedent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument against allowing a contradictory antecedent in a coincidental conditional and evaluate the commentator's rebuttal.

Core Idea
A coincidental conditional with a contradictory antecedent would imply the impossible—a conjunction of contradictories.

After presenting the two interpretations, the author engages in a debate. He argues that if the consequent contradicts the antecedent, the coincidental conditional cannot be true, because it would require the coexistence of two mutually exclusive things. A commentator disagrees, asserting that the only condition is the actuality of the consequent. This discussion sharpens our understanding of the logical implications of conditional truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's position (al-ḥaqq)
The author says if the consequent is contrary to the antecedent, the coincidental conditional is false.
2
Extract the author's reasoning
He argues that a conditional judgment implies the coexistence (istiṣḥāb) of the consequent with the antecedent upon assuming the antecedent. If they are contrary, this would mean the conjunction of two contradictories in reality.
3
Analyze the commentator's objection
The commentator holds that the truth of the coincidental conditional depends only on the actuality of the consequent, so even if the antecedent is contrary, no actual conjunction is judged.
4
Assess the strength of each view
Consider the author's point about real coexistence vs. the commentator's focus on the truth of the consequent alone. The author also criticizes the claim that suppositions don't change reality as unproven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ: الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ قَدِ اعْتُبِرَ فِيهَا صِدْقُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، فَيُفَسَّرُ بِالَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِتَحَقُّقِ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ تَحَقُّقِ أُخْرَى فِيهَا لَا بِعَلَاقَةٍ، وَيَكُونُ الْحُكْمُ فِي كِلَيْهِمَا عَلَى التَّقَادِيرِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ، وَقَدْ يُكْتَفَى فِيهَا، أَيْ فِي الاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، بِصِدْقِ التَّالِي فَقَطْ، فَيُفَسَّرُ بِالَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِصِدْقِ قَضِيَّةٍ فِي الْوَاقِعِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ فَرْضِ تَحَقُّقِ أُخْرَى، فَيَجُوزُ تَرَكُّبُهَا مِنْ مُقَدَّمٍ كَاذِبٍ، بَلْ مُحَالٍ وَتَالٍ صَادِقٍ، بِخِلَافِهَا بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَوَّلِ؛ فَإِنَّ الصَّادِقَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بَاقٍ عَلَى فَرْضِ كُلِّ كَاذِبٍ، بَلْ مُحَالٍ؛ لِأَنَّ التَّقْدِيرَاتِ لَا تُغَيِّرُ الْأَشْيَاءَ الْوَاقِعِيَّةَ. صَرَّحَ بِهِ الشَّيْخُ الرَّئِيسُ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الثَّالِيَ لَوْ كَانَ مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّمِ لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الْإِنْفَاقِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا أَمْكَنَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، أَيِ الْمُتَنَافِيَيْنِ؛ لِأَنَّ حَاصِلَ الْحُكْمِ الشَّرْطِيِّ اسْتِصْحَابُ التَّالِي لِلْمُقَدَّمِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ فَرْضِهِ، فَلَوْ صَحَّ تَرَكُّبُ الإِنْفَاقِيَّةِ مِنْ مُتَنَافِيَيْنِ كَانَ الْحُكْمُ فِيهَا بِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا بَعْدَ فَرْضِ الْمُقَدَّمِ، وَهُوَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، وَهَذَا ظَاهِرٌ جِدًّا إِذَا كَانَ الْمُقَدَّمُ الْمُنَافِي أَمْرًا مُمْكِنًا فِي الْوَاقِعِ، بِخِلَافِ اللُّزُومِيَّةِ؛ إِذْ لَا حُكْمَ فِيهَا إِلَّا بِوُقُوعِ مُحَالٍ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ مُحَالٍ، لَا اسْتِصْحَابَ وَاقِعِيٍّ لِأَمْرٍ بَعْدَ فَرْضِهِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ. وَقَوْلُهُ إِنَّ التَّقْدِيرَاتِ لَا تُغَيِّرُ الْأَشْيَاءَ الْوَاقِعِيَّةَ دَعْوَى مِنْ غَيْرِ دَلِيلٍ، بَلِ الظَّاهِرُ أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ فَإِذَا لَمْ يَكُنِ التَّقْدِيرُ مُنَاقِضًا، وَحِينَئِذٍ قَوِيَ الْمَنْعُ الَّذِي مَرَّ فِي أَوَائِلِ الْكِتَابِ عَلَى اسْتِلْزَامِ الدَّوْرِ التَّسَلْسُلَ. قَالَ بَعْضُ الشُّرَّاحِ: إِنَّمَا صِدْقُ الِاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ بِتَحَقُّقِ التَّالِي فِي الْوَاقِعِ، سَوَاءٌ كَانَ مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّمِ أَوْ لَا؛ فَإِنَّ تَحَقُّقَ شَيْءٍ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ شَيْءٍ لَيْسَ بَيْنَهُمَا عَلَاقَةٌ إِنَّمَا يُتَصَوَّرُ إِذَا كَانَ هَذَا الشَّيْءُ مُتَحَقِّقًا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، فَمَرْجِعُ صِدْقِ الِاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ لَيْسَ إِلَّا صِدْقَ التَّالِي، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ مُنَافَاةِ الْمُقَدَّمِ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، كَيْفَ وَلَمْ يُحْكَمْ فِيهَا بِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ؟ فَإِنَّ مَا لِلاِتِّصَالِ فِيهَا نَفْسُ تَحَقُّقِ التَّالِي، كَمَا أَنَّ مَا لِلُّزُومِ نَفْسُ تَحَقُّقِ الْعَلَاقَةِ بَيْنَ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي. وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ؛ فَإِنَّكَ قَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ الشَّرْطِيَّ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا عَلَى
The fourth: the coincidental conditional. In it, the truth of both sides is considered. It is interpreted as that in which a judgment is made of the actuality of a relation in reality upon the supposition of the actuality of another relation in it without a connection, and the judgment in both of them is according to actual suppositions. And sometimes in it, i.e., in the coincidental conditional, only the truth of the consequent is deemed sufficient. It is then interpreted as that in which a judgment is made of the truth of a proposition in reality upon the supposition of assuming the actuality of another. So it is permissible for it to be composed of an antecedent that is false, even impossible, and a true consequent, unlike the first meaning. For the truth in reality persists upon the supposition of any false, even impossible, thing, because suppositions do not change real things. The Chief Master (Ibn Sina) explicitly stated this. But the correct view is that if the consequent were contrary to the antecedent, the coincidental conditional would not be true, otherwise the conjunction of two contradictories would be possible, i.e., the two mutually exclusive things. For the upshot of the conditional judgment is the co-existence of the consequent with the antecedent upon the supposition of its assumption. So if the composition of the coincidental conditional from two mutually exclusive things were valid, the judgment in it would be of their conjunction after assuming the antecedent, which is the conjunction of two contradictories. And this is very clear when the contrary antecedent is something possible in reality, unlike the implicative conditional; for in it there is only a judgment of the occurrence of an impossible upon the supposition of an impossible, not a real co-existence of a thing after its assumption in reality. And his statement that suppositions do not change real things is a claim without proof; rather, the apparent is that if it were so, then when the supposition is not contradictory. And then the objection that was mentioned at the beginning of the book against the entailment of vicious regress by circularity becomes strong. One of the commentators said: The truth of the coincidental conditional is only due to the actuality of the consequent in reality, whether it is contrary to the antecedent or not; for the actuality of something upon the supposition of something else with no relation between them is only conceivable if this thing is actual in reality. So the basis of the truth of the coincidental conditional is only the truth of the consequent, and the contradiction of the antecedent does not entail the conjunction of two contradictories—how so, when no judgment of their conjunction in reality is made? For what there is of connection in it is the very actuality of the consequent, just as what there is of implication is the very actuality of the relation between the antecedent and consequent. And what is in it is not hidden; for you have known that the conditional judgment is only upon...
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of conditional proposition: the coincidental (ittiqāqiyya). It presents two interpretations. The first requires both the antecedent and consequent to be true in reality, with the judgment being based on actual suppositions, and there is no causal connection (ʿalāqa) between them. The second interpretation suffices with only the truth of the consequent, allowing the antecedent to be false or even impossible, because suppositions do not alter reality. The author attributes this second view to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh al-Raʾīs). However, the author then argues that the correct position (al-ḥaqq) is that if the consequent were contrary to the antecedent, the coincidental conditional would not be true, because the conditional judgment implies the coexistence of the two upon the supposition of the antecedent. If they were mutually exclusive, this would entail the conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible. This reasoning applies when the antecedent is possible in reality; for implicative conditionals (luzūmiyya) involving impossible antecedents, this issue does not arise because they only state the occurrence of an impossible under an impossible supposition, not a real coexistence. The author then criticizes the claim that suppositions do not change real things as unsupported, suggesting that if the supposition is not contradictory, then the objection about circularity leading to regress becomes strong. Finally, a commentator is cited who defends the second view, asserting that the truth of the coincidental conditional depends solely on the truth of the consequent, and that even if the antecedent is contrary, no conjunction of contradictories is actually judged because the only connection is the actuality of the consequent. The author hints that this view has flaws, but the text cuts off before elaborating.

Checkpoint
According to the author's argument (al-ḥaqq), why is a coincidental conditional with a contradictory antecedent false?
A) Because the antecedent is false in reality.
B) Because it would imply the conjunction of two contradictories.
C) Because the consequent is impossible.
D) Because the conditional has no causal link.

Key Takeaway
The author insists that a true coincidental conditional cannot have a consequent that contradicts the antecedent, as it would imply a logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Write a short jadal exchange: One party claims that the statement 'If the sun rises from the west, then prayer is obligatory' is a true coincidental conditional. The other objects that it cannot be true because the antecedent is impossible and the consequent is false. Use the concepts from this lesson to argue who wins.
?
2. Consider the conditional 'If I am a prophet, then I should be obeyed.' According to the author's argument in this passage, is this a true coincidental conditional? Explain why or why not.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Truth Condition of Accidental Conditionals: The Contradiction Problem
Author defines truth condition and contradiction problem of accidental conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why an accidental conditional cannot have an antecedent that contradicts its consequent, and distinguish this from implicative conditionals.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals break if antecedent contradicts consequent, forcing a real contradiction.

This passage defines the accidental conditional and sets its truth condition: the consequent must be true in reality upon the supposition of the antecedent. A critical implication follows: if the antecedent and consequent are contradictory, the conditional would assert the coexistence of contradictories in reality, which is impossible. This problem does not affect implicative conditionals, since their judgment operates only in the world of supposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
The passage defines an accidental conditional as one where the consequent is true in reality upon the supposition of the antecedent, regardless of any logical or causal relationship. It can even have a false or impossible antecedent as long as the consequent is actually true.
2
Recognize the contradiction problem
If the antecedent contradicts the consequent (i.e., they cannot both be true in reality), then assuming the antecedent forces the consequent to be true as well, which would mean that both a thing and its opposite are true in reality—an impossibility.
This is because the conditional judgment is about actual continuation, not just hypothetical.
3
Compare with implicative conditionals
In an implicative conditional, even if the antecedent contradicts the consequent, the judgment only states that if the antecedent were true, the consequent would be true, but this conjunction occurs in the world of supposition (ʿālam al-taqdīr), not in actual reality. Therefore no real contradiction arises.
4
Apply to an example
Construct an accidental conditional: 'If the sun is not shining, then 2+2=4.' The antecedent contradicts the consequent? No, they are not contradictory. But consider 'If a number is even, then it is odd.' That would be impossible because the consequent is not actually true. Now consider 'If 2 is odd, then water is wet.' The antecedent is false, consequent true, but they are not contradictory. If we try 'If water is not wet, then water is wet,' the antecedent contradicts consequent, and this conditional would be false because it would assert that on the assumption water is not wet, it is still wet—a real contradiction. Islamic example: 'If the world is eternal, then it is created.' The antecedent and consequent are contradictory in Islamic theology, so the accidental conditional would be false.

تقدير فرض تحقق أخرى، فيجوز تركيبها من مقدم كاذب، بل محالٍ وتال صادق، بخلافها بالمعنى الأول؛ فإن الصادق في نفس الأمر باق على فرض كل كاذب، بل محالٍ؛ لأن التقديرات لا تغير الأشياء الواقعية، صرح به الشيخ الرئيس. والحق أن الثالث لو كان منافياً للمقدم لم تصدق الإنفاقية، وإلا أمكن اجتماع النقيضين، أي المتنافيين؛ لأن حاصل الحكم الشرطي استصحاب التالي للمقدم على تقدير فرضه، فلو صح تركيب الاتفاقية من متنافيين كان الحكم فيها باجتماعهما بعد فرض المقدم، وهو اجتماع النقيضين، وهذا ظاهر جدا إذا كان المقدم المنافي أمرا ممكنا في الواقع، بخلاف اللزومية؛ إذ لا حكم فيها إلا بوقوع محال على تقدير محال، لا استصحاب واقعي لأمر بعد فرضه في الواقع. وقوله إن التقديرات لا تغير الأمور الواقعية دعوى من غير دليل، بل الظاهر أنه لو كان فإذا لم يكن التقدير مناقضا، وحينئذ قوي المنع الذي مر في أوائل الكتاب على استلزام الدور التسلسل. قال بعض الشراح: إن مال صدق الاتفاقية تحقق التالي في الواقع، سواء كان منافيا للمقدم أو لا؛ فإن تحقق شيء على تقدير شيء ليس بينهما علاقة إنما يتصور إذا كان هذا الشيء متحققا في نفس الأمر، فمرجع صدق الاتفاقية ليس إلا صدق التالي، ولا يلزم من منافاة المقدم اجتماع النقيضين، كيف ولم يحكم فيها باجتماعهما في نفس الأمر؛ فإن مال الاتصال فيها نفس تحقق التالي، كما أن مال اللزوم نفس تحقق العلاقة بين المقدم والتالي. ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإنك قد عرفت أن الحكم الشرطي لا يكون إلا على تقدير فرض المقدم، فالحكم في الاتفاقية ليس إلا بتحقق التالي في نفس الأمر تقدير فرض مقدمه فيها، فلو كان المقدم منافيا له يرجع الحاصل إلى تحقق أمر واقعي في الواقع، مع فرض مناقضه فيه، وهو الحكم بالجمع بينهما. وأما اللزومية فالحكم وإن كان فيها بتحقق التالي على تقدير تحقق المقدم، لكن لا يلزم منه على تقدير المنافاة إلا اجتماعهما في عالم التقدير، ولا خلف فيه؛ إذ ليس الحكم فيه بلزوم واقعي لمنافيه المقدم. وهذا والعلم الحقيقي عند علام الغيوب. وتسمى الأولى اتفاقية خاصة، والثانية اتفاقية عامة، قيل في «شرح المطالع»: إن الاتفاقيات مشتملة على العلاقة، بها يستصحب المقدم التالي، كما في اللزوميات؛ لأن المعية التي بين طرفيها ليست مستحيلة، فإما واجبة وفيها المطلوب، وإما ممكنة، فلها علة توجبها؛ لاستحالة تحقق ممكن من غير علة، والفرق أن تلك العلاقة في اللزوميات مشعور بها ابتداء ضرورة، أو بعد نظريتها يحكم باستصحاب المقدم للتالي، بخلاف الاتفاقيات؛ فإنه لا يشعر بتلك العلاقة ابتداء، بل إنما يشعر بصدقهما وتحققهما في نفس الأمر أولا، ثم يستدل به على تحقق تلك العلاقة، ولهذا لا يصلح لوقوعها في القياس الاستثنائي. وفيه نظر؛ لجواز أن تكون اتفاقية، بأن يكون الطرفان معلولين لثالث، أو لأمرين هما معلولان لثالث، فاجتمعا اتفاقا من غير اقتضاء العلة، ومطلق العلية، أي ثبوت العلة الواحدة لهما مطلقا لا يستوجب الارتباط الذي به يمتنع تحقق المقدم من غير تال
Assuming the supposition of the realization of another, so it is permissible for it to be composed from a false, rather impossible, antecedent and a true consequent, contrary to it in the first sense; for the true in itself remains upon the assumption of every false, rather impossible, because suppositions do not change actual things, as stated by the Chief (Ibn Sina). And the truth is that if the consequent were contradictory to the antecedent, the accidental conditional would not be true, otherwise the conjunction of two contradictories would be possible, i.e., the two mutually exclusive; because the gist of the conditional judgment is the continuation of the consequent with the antecedent upon the supposition of its assumption. So if it were permissible for the accidental conditional to be composed from two contradictories, the judgment in it would be of their conjunction after assuming the antecedent, which is the conjunction of two contradictories. And this is very clear if the contradictory antecedent is a thing possible in reality, contrary to the implicative conditional; for there is no judgment in it except the occurrence of an impossible upon the assumption of an impossible, not an actual continuation of a thing after its assumption in reality. And his saying that suppositions do not change actual things is a claim without proof; rather the apparent is that if it were so, then if the assumption is not contradictory, then the denial that occurred in the beginning of the book against the entailment of the circular argument to infinite regress becomes strong. Some commentators said: Indeed, the purport of the truth of the accidental conditional is the realization of the consequent in reality, whether it is contradictory to the antecedent or not; for the realization of a thing upon the assumption of a thing that has no relation between them is only conceivable if this thing is realized in itself. So the reference of the truth of the accidental conditional is nothing but the truth of the consequent, and it does not follow from the contradiction of the antecedent the conjunction of two contradictories, how? For it is not judged in it that they are conjoined in reality; for the purport of the connection in it is the very realization of the consequent, just as the purport of the implicative is the very realization of the relationship between the antecedent and the consequent. And it is not hidden what is in it; for you have known that the conditional judgment is only upon the supposition of assuming the antecedent. So the judgment in the accidental conditional is only by the realization of the consequent in reality upon the supposition of assuming its antecedent in it. So if the antecedent were contradictory to it, the result returns to the realization of an actual thing in reality, with the assumption of its contradictory in it, which is the judgment of combining them. As for the implicative, the judgment, although it is in it by the realization of the consequent upon the assumption of the realization of the antecedent, yet it does not follow from it upon the assumption of contradiction except their conjunction in the world of supposition, and no fallacy in it; for there is no judgment in it of an actual implication for its contradictory antecedent. And this, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. And the first is called 'specific accidental', and the second 'general accidental'. It was said in Sharh al-Mutali': Indeed, accidental conditionals include a relationship, by which the antecedent continues to the consequent, as in implicative ones; because the coexistence between the two sides is not impossible, so it is either necessary—and therein is the required—or possible, so it has a cause that necessitates it, due to the impossibility of a possible being realized without a cause. And the difference is that that relationship, in implicative conditionals, is perceived initially either necessarily or after its theoretical examination, and judgment is made by the continuation of the antecedent to the consequent, unlike accidental conditionals; for that relationship is not perceived initially, rather one first perceives their truth and realization in reality, then uses that to infer the realization of that relationship. Hence, they are not suitable for inclusion in the exception syllogism. And there is a critical view of this; for it is possible that it be accidental, in that the two sides are effects of a third cause, or of two causes that are effects of a third, so they coincide accidentally without the causality requiring them, and absolute causality, i.e., the existence of a single cause for both absolutely, does not necessitate the connection by which it becomes impossible for the antecedent to occur without the consequent.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of two types of conditional propositions: the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) and the implicative conditional (luzumiyya). The author begins by establishing that an accidental conditional can be composed of an impossible antecedent and a true consequent, unlike an implicative conditional where the antecedent and consequent have a necessary relationship. The key point is that an accidental conditional cannot have an antecedent that is contradictory to the consequent in reality, because the judgment in a conditional is that upon assuming the antecedent, the consequent is realized in reality. If the antecedent were contradictory, the judgment would entail the conjunction of two contradictories in reality, which is impossible. This problem does not arise in implicative conditionals because their judgment is only about the occurrence of an impossible upon an impossible assumption, not about actual reality. The author then critiques the view that suppositions do not change actual things, deeming it unproven. Next, the author presents an objection from some commentators: they argue that the truth of an accidental conditional depends solely on the actual truth of the consequent, regardless of any contradiction with the antecedent, because the conditional only asserts the consequent's reality on the supposition of the antecedent, not an actual conjunction. The author rebuts this by insisting that the conditional judgment always involves the supposition of the antecedent; if the antecedent is contradictory to the consequent, then the judgment becomes that of combining two contradictories in reality. For implicatives, the conjunction occurs only in the world of supposition, not in actuality. The author then introduces a distinction between specific accidental (first type) and general accidental (second type). The passage continues by quoting from Sharh al-Mutali' that accidental conditionals also involve a relationship (cause) that necessitates the coexistence, otherwise the coexistence would be impossible without a cause. The difference is that in implicatives this relationship is perceived directly, while in accidentals it is inferred from the actual truth of both propositions. The author critiques this view, arguing that it is possible for two events to coincide accidentally without a direct causal link, e.g., both are effects of a third cause or have independent causes. Mere common causality does not necessitate the specific connection that prevents the antecedent from occurring without the consequent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is an accidental conditional with a contradictory antecedent and consequent impossible?
A) Because the antecedent must be true for the conditional to be meaningful.
B) Because it would require the conjunction of two contradictories in reality upon the supposition.
C) Because accidental conditionals are always false if the antecedent is impossible.
D) Because such a conditional would actually be an implicative conditional.

Key Takeaway
An accidental conditional is false if its antecedent contradicts its consequent, because it would entail a real contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Test the following accidental conditional from Islamic theology: 'If the Qur'an is created, then it is uncreated.' Determine whether it is a valid accidental conditional according to the author's condition. Explain your reasoning.
?
Construct an accidental conditional that avoids the contradiction problem, using terms from Islamic law (e.g., prayer, fasting).

Propositions

12 min
Do Accidental Conditionals Have a Relationship? The Causal Debate
Author critiques the view that accidental conditionals have a causal relationship and discusses implications for syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that accidental conditionals imply a causal relationship, and assess their suitability for exceptional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals may hide a relationship inferred from concurrent truth, but not all concurrence implies causation.

This passage extends the discussion of accidental conditionals by examining whether they involve a relationship between antecedent and consequent. Some commentators argue that since the two sides are true together, they must have a cause, making accidental conditionals similar to implicative ones, except that the relationship is not initially perceived. The author questions this, suggesting that two events could coincide accidentally without a direct causal link, e.g., both are effects of a third cause. This debate affects whether accidental conditionals can be used in exceptional syllogisms (modus ponens/tollens).

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentators' position
The commentators (e.g., from Sharh al-Mutali') argue that accidental conditionals do include a relationship (cause) that necessitates the coexistence of antecedent and consequent. The difference from implicatives is that in accidentals this relationship is not perceived initially but must be inferred from the fact that both sides are true in reality.
They rely on the principle that any possible coexistence requires a cause.
2
Understand the implication for syllogisms
If accidental conditionals have a relationship, they could be used in exceptional syllogisms (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī), such as modus ponens. The passage states that they are not suitable precisely because the relationship is not perceived; you cannot assert that the antecedent implies the consequent directly.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author objects that two propositions could be true together without a direct causal link between them. For example, both could be effects of a third cause, or each have its own independent cause that happen to coincide. In such cases, the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent, so the conditional remains accidental in a strong sense.
Absolute common causation does not guarantee the specific connection required for implication.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the conditional: 'If the sun rises, then the prayer time enters.' In reality, both occur together but the relationship is causal (sunrise causes prayer time). That would be implicative. But consider: 'If the sky is clear, then the market is crowded.' These might be true together without a direct relationship (both depend on weather). If so, the conditional is accidental and cannot be used to infer market crowdedness from clear sky.
In usul al-fiqh, such conditionals are weak for establishing rulings.

So if it were permissible for the accidental conditional to be composed from two contradictories, the judgment in it would be of their conjunction after assuming the antecedent, which is the conjunction of two contradictories. And this is very clear if the contradictory antecedent is a thing possible in reality, contrary to the implicative conditional; for there is no judgment in it except the occurrence of an impossible upon the assumption of an impossible, not an actual continuation of a thing after its assumption in reality. And his saying that suppositions do not change actual things is a claim without proof; rather the apparent is that if it were so, then if the assumption is not contradictory, then the denial that occurred in the beginning of the book against the entailment of the circular argument to infinite regress becomes strong. And it is not hidden what is in it; for you have known that the conditional judgment is only upon the supposition of assuming the antecedent. Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of two types of conditional propositions: the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) and the implicative conditional (luzumiyya). The author begins by establishing that an accidental conditional can be composed of an impossible antecedent and a true consequent, unlike an implicative conditional where the antecedent and consequent have a necessary relationship. If the antecedent were contradictory, the judgment would entail the conjunction of two contradictories in reality, which is impossible. This problem does not arise in implicative conditionals because their judgment is only about the occurrence of an impossible upon an impossible assumption, not about actual reality. The author then critiques the view that suppositions do not change actual things, deeming it unproven. Next, the author presents an objection from some commentators: they argue that the truth of an accidental conditional depends solely on the actual truth of the consequent, regardless of any contradiction with the antecedent, because the conditional only asserts the consequent's reality on the supposition of the antecedent, not an actual conjunction. The author rebuts this by insisting that the conditional judgment always involves the supposition of the antecedent; if the antecedent is contradictory to the consequent, then the judgment becomes that of combining two contradictories in reality. For implicatives, the conjunction occurs only in the world of supposition, not in actuality. The difference is that in implicatives this relationship is perceived directly, while in accidentals it is inferred from the actual truth of both propositions. The author critiques this view, arguing that it is possible for two events to coincide accidentally without a direct causal link, e.g., both are effects of a third cause or have independent causes. Mere common causality does not necessitate the specific connection that prevents the antecedent from occurring without the consequent.

According to the author's critique in this passage, why might accidental conditionals still be unsuitable for exception syllogisms even if they have a cause?
A) Because the cause is not perceived directly, so the deduction is not certain.

D) Because the cause is impossible to determine.

Key Takeaway
Accidental conditionals may lack a direct relationship between antecedent and consequent, making them unsuitable for deduction even if they involve a common cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example from Islamic theology where two propositions are true together but without a direct causal relationship (e.g., 'If God is one, then the sun rises.'). Explain why this is an accidental conditional according to the author's critique.
?
Evaluate whether the following conditional is suitable for modus ponens: 'If a person prays, then they are rewarded.' Assume the relationship is causal (reward for prayer) – is that perceived? If not, does it become an accidental conditional?

Accidental vs Implicative Conditionals: Key Distinctions
Author defines accidental and implicative conditionals and their subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between accidental (ittifaqiyya) and implicative (luzumiyya) conditionals and identify their subtypes (specific and general).

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals lack perceived implication; implicatives have it—yet both rely on a relation.

In this passage, the author clearly defines two fundamental types of conditional propositions. The accidental conditional merely asserts that the consequence is true when the antecedent is true, even if they are contradictory. The implicative conditional adds that the antecedent entails the consequent. Understanding this distinction is the first step to using conditionals correctly in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Look for the terms الاتفاقية (accidental) and اللزومية (implicative). The passage defines each by how the judgment relates the antecedent to the consequent.
2
Understand the subtypes of accidental
The passage mentions two kinds: specific accidental (where the antecedent contradicts the consequent) and general accidental (where it does not). Note the difference in the judgment of combination.
3
Compare the role of the relation (علاقة)
In implicatives, the relation is perceived initially; in accidentals, it is inferred from actual truth. This difference makes accidentals unsuitable for exceptional syllogisms.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider a conditional like 'If something is wine (khamr), then it is forbidden (haram).' Is this accidental or implicative? Why? The relation between being wine and being forbidden is based on the known legal cause (sukr), so it is implicative.
The relation in this example is perceived from Islamic legal knowledge.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات
تقدير فرض المقدم، فالحكم في الاتفاقية ليس إلا بتحقق التالي في نفس الأمر تقدير فرض مقدمه فيها، فلو كان المقدم منافيا له يرجع الحاصل إلى تحقق أمر واقعي في الواقع، مع فرض مناقضه فيه، وهو الحكم بالجمع بينهما. وأما اللزومية فالحكم وإن كان فيها بتحقق التالي على تقدير تحقق المقدم، لكن لا يلزم منه على تقدير المنافاة إلا اجتماعهما في عالم التقدير، ولا خلف فيه ؛ إذ ليس الحكم فيه بلزوم واقعي لمنافيه المقدم. هذا والعلم الحقيقي عند علام الغيوب.
(وَتُسَمَّى الْأُولَى اتَّفَاقِيَّةً خَاصَّةً، وَالثَّانِيَةُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةً عَامَةَ، قِيلَ) في «شرح المطالع»: (إِنَّ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ مُشْتَمِلَةٌ عَلَى الْعَلَاقَةِ، بها يستصحب المقدم التالي، كما في اللزوميات ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمَعِيَّةَ التي بين طرفيها ليست مستحيلة، فإما واجبة وفيها المطلوب ، وإما (مُمْكِنَةٌ ، فَلَهَا عِلَّةٌ توجبها ؛ لاستحالة تحقق ممكن من غير علة، (وَالْفَرْقُ أَنَّهَا ، أي تلك العلاقة (فِي اللُّزُومِيَّاتِ مَشْعُورٌ بِهَا) ابتداء ضرورة، أو بعد نظريتها يحكم باستصحاب المقدم للتالي، (بِخِلَافِ الاتفاقِيَّاتِ (۱) ؛ فإنه لا يشعر بتلك العلاقة ابتداء، بل إنما يشعر بصدقهما وتحققهما في نفس الأمر أولا، ثم يستدل به على تحقق تلك العلاقة، ولهذا لا يصلح لوقوعها في القياس الاستثنائي.
(وَفِيهِ نَظُرُ) ؛ فإن وجوب تلك المعية بالعلة ممنوع؛ (لِجَوَازِ أَنْ تَكُونَ اتَّفَاقِيَّةً)، بأن يكون الطرفان معلولين لثالث، أو لأمرين هما معلولان لثالث، فاجتمعا اتفاقا من غير اقتضاء العلة ، وَمُطْلَقُ الْعِلّيّةِ)، أي ثبوت العلة الواحدة لهما مطلقا (لَا يَسْتَوْجِبُ الاِرْتِبَاطَ الذي به يمتنع تحقق المقدم من غير تال
إِذَا كَانَتْ تلك العلة ( بِجِهَتَيْن (۱) مُخْتَلِفَتَيْن) ، أي لا يكفي معلوليتهما لثالث للارتباط الذي هو اللزوم؛ لجواز كونه علة لأحدهما من جهة والآخر من جهة أخرى ، بل لا بد من ارتباط افتقاري بينهما، وهو مفقود فيهما
( هذا، وجوابه ظاهر ؛ فإن المقدم والتالي فيهما متحققان في الواقع قطعا، فإما مستندان إلى علة موجبة واحدة من جميع الجهات، فقد ثبت اللزوم بالشكل الأول، أو إلى علة من جهتين، أو إلى علتين، فتلك الجهتان أو العلتان إن لم تكونا معيتين بطل المعية بين الطرفين وإن كانتا معيتين فيعود الشقوق، ولا يتسلسل، بل ينتهي إلى علة واحدة موجبة من جميع الجهات، فثبت التلازم بين معلوليها الذين هما جهتان أو علتان فثبت التلازم بين طرفي الاتفاقية، فإذن ظهر لك أن الحق لا يتجاوز عما قال شارح المطالع»، والعلم الحقيقي عند علام الغيوب.
Completion of the discussion on conditionals: The judgment in the accidental conditional is only the realization of the consequent in actual fact, on the condition of supposing its antecedent therein; so if the antecedent were contradictory to it, the result reverts to the realization of an actual thing in reality, with the supposition of its opposite in it, and that is the judgment of combination between them. As for the implicative, the judgment, although it is the realization of the consequent upon the supposition of the realization of the antecedent, yet from it upon the supposition of contradiction nothing follows except their conjunction in the world of supposition, and there is no absurdity therein; for the judgment in it is not of a real implication for the antecedent contradictory to it. This, and the true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. (And the first is called specific accidental, and the second general accidental. It was said) in Sharh al-Mutali': (Verily the accidental conditionals include a relation, by which the antecedent entails the consequent, just as in implicatives; because the concomitance between their two sides is not impossible; so either it is necessary, and in that case the required [conclusion] is reached, or it is possible, and then it has a cause that necessitates it, because it is impossible for a possible thing to occur without a cause. (And the difference is that that relation (in implicatives is perceived) from the outset, necessarily, or after its theoretical [examination] one judges by the entailment of the antecedent for the consequent, (unlike the accidental ones); for one does not perceive that relation initially, but rather one first perceives their truth and realization in actual fact, then infers from that the realization of that relation; and because of this, they are not suitable for occurrence in the exceptional syllogism. (And in it there is a consideration); for the necessity of that concomitance by a cause is denied; (because it is possible that it be accidental), namely that the two sides are effects of a third thing, or of two things that are effects of a third, so they coincide accidentally without the cause's requiring [the entailment]; and (absolute causality), i.e., the affirmation of a single cause for both absolutely, (does not necessitate the connection by which it is impossible for the antecedent to occur without the consequent) when that cause is (in two different respects); i.e., it is not sufficient for them both to be effects of a third for the connection that is implication; because it is possible that it is a cause for one in one respect and for the other in another respect; rather, an interdependent connection between them is necessary, and it is absent in them. This, and its answer is clear: for the antecedent and consequent in them are both realized in actual fact with certainty; so either they are dependent on one cause that necessitates both from all respects, in which case implication is established by the first figure; or on a cause from two respects, or on two causes; so those two respects or two causes, if they are not mutually concomitant, the concomitance between the two sides is invalid, and if they are concomitant, then the branches return, and there is no infinite regress, but rather it terminates in a single cause that necessitates from all respects; so the mutual entailment between their effects, which are the two respects or two causes, is established; so the mutual entailment between the two sides of the accidental conditional is established. So it has become clear to you that the truth does not go beyond what the author of Sharh al-Mutali' said. And the true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of conditional propositions (al-shartiyyat). The author first distinguishes between two types: the accidental conditional (al-ittifaqiyya) and the implicative conditional (al-luzumiyya). In the accidental conditional, the judgment is simply that the consequent is true in reality when the antecedent is supposed true; if the antecedent contradicts the consequent, the judgment still only combines them in the realm of supposition without implying a real impossibility. In the implicative, the judgment is that the consequent is true upon the supposition of the antecedent, but if they are contradictory, no real impossibility follows, only a conjunction in the world of supposition, since the judgment is not of a real implication for something contradictory to the antecedent. The author then notes that the first kind is called 'specific accidental' (ittifaqiyya khaassa) and the second 'general accidental' (ittifaqiyya 'aamma). Next, a quote from Sharh al-Mutali' is presented, claiming that accidental conditionals also contain a relation (alaqa) that makes the antecedent entail the consequent, just as in implicatives. The reason given is that the concomitance (ma'iyya) between the two sides is not impossible; either it is necessary (and then the required conclusion is reached) or it is possible, and then it must have a cause, because a possible cannot occur without a cause. The difference, according to that view, is that in implicatives the relation is perceived from the outset, either necessarily or after theoretical examination, so one judges that the antecedent entails the consequent; in accidental conditionals, one does not perceive that relation initially, but first perceives their actual truth and then infers the relation from that, which is why accidental conditionals are not suitable for use in a hypothetical syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). The author then raises a consideration (nazar) against this: the necessity of the concomitance by a cause is denied, because it is possible that the two sides are accidental, i.e., effects of a third cause or of two causes that are effects of a third, so they coincide accidentally without the cause's requiring the entailment. Moreover, absolute causality (a single cause for both) does not necessitate the connection (irtibat) that would make it impossible for the antecedent to occur without the consequent, because that cause may operate in two different respects: it could be a cause for one in one respect and for the other in another respect. What is needed for implication is an interdependent connection (irtibat iftiqari) between them, which is lacking. The author then gives the answer (jawab) to this critique: the antecedent and consequent are certainly realized in reality. They are either dependent on one cause that necessitates both from all respects—in which case implication is established by the first figure of the syllogism—or on a cause from two respects, or on two causes. If those two respects or causes are not mutually concomitant, the concomitance between the two sides fails; if they are concomitant, then we have a branching that terminates in a single cause that necessitates both from all respects, establishing the mutual entailment between the effects (the respects or causes) and thus between the sides of the accidental conditional. Hence, the truth is with the author of Sharh al-Mutali', who claimed that accidental conditionals also involve a relation, albeit not perceived initially.

Key Terms
الاتفاقية
اللزومية
المعية
العلاقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the discussion, why are accidental conditionals not suitable for use in an exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i)?
A) Because they involve no relation between antecedent and consequent.
B) Because the relation in them is not perceived initially but only inferred after verifying the actual truth of both parts.
C) Because they are always false.
D) Because they lead to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Accidental conditionals differ from implicatives in that their relation is not initially perceived but inferred, yet they still contain a relation that grounds their logical use.

The Critique of Causal Necessity in Accidental Conditionals
Author critiques and defends the claim that accidental conditionals contain a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the critique of Sharh al-Mutali's claim and the counter-response that defends the presence of a relation in accidentals using syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
Even accidental conditionals have a relation, but it is not immediately perceived—and causality alone does not guarantee entailment.

This part of the passage presents a vigorous debate. The author first quotes Sharh al-Mutali' arguing that accidentals must have a relation because their concomitance is either necessary or caused. Then he raises a powerful objection: a common cause with different respects does not create entailment. Finally, he offers a response that uses the first-figure syllogism to show that a chain of causes must terminate in a single cause, establishing the entailment. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being made
Sharh al-Mutali' claims that accidental conditionals also contain a relation (عالقة) that makes the antecedent entail the consequent, just like implicatives. The argument: the concomitance between them is either necessary or possible with a cause.
2
Understand the author's objection (nazar)
The author denies that the cause necessarily creates entailment. He argues that the two sides could be effects of a third thing without the cause requiring interdependence, especially if the cause operates in different respects.
The key phrase: 'absolute causality does not necessitate the connection'.
3
Follow the response (jawab)
The response uses a disjunctive syllogism: either the antecedent and consequent depend on one cause from all respects (implying entailment via first figure) or on multiple causes. If multiple, those causes must be concomitant themselves, leading to a single cause from all respects, thus establishing entailment.
The response relies on the impossibility of infinite regress of concomitant causes.
4
Evaluate the argument
The conclusion is that the truth lies with Sharh al-Mutali': accidental conditionals do have a relation, albeit not perceived initially. This justifies their use in logical reasoning.

8 min
Why True Disjunction Requires Exactly Two Parts
Author asserts and defends the binary nature of true disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a true disjunction (al-munfasila al-ḥaqīqiyya) cannot have more than two parts and how a three-part candidate reduces to a binary disjunction.

Core Idea
A true disjunction must be binary; any multi-part candidate is actually a disjunction between one part and the rest.

In this lesson, we examine a key claim from the logic tradition: that a true disjunction (الانفصال الحقيقي) can only exist between two parts. The passage provides a proof: if there were three parts, the necessary mutual exclusivity in truth and falsehood would break down. An objection is raised and answered, showing that any apparent three-part disjunction reduces to a binary one. By the end, you will grasp why this is a foundational definitional rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition of a true disjunction
Note that a true disjunction requires mutual exclusivity in both truth (cannot all be true) and falsehood (cannot all be false). Exactly one part is true, all others false.
2
Test a three-part candidate
Assume three parts. If one part is eliminated (i.e., false), then examine the remaining two. If both are true, they violate truth exclusivity; if one is false, that false part and the eliminated part are both false, violating falsehood exclusivity. Hence impossible.
3
Consider the objection
The objector claims that if three parts are such that the truth of any one implies the falsehood of the others, and the falsehood of any two implies the truth of the third, then wouldn't the conditions be satisfied?
4
Resolve the objection
The author replies that in that case, the disjunction is actually between one part and the disjunction of the remaining two. So it is still binary, not truly three parts.
5
Generalize the insight
Any multi-part exclusive disjunction is logically equivalent to a binary disjunction between one part and the disjunction of the others. This preserves the binary nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ : قَالُوا الإِنْفِصَالُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ لَا يُمْكِنُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ جُزْتَيْنِ)؛ إذ لو من ثلاثة ، فإذا فرض ارتفاع واحد فالباقيان إن صدقا لم يكونا متنافيين في الصدق، وإن كذب أحدهما لم يكن هو والمرتفع متنافيين في الكذب، ولا بد في المنفصلة الحقيقية من التنافي فيهما.
فإن قلت: القدر الضروري للمنفصلة الحقيقية أن لا يرتفع أجزاؤه جميعا، ولا يصدق، فلو كان هناك ثلاثة أجزاء بحيث يكون صدق أحدها مستلزما لكذب الباقيين، وصدقهما معا لكذبه انعقد الحقيقية قطعا ؟
قلت: لا يخفى على من له أدنى دراية أنه حينئذ يكون الانفصال بين أحد الأجزاء وبين المفهوم المردد بين الباقيين بالذات إلا بين ثلاثة أجزاء.
The fifth: They said: The true disjunction is only possible between two parts; because if it were of three, then when one is supposed to be eliminated, the remaining two, if both true, would not be mutually exclusive in truth, and if one of them is false, it and the eliminated one would not be mutually exclusive in falsehood; and in the true disjunction, mutual exclusivity in both is required. If you say: The necessary condition for the true disjunction is that its parts cannot all be false, nor all true; so if there are three parts such that the truth of one necessitates the falsehood of the other two, and their truth together necessitates its falsehood, then the true disjunction is certainly formed? I say: It is not hidden from anyone with the least expertise that then the disjunction is actually between one part and the meaning that is hesitant between the remaining two, not between three parts.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the condition for a true disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya). The author states the claim that a true disjunction can only be between two parts. The reasoning is given: if it were among three parts, then suppose one part is removed; the remaining two, if both true, would not be mutually exclusive in truth (since they would both be true together, but in a true disjunction only one can be true at a time). Also, if one of the remaining two is false, that false one and the eliminated one would not be mutually exclusive in falsehood (since a true disjunction requires that all parts except one are false; here, if one of the remaining is false, the eliminated part is also false, so two parts are false, which violates the condition that exactly one must be true). The author then anticipates an objection: one might argue that the necessary condition for a true disjunction is only that not all parts be false together and not all be true together. So if there are three parts such that the truth of any one necessitates the falsehood of the other two, and the truth of any two together necessitates the falsehood of the third, then wouldn't a true disjunction be formed? The answer is that in such a case, the disjunction is actually not among three parts directly; rather, it is between one part and the disjunctive concept that hesitates between the remaining two. In other words, the three-part disjunction reduces to a binary disjunction: either the first part, or the disjunction of the other two. Thus, the true disjunction remains essentially binary.

Key Terms
الانفصال الحقيقي
التنافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a true disjunction not have three parts directly?
A) Because three parts would always be mutually exclusive in truth and falsehood.
B) Because it would reduce to a binary disjunction between one part and the disjunction of the other two.
C) Because three parts are impossible in logic.
D) Because the true disjunction requires all parts to be true together.

Key Takeaway
A true disjunction must have exactly two parts; any apparent multi-part exclusive disjunction is logically equivalent to a binary one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following statement is a true disjunction and, if not, explain why: 'The world is either eternal, temporally originated, or non-existent.' (Hint: Consider if these three can all be false together?)
?
Construct a true binary disjunction from a two-option scenario in Islamic theology, such as the statement about the Quran being created or uncreated.

Two Views on the Number of Parts in Disjunctive Propositions
Presents two competing views on disjunction part count.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the well-known view (allowing three parts for maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluw) and the group's view (requiring exactly two parts for all disjunctions).

Core Idea
Disjunction's part count is contested – three for exclusive/exhaustive, but some insist on two for all.

This passage opens the debate on how many parts a disjunctive proposition can have. The author first states the well-known view that maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluw can have three parts, then introduces a group that argues all disjunctions are strictly two-part. This debate is foundational for understanding later arguments about the nature of disjunction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate in the Arabic text the sentence that begins 'هذا هو المشهور' and the sentence beginning 'وَذَهَبَ جَمَاعَةٌ'. These state the well-known view and the dissenting view respectively.
The well-known view treats maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluw differently from true disjunction.
2
Compare the part counts
Note that for maniat al-jam', the well-known view allows three parts where no two are true together; for maniat al-khuluw, three parts where no two are false together. The dissenting view says all disjunctions have exactly two parts.
3
Understand the implication
If the dissenting view is correct, then apparent three-part disjunctions must be analyzed as composite, as will be shown in the next part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات (بِخِلَافِ مَانِعَةِ الْجَمْع؛ إذ يجوز تركبها من ثلاثة أجزاء، لا يمكن اجتماع كل اثنين منها في الصدق، ويجوز اجتماعهما في الكذب، (وَمَانِعَة الْخُلَو)؛ إذ يجوز تركبها من ثلاثة لا يجوز اجتماع اثنين منها في الكذب، ويجوز اجتماعهما في الصدق ، هذا هو المشهور . (وَذَهَبَ جَمَاعَةٌ إِلَى أَنَّ الاِنْفِصَالَ مُطْلَقًا، سواء كان حقيقيا أو منع جمع أو منع خلو (لَا يَتَحَصَّلُ إِلَّا مِنْ جزئين (اثْنَيْنِ لَا أَزْيَدَ وَلَا أَنْقَصَ)، ولما كان لمتوهم أن يتوهم النقض بنحو إما أن يكون المفهوم واجبا، وإما أن يكون ممكنا، وإما أن يكون ممتنعا قال: (وَمِثْلُ كُلَّ مَنْهُوم إِمَّا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْكِنُ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ مُرَكَّبٌ)، ليس من حمليات ثلاث حتى يرد نقضه، بل (مِنْ حَمْلِيَّة وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ مانعة الخلو ؛ فإن قولنا : هذا المفهوم إما واجب» إن كذب صدق المفهوم إما ممكن أو ممتنع على سبيل منع الخلو ، وإن كذب مانعة الخلو صدقت، فينعقد منهما انفصال حقيقي، وليس مأخوذا على أنه منع جمع، حتى يرد أنهما صادقان، فكيف ينعقد انفصال حقيقي. . (وَزَعَمَ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ مُطْلَقًا يُمْكِنُ تَرْكِيبُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ فَوْقَ اثْنَيْنِ، وَالْحَقُّ هُوَ الثَّانِي ؛ لِأَنَّ الاِنْفِصَالَ نِسْبَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالنَّسْبَةُ الْوَاحِدَةُ لَا تُتَصَوَّرُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ ، وَمَا قِيلَ إِنَّ فِيهِ)، أي في هذا التعليل (مُصَادَرَةً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ أَرَادَ) المستدل في الكبرى (أَنَّ كُلَّ نِسْبَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ انْفِصَالِيَّةٍ أَوْ غَيْرِهَا، فَهُوَ مَحَلُّ النَّزَاع) الذي هو الدعوى ، (وَإِلَّا) ، أي وإن لم يرد ذلك، بل أراد أن بعض النسب الواحدة لا تكون إلا بين اثنين، (فَلَا يَنْفَعُ) ؛ لكونه جزئيا، (فَمَدْفُوعٌ بِمَا يُدْفَعُ بِهِ ، أي المصادرة (فِي كُبْرَى) الشكل (الْأَوَّلِ)، من الفرق بين الإجمال لُزُومُهَا)
Continuation of the section on conditionals. Unlike maniat al-jam', because it can be composed of three parts where no two can be true together but they can be false together, and maniat al-khuluw, because it can be composed of three parts where two cannot be false together but can be true together. This is the well-known view. And a group held that disjunction absolutely, whether true, maniat al-jam', or maniat al-khuluw, can only consist of two parts, no more and no less. And because it might be imagined that there is a counterexample like 'Every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible,' he said: 'Such an example is composite,' not consisting of three categoricals so that it would be a counterexample, but rather of one categorical and a maniat al-khuluw disjunction. For if we say 'This concept is either necessary' and it is false, then the concept is either possible or impossible on the basis of maniat al-khuluw, and if the maniat al-khuluw is false, the first is true. So a true disjunction is formed from them, and it is not taken as maniat al-jam' such that they could both be true, then how would a true disjunction be formed? And some claimed that absolutely it can be composed of parts beyond two, but the truth is the second view, because the disjunction is a single relation, and a single relation can only be conceived between two. And what was said that there is in this reasoning a circularity, because if the arguer intends in the major premise that every single relation, whether disjunctive or otherwise, then that is the point of dispute; and if he does not intend that but rather intends that some single relations are only between two, then it does not help because it is particular. So it is rebutted by what rebuts the circularity in the major premise of the first figure, by the distinction between generality and necessity (luzum).
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the number of parts in disjunctive propositions (munfasila). It begins by noting that unlike true disjunction, the exclusive disjunction (maniat al-jam') and the exhaustive disjunction (maniat al-khuluw) can have three parts. Then it presents the view of a group who insist that all disjunctions, regardless of type, consist of exactly two parts. They provide an example: 'Every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible.' This might seem a counterexample, but they argue it is composite—actually a categorical (the concept is Wajib/possible/impossible) plus a maniat al-khuluw disjunction. They break it down: if 'this concept is necessary' is false, then the disjunction of possible and impossible (as a maniat al-khuluw) is true; if that disjunction is false, then 'necessary' is true. Thus a true disjunction emerges between two parts. The author then states the truth: the second view (two parts) is correct because disjunction is a single relation, and a single relation requires exactly two relata. He addresses the potential charge of circularity: if the major premise in the argument claims all single relations are between two, that is the very point in dispute; but if it claims only some, it fails to prove the general case. However, this objection is rebutted by distinguishing between generality and necessity (luzum) in the first figure of syllogism.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
انفصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the group mentioned in the passage, how many parts does any disjunctive proposition have?
A) Three parts for maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluw
B) Exactly two parts, no more no less
C) As many parts as needed
D) Only true disjunction has two parts

Key Takeaway
The debate between the well-known view and the group's view centers on whether disjunctions can have more than two parts, with the group insisting on exactly two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'An action is either obligatory, recommended, or permissible.' Explain why, according to the group's view, this must be reduced to a two-part disjunction.

The Composite Example: Necessary, Possible, Impossible
Author rebuts a three-part counterexample by analyzing it as composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an apparent three-part disjunction (e.g., necessary, possible, impossible) and show it is actually composite, consisting of a categorical and a maniat al-khuluw disjunction.

Core Idea
Seemingly three-part disjunctions are often composite, reducing to two-part true disjunctions.

An objector might present 'Every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible' as a counterexample to the two-part rule. The author rebuts by showing this statement is not a simple three-part disjunction but a composite of a categorical and a maniat al-khuluw disjunction. This lesson teaches you to dissect such complex examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alleged counterexample
Find in the text the phrase 'إما أن يكون المفهوم واجبا، وإما أن يكون ممكنا، وإما أن يكون ممتنعا'. This is the three-part example used by the objector.
The objector thinks this refutes the two-part claim.
2
Understand the author's response
The author says this example is 'مُرَكَّب' (composite). It is not three categoricals but one categorical ('this concept is necessary') and one maniat al-khuluw disjunction ('possible or impossible').
The key is that 'possible or impossible' functions as a maniat al-khuluw (no two false together).
3
Trace the logical reduction
If 'this concept is necessary' is false, then the maniat al-khuluw 'possible or impossible' must be true (since both cannot be false). If the maniat al-khuluw is false, then 'necessary' is true. Thus, a true disjunction emerges between 'necessary' and the disjunction of 'possible or impossible', which itself is two-part.
This shows the overall structure is a two-part true disjunction, not a three-part simple disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات (بِخِلَافِ مَانِعَةِ الْجَمْع؛ إذ يجوز تركبها من ثلاثة أجزاء، لا يمكن اجتماع كل اثنين منها في الصدق، ويجوز اجتماعهما في الكذب، (وَمَانِعَة الْخُلَو)؛ إذ يجوز تركبها من ثلاثة لا يجوز اجتماع اثنين منها في الكذب، ويجوز اجتماعهما في الصدق ، هذا هو المشهور . (وَذَهَبَ جَمَاعَةٌ إِلَى أَنَّ الاِنْفِصَالَ مُطْلَقًا، سواء كان حقيقيا أو منع جمع أو منع خلو (لَا يَتَحَصَّلُ إِلَّا مِنْ جزئين (اثْنَيْنِ لَا أَزْيَدَ وَلَا أَنْقَصَ)، ولما كان لمتوهم أن يتوهم النقض بنحو إما أن يكون المفهوم واجبا، وإما أن يكون ممكنا، وإما أن يكون ممتنعا قال: (وَمِثْلُ كُلَّ مَنْهُوم إِمَّا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْكِنُ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ مُرَكَّبٌ)، ليس من حمليات ثلاث حتى يرد نقضه، بل (مِنْ حَمْلِيَّة وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ مانعة الخلو ؛ فإن قولنا : هذا المفهوم إما واجب» إن كذب صدق المفهوم إما ممكن أو ممتنع على سبيل منع الخلو ، وإن كذب مانعة الخلو صدقت، فينعقد منهما انفصال حقيقي، وليس مأخوذا على أنه منع جمع، حتى يرد أنهما صادقان، فكيف ينعقد انفصال حقيقي. . (وَزَعَمَ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ مُطْلَقًا يُمْكِنُ تَرْكِيبُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ فَوْقَ اثْنَيْنِ، وَالْحَقُّ هُوَ الثَّانِي ؛ لِأَنَّ الاِنْفِصَالَ نِسْبَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالنَّسْبَةُ الْوَاحِدَةُ لَا تُتَصَوَّرُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ ، وَمَا قِيلَ إِنَّ فِيهِ)، أي في هذا التعليل (مُصَادَرَةً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ أَرَادَ) المستدل في الكبرى (أَنَّ كُلَّ نِسْبَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ انْفِصَالِيَّةٍ أَوْ غَيْرِهَا، فَهُوَ مَحَلُّ النَّزَاع) الذي هو الدعوى ، (وَإِلَّا) ، أي وإن لم يرد ذلك، بل أراد أن بعض النسب الواحدة لا تكون إلا بين اثنين، (فَلَا يَنْفَعُ) ؛ لكونه جزئيا، (فَمَدْفُوعٌ بِمَا يُدْفَعُ بِهِ ، أي المصادرة (فِي كُبْرَى) الشكل (الْأَوَّلِ)، من الفرق بين الإجمال لُزُومُهَا)
