Compound Propositions: Definition and Negation
Author defines compound proposition and its negation rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a compound proposition and construct its contradictory as an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its parts.

Core Idea
The negation of a universal compound is an exclusive disjunction.

In this passage, the author defines a compound proposition as one composed of an affirmative and a negative part. Understanding how to negate such propositions is crucial for precise logical analysis in Islamic sciences, especially when dealing with contradictory statements in legal or theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Recognize that a compound proposition has two parts: one affirmative and one negative. The example given is 'Every writer is moving his fingers always, not actually', which breaks down into an affirmative universal and a negative universal.
2
Apply the negation rule
The negation of a universal compound is not a simple proposition but a disjunction of the negations of the parts, with the condition that they cannot both be false (mani'at al-khuluw). This prevents emptiness.
3
Construct the contradictory
For each part of the compound, find its strict contradictory. Then combine those contradictories with an exclusive 'or'. For the example, the contradictories are 'some writer is not moving his fingers when writing' and 'some writer is always moving his fingers'.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Form a compound proposition on a fiqh topic, e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden and some intoxicant is not forbidden'. Its contradictory would be an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories: 'Either some intoxicant is not forbidden or every intoxicant is forbidden'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُرَكَّبَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة، وَرَفْعُ الْمُتَعَدِّدَةِ مُتَعَدِّدٌ، وَهُوَ رَفْعُ أَحَدِ الْجُزْتَيْنِ عَلَى سَبِيلِ مَنْعِ الْخُلُو؛ فإن رفع المجموع إنما يكون برفع جميع الأجزاء أو بعضها لا على التعيين، (وَالْكُلَّيَّةُ مِنْهَا)، أي من المركبات (لَا تَتَفَاوَتُ عِنْدَ التَّحْلِيلِ وَالتَّرْكِيبِ)؛ فإن جميع الأفراد لا يتغير تركيبا وتحليلا، فالمركبة الكلية الواحدة والكليتان المقيدتان بتلك الجهة واحدتان مصداقا (فَنَقِيضُهَا) موجبة مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُو مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنْ نَقِيضَيِّ الْجُزْتَيْنِ) بعد التحليل، مثلا قولنا كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع دائما ما دام كاتبا لا دائما» ، أي لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل، فالجزء الأول منها موجبة عرفية عامة ونقيضها سالبة جزئية حينية مطلقة، والثانية مطلقة عامة سالبة كلية فنقيضها دائمة موجبة جزئية ، فأخذناهما ورددنا بينهما على سبيل منع الخلو، وقلنا إما بعض الكاتب ليس بمتحرك الأصابع حين هو كاتب، وإما بعض الكاتب متحرك دائما ، فحصل نقيض العرفية الخاصة ، ولما كانت صادقة كذب هذه المنفصلة .
ولما كان لقائل أن يقول : قد سبق اشتراط الاختلاف كيفا في التناقض . وههنا أخذتم نقيض الموجبة المركبة موجبة مانعة الخلو، وقد سبق أيضا اشتراط وحدة النسبة الحكمية، وقد أخذتم في نقيض الحملية شرطية مانعة الخلو أجاب بقوله: (وَإِذَا أُرِيدَ مِنَ النَّقِيضِ هَهُنَا أَعَمَّ مِنَ) النقيض (الصَّرِيحِ وَاللَّازِمِ الْمُسَاوِي) له فَلَا يُسْتَبْعَدُ فِي كَوْنِهِ شَرْطِيَّةٌ أَوْ مُوجَبَةٌ)، أن ما ذكرنا سابقا من الشرائط إنما كان في النقيض الصريح، والمراد ههنا أعم من الصريح واللازم المساوي، ولا استبعاد في كون موجبة شرطية لازما مساويا لنقيض الموجبة الحملية.
And the compound proposition is a multiple proposition, one affirmative and the other negative. And the negation of the multiple is multiple, and it is the negation of one of the two parts on the basis of preventing emptiness; for the negation of the whole is only by negating all parts or some of them without specification. And the universal of them, i.e., of the compounds, does not differ under analysis and composition; for all individuals do not change in composition and analysis. So the one universal compound and the two universals qualified by that respect are identical in reference. So its contradictory is an affirmative exclusive disjunction composed of the contradictories of the two parts after analysis. For example, our statement 'Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is a writer not always', i.e., Nothing of the writer is moving his fingers actually. So the first part of it is a general conventional affirmative and its contradictory is a particular absolute temporal negative; and the second is a general absolute universal negative, so its contradictory is a particular perpetual affirmative. So we took them and disjoined between them on the basis of preventing emptiness, and we said: Either some writer is not moving his fingers when he is a writer, or some writer is moving his fingers always. So the contradictory of the special conventional proposition was obtained, and since it was true, this disjunction was false. And since it is possible for a speaker to say: The condition of difference in quality has preceded in contradiction, and here you have taken the contradictory of the affirmative compound as an affirmative exclusive disjunction, and also the condition of unity of the relative judgment has preceded, and you have taken in the contradictory of the categorical a conditional exclusive disjunction, he answered by his saying: And if by 'contradictory' here is intended the broader meaning than the strict contradictory and the equivalent necessary, then it is not unlikely that it is conditional or affirmative—that what we mentioned earlier of conditions was only for the strict contradictory, and what is intended here is broader than the strict and the equivalent necessary, and there is no improbability in an affirmative conditional being an equivalent necessary for the contradictory of the affirmative categorical.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage defines compound propositions and their negation, then addresses an objection. The author first defines a compound proposition (murakkaba) as one composed of two parts: one affirmative and one negative. Its negation (raf‘) is also multiple—it is the negation of one of the two parts, specifically in a way that prevents both parts from being false (man‘ al-khuluw). For a universal compound, since the individuals do not change under analysis or composition, the universal compound and the two universals qualified by that respect are identical in reference. Therefore, its contradictory is an affirmative exclusive disjunction composed of the contradictories of the two parts after analysis. An example is given: 'Every writer is moving his fingers always as long as he is a writer—not always' means 'Nothing of the writer is moving his fingers actually'. This is a compound of an affirmative part (a general conventional) and a negative part (a general absolute). Their contradictories are a particular temporal negative and a particular perpetual affirmative, respectively. Disjoining them exclusively yields the contradictory. The author then considers an objection: previously, conditions for contradiction included difference in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and unity of the relational judgment. But here the contradictory of an affirmative compound is taken as an affirmative exclusive disjunction (which is affirmative, not negative), and in the contradictory of a categorical, a conditional exclusive disjunction is used. The response is that if 'contradictory' here means the broader sense that includes the strict contradictory (naqīḍ ṣarīḥ) and the equivalent necessary (lāzim musāwī), then it is acceptable. The earlier conditions apply only to the strict contradictory. Thus an affirmative conditional can be an equivalent necessary for the contradictory of an affirmative categorical proposition.

Key Terms
المركبة
رفع المجموع
مانعة الخلو

The negation of a universal compound proposition is an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its parts.

The Broad Contradictory: Strict vs Equivalent
Author resolves objection about contradictory of compound being affirmative conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the strict contradictory (naqīḍ ṣarīḥ) and the equivalent necessary (lāzim musāwī) and explain why the contradictory of a compound can be an affirmative conditional.

Core Idea
The contradictory in logic includes both strict and equivalent propositions.

The author anticipates an objection: the conditions for contradiction require difference in quality and unity of relational judgment, but the proposed contradictory of a compound is an affirmative conditional. He clarifies that 'contradictory' here is broader, including logically equivalent propositions, thus resolving the apparent inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Recall the strict contradictory conditions
From earlier lessons, the strict contradictory (naqīḍ ṣarīḥ) must satisfy conditions: unity of subject, predicate, time, place, condition, and difference in quality (one affirmative, one negative).
2
Identify the apparent violation
In the lesson, the contradictory of a compound is an affirmative exclusive disjunction (affirmative), while the original compound may be affirmative or negative. This seems to violate the difference in quality condition.
3
Understand the broader sense
The author says 'if by 'contradictory' here is intended the broader meaning than the strict contradictory and the equivalent necessary, then it is not unlikely.' The earlier conditions apply only to the strict contradictory. The compound's contradictory can be an equivalent necessary (lāzim musāwī) that is logically equivalent but not strictly contradictory.
4
Apply the distinction
Recognize when a logical tool uses equivalent propositions to maintain consistency. For example, in theological debates, the contradictory of a compound may be expressed as a conditional that is logically equivalent to the strict contradictory.

Negation of Particular Compound Propositions
Author contrasts negation of universal and particular compounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory of a particular compound proposition and explain why it differs from that of a universal compound.

Core Idea
The negation of a particular compound is a non-exclusive disjunction because its subject is one.

In logic, compound propositions like 'Some S is P and some S is not P' are called particular compounds. Their negation is not an exclusive disjunction (as it would be for universal compounds). The passage explains why: the subject of both parts is the same, and the principle 'the contradictory of the more general is more specific' shows that the negation of the particular compound is a non-exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its parts. This distinction is essential for correct reasoning in Islamic sciences where such compounds appear in arguments about rulings or attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound type
Determine whether the compound is universal (e.g., 'Every S is P and every S is not P') or particular (e.g., 'Some S is P and some S is not P'). The passage focuses on the particular compound.
Universal compounds have an exclusive disjunction as their contradictory; particular compounds do not.
2
Check subject unity
In a particular compound, the subject of the affirmative part and the negative part is the same (S). For example, 'Some animal is human and some animal is not human' has a single subject 'animal'. This contrasts with two independent particulars where subjects may differ (e.g., 'Some animal is human' and 'Some stone is not liquid').
3
Apply the principle of generality
Two independent particulars are more general than a particular compound because their subjects can vary. The principle 'the contradictory of the more general is more specific' (naqīḍ al-a‘amm akhaṣṣ) implies that the contradictory of the independent particulars is more specific and thus cannot be equivalent to the contradictory of the particular compound.
4
Formulate the correct negation
The contradictory of a particular compound (e.g., 'Some S is P and some S is not P') is a non-exclusive disjunction: 'Either no S is P or every S is P'. This disjunction must be non-exclusive because both disjuncts can be false (as shown by the animal example).
The exclusive disjunction 'Either every S is P or no S is P, but not both' is not the contradictory because it can be false when the original is false.
5
Test with a real example
Consider the particular compound 'Some animal is human at some time and some animal is not human at some time' (false because no animal is human). Its negation 'Either no animal is human at any time or every animal is human at all times' is true (since the first disjunct is true). The exclusive disjunction 'Either every animal is human at all times or no animal is human at any time, but not both' is false because both disjuncts are false. This confirms the correct negation is non-exclusive.
The passage uses an example to show that both the original and the exclusive disjunction can be false, proving the exclusive disjunction is not the contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِخِلَافِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ؛ فإن نقيضها ليست منفصلة مانعة الخلو؛ فَإِنَّ مَوْضُوعَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ فِيهَا وَاحِدٌ، فَالْجُزْئِيَّتَانِ المستقلتان أَعَمُّ من المركبة الجزئية؛ إذ الموضوع فيهما قد يكون متعددا، وَنَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيض الْأَخَصَّ، فنقيض المركبة الجزئية أعم، ونقيض الجزئيتين المستقلتين أخص، فلا يكون نقيضا لهما ولا مساويا له ولهذا قد يكذب الجزئية المركبة وتلك المنفصلة معا ؛ فإن قولنا بعض الحيوان إنسان بالفعل لا دائما كاذب، وقولنا إما كل حيوان إنسان دائما، وإما لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان دائما أيضا كاذب. فَالطَّرِيقُ في أخذ النقيض هناك أن تردد بين نقيضي الجزئين بالنسبة
Contrary to the particular; for its contradictory is not an exclusive disjunction; because the subject of affirmation and negation in it is one. So the two independent particulars are more general than the particular compound; since the subject in them may be multiple. And the contradictory of the more general is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific. So the contradictory of the particular compound is more general, and the contradictory of the two independent particulars is more specific. So it is not a contradictory for them nor equivalent to them. And for this reason, the particular compound and that disjunction may both be false. For our statement 'Some animal is actually human not always' is false, and our statement 'Either every animal is human always, or nothing of the animal is human always' is also false. So the method for taking the contradictory there is to disjunct between the contradictories of the two parts with respect to
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage addresses the negation of particular compound propositions, contrasting them with universal compounds. The author states that the contradictory of a particular compound is not an exclusive disjunction (māni‘at al-khuluw). This is because the subject of the two parts (affirmation and negation) is one and the same. In contrast, two independent particular propositions (each with its own subject) are more general than a particular compound, since the subject in the independent propositions may be multiple. Consequently, the contradictory of the more general (the independent particulars) is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific (the compound). Therefore, the contradictory of a particular compound is more general, and the contradictory of the two independent particulars is more specific, so they are not contradictories nor equivalent. This leads to a situation where both the particular compound and the disjunction can be false simultaneously. An example: 'Some animal is actually human not always' (a particular compound) is false because no animal is human. The disjunction 'Either every animal is human always, or nothing of the animal is human always' is also false because both disjuncts are false (the first is false because not every animal is human, the second is false because some animals are human). Thus, the method for finding the contradictory of a particular compound is to disjunct between the contradictories of the two parts with respect to the same subject (the text cuts off, but the method is clear: the negation is a disjunction of the negations of each part, but not an exclusive one).

Key Terms
الجزئية المركبة
الجزئيتان المستقلتان
نقيض الأعم أخص

D) Because the parts are both affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a particular compound (some S is P and some S is not P) is a non-exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its parts (no S is P or every S is P), not an exclusive disjunction.

Primary, Innate, and Intuitive Premises
Author distinguishes first three certain premises by middle term availability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish primary (awwaliyyat), innate (fitriyyat), and intuitive (hadsiyyat) premises based on the presence and nature of the middle term.

Core Idea
Three premises differ by how the mind accesses the middle term: absent, immediately present, or latently present.

The author of Bahr al-Ulum classifies certain premises into six types. The first three — primary, innate, and intuitive — are distinguished by how the intellect obtains the middle term that connects subject and predicate. Understanding this distinction is key to evaluating the force of demonstrative proofs in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Note that awwaliyyat require no middle term, fitriyyat require a middle term that is immediately present after conceptualizing extremes, and hadsiyyat require a middle term that is present but not immediately obvious, yet grasped without active reflection.
The passage defines each type by the role of the middle term.
2
Analyze the example for awwaliyyat
The example 'the whole is greater than the part' is self-evident; no syllogism is needed. The truth is known just by understanding the concepts.
3
Analyze the example for fitriyyat
The example 'one is half of two' requires a middle term (the relation of containment), but it is immediately present once you think of two as composed of two ones.
The intellect observes that two contains its like (one), so the middle term is instantly available.
4
Analyze the example for hadsiyyat
The example 'the moon receives light from the sun' requires a middle term (the correlation of phases with opposition), which is not instantly seen but comes to mind without active searching once the extremes are understood.
The intellect grasps the connection through observation of phases, but not as automatically as fitriyyat.

شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم يصح وما لا يفيد تصديقا ولا تأثيرا آخر فلا اعتداد به عند أصحاب الصناعات ، كالمشكوكات مثلا والواجب قبولها إما أن يكفي للتصديق بها بقدر الطرفين و النسبة ، فهي الأوليات، أو لا يكفي، فإن احتيج إلى وسط لا يغيب عن الذهن بعد تصور الطرفين فهي الفطريات والقضايا قياساتها معها، أو إلى وسط يغيب عن الذهن بعد تصور الطرفين، لكن لا يحتاج إلى فكر ونظر، فهي الحدسيات، أو إلى حس، وهي المشاهدات ، أو إلى إخبار جماعة لا عند العقل تواطئهم على الكذب، فهي المتواترات أو إلى تكرار المشاهدات ، فهي المجربات . فهذه ثلاثة عشر صنفا (١) ، فالأوليات نحو الكل أعظم من الجزء، والفطريات نحو الواحد نصف الاثنين؛ فإن العقل لا يحكم به إلا وهو ملاحظة أنه مشتمل على مثله ، والحدسيات نحو «القمر يستضيئ من الشمس» ؛ فإن العقل إنما يجزم بها بملاحظة أن نوره يزداد بازدياد المقابلة، وينقص بنقصانها، والمشاهدات وهي إما مشاهدة بِحِس ظاهر، نحو «النار حارة»، أو بحس باطن، نحو إن لنا جوعا»، والمتواترات نحو مكة موجودة) ، والمجربات نحو «السَّقْمُونِيا مُسْهِلٌ للصَّفراء».
The commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: It is correct. And what does not yield assent (tasdiq) nor any other effect is not considered by the practitioners of the arts, such as the doubtful things. And what must be accepted: either it is sufficient for assent by the two extremes and the relation, so these are primary (awwaliyyat); or it is not sufficient, so if a middle term is needed that does not disappear from the mind after conceptualizing the two extremes, these are innate (fitriyyat) and the propositions whose syllogisms are with them; or a middle term that disappears from the mind after conceptualizing the two extremes but does not require thought and reflection, these are intuitive (hadsiyyat); or it requires sense, these are observational (mushahadat); or it requires the report of a group whose collusion on falsehood is rationally inconceivable, these are recurrently narrated (mutawatirat); or it requires repetition of observations, these are experiential (mujarrabat). So these are thirteen kinds (1). The primaries are like 'the whole is greater than the part'; the innates are like 'one is half of two'—for the intellect only judges it while observing that it contains its like; the intuitives are like 'the moon receives light from the sun'—for the intellect only asserts it by observing that its light increases with increasing opposition and decreases with its decrease; the observables are either by an external sense, like 'fire is hot', or by an internal sense, like 'we have hunger'; the recurrently narrated are like 'Mecca exists'; the experiential are like 'scammony is a purgative for bile'.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of certain premises (yaqiniyyat) that are accepted without doubt and form the basis of demonstration. The author first distinguishes premises that yield assent (tasdiq) from those that do not, like doubts. Then he classifies the acceptable ones into six types based on how the intellect arrives at certainty. The first, primary (awwaliyyat), are self-evident, requiring only grasping the subject and predicate, e.g., 'the whole is greater than the part'. Second, innate (fitriyyat) require a middle term that is immediately present, e.g., 'one is half of two'—one realizes it contains two halves. Third, intuitive (hadsiyyat) require a middle term that is not immediately present but still comes to mind without effort, e.g., the moon's light coming from the sun due to its phases. Fourth, observational (mushahadat) rely on external or internal senses, e.g., 'fire is hot' or 'we are hungry'. Fifth, recurrent (mutawatirat) are based on overwhelming testimony, e.g., the existence of Mecca. Sixth, experiential (mujarrabat) are derived from repeated observation, e.g., scammony purges bile. The author notes these are thirteen kinds (including others), but only these six are elaborated here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type requires a middle term that does NOT disappear from the mind after conceptualizing the two extremes?
A) Primary (awwaliyyat)
B) Innate (fitriyyat)
C) Intuitive (hadsiyyat)
D) Observational (mushahadat)

Key Takeaway
Primary, innate, and intuitive premises are distinguished by how the middle term is accessed: absent, immediately present, or latently present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the proposition 'Contradictories cannot both be true' is awwaliyyat, fitriyyat, or hadsiyyat, and justify based on the passage's criteria.
?
Provide an Islamic example of a hadsiyyat premise from the natural world that a mutakallim might use in a proof for God's existence.

Propositions
beginner
5 min
Observational, Recurrent, and Experiential Premises
Author distinguishes last three certain premises by source: sense, testimony, or repeated experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish observational (mushahadat), recurrent (mutawatirat), and experiential (mujarrabat) premises based on their reliance on sense, testimony, or repeated observation.

Core Idea
Three premises rely on external input: direct sense, overwhelming testimony, or repeated experience.

The last three certain premises are grounded in perception and cumulative evidence. Observational premises come from immediate senses, recurrent from mass testimony, and experiential from repeated experiments. In Islamic sciences, these are crucial for establishing empirical facts and historical reports.

Guided Path
1
Note the three types
Mushahadat (observational) rely on external or internal senses, mutawatirat (recurrent) rely on testimony from a group that cannot collude on falsehood, and mujarrabat (experiential) rely on repeated observations yielding a causal law.
Each type has a distinct source of certainty.
2
Analyze the example for mushahadat
The examples 'fire is hot' (external sense) and 'we have hunger' (internal sense) are immediate sensory experiences. They are certain without further reasoning.
3
Analyze the example for mutawatirat
The example 'Mecca exists' is known through universal report. Since the group reporting is too large to conspire on a lie, the knowledge is certain.
This type is fundamental in hadith science for accepting mutawatir reports.
4
Analyze the example for mujarrabat
The example 'scammony is a purgative for bile' is derived from repeated observation that scammony consistently induces bile purging. This establishes a causal link, yielding certainty.
Experiential premises are used in medicine and natural sciences.

Which type of certain premise is the statement 'Scammony is a purgative for bile' according to the passage?

Key Takeaway
Observational, recurrent, and experiential premises are certain because they are based on direct or cumulative sensory evidence, not mere reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
